# Rust Moves Dependency Graph

This graph is the curriculum spine for EduRatchet-2. Keep entries terse.
The lesson body teaches; this file tracks prerequisite structure.

## Node Schema

```text
id: NNN-short-slug
move: learner-facing action
main_concept: concept installed by the move
depends_on: node ids or explicit assumptions
unlocks: likely future node ids or concept names
evidence: corpus paths and probe paths
status: draft|accepted
```

## Accepted Nodes

```text
id: 001-rustc-compile-and-run
move: compile a tiny Rust source file with `rustc` and run the produced executable
main_concept: rustc turns one .rs source file into a separate executable file in the same directory; running that executable is a second, distinct step
depends_on: ordinary computer-use (terminal, plain-text editor, rustc on PATH, Linux/macOS shell where ./name runs an executable)
unlocks: 002-fn-main-entry-point, 003-read-rustc-diagnostic, future let-binding moves, future cargo moves
evidence:
  - output/docs/rust/book/ch01-02-hello-world.md
  - output/docs/rust/rustc/what-is-rustc.md
  - output/docs/rust/rust-by-example/hello.md
  - experimental/eduratchet2/runs/rust-moves/observations/001-rustc-compile-and-run.rs
status: accepted
```

```text
id: 002-fn-main-entry-point
move: recognize `fn main()` as the entry point of a tiny Rust program
main_concept: every tiny rustc-compiled Rust program needs `fn main() { ... }`; the body of main runs when the executable is launched, and if there is no main, rustc refuses to build an executable (E0601)
depends_on: 001-rustc-compile-and-run, ordinary computer-use (editor)
unlocks: 003-read-rustc-diagnostic, future let-binding moves, future "define your own function" moves
evidence:
  - output/docs/rust/book/ch01-02-hello-world.md
  - output/docs/rust/reference/crates-and-source-files.md
  - output/docs/rust/error_codes/E0601.md
  - output/docs/rust/rust-by-example/hello.md
  - experimental/eduratchet2/runs/rust-moves/observations/002-fn-main-entry-point.rs
status: accepted
```

```text
id: 003-read-rustc-diagnostic
move: read a single rustc diagnostic by locating the headline, the --> location, the source excerpt with caret, and any help/note lines
main_concept: rustc diagnostics have a consistent structure; the optional E#### code and --explain trailer vary, but headline + location + source excerpt with caret are always there
depends_on: 001-rustc-compile-and-run, 002-fn-main-entry-point, ordinary computer-use (editor)
unlocks: future let-binding moves, future "fix the diagnostic" moves, future cargo moves
evidence:
  - output/docs/rust/rustc/command-line-arguments.md
  - output/docs/rust/error_codes/index.md
  - output/docs/rust/error_codes/E0601.md
  - experimental/eduratchet2/runs/rust-moves/observations/003-read-rustc-diagnostic.rs
  - experimental/eduratchet2/runs/rust-moves/lessons/002-fn-main-entry-point.md (E0601 cross-reference)
status: accepted
```

```text
id: 004-statements-in-order
move: write more than one `;`-terminated line in `fn main` and observe that the executable runs them in source order
main_concept: the body of `fn main` is a sequence of statements (lines ending in `;`); they execute top-to-bottom in source order
depends_on: 001-rustc-compile-and-run, 002-fn-main-entry-point, ordinary computer-use (editor)
unlocks: 005-let-binding, future "comments" moves, future "expression vs statement" moves, future function-call moves
evidence:
  - output/docs/rust/book/ch01-02-hello-world.md
  - output/docs/rust/book/ch03-03-how-functions-work.md
  - experimental/eduratchet2/runs/rust-moves/observations/004-statements-in-order.rs
status: accepted
```

```text
id: 005-let-binding
move: bind a name to a value with `let name = value;`, then use the name in a later statement
main_concept: `let name = value;` is a statement that binds a name to a value; the name refers to that value in subsequent statements; without the binding, rustc errors with E0425 "cannot find value in this scope"
depends_on: 001-rustc-compile-and-run, 002-fn-main-entry-point, 003-read-rustc-diagnostic, 004-statements-in-order, ordinary computer-use (editor)
unlocks: future "mut / mutability" moves, future "type annotations on let" moves, future "shadowing" moves, future "format-string DSL" moves
evidence:
  - output/docs/rust/book/ch03-01-variables-and-mutability.md
  - output/docs/rust/book/ch03-03-how-functions-work.md
  - output/docs/rust/error_codes/E0425.md
  - experimental/eduratchet2/runs/rust-moves/observations/005-let-binding.rs
status: accepted
```

```text
id: 006-mut-binding
move: write `let mut name = value;` to make a binding reassignable, then assign a new value to it in a later statement
main_concept: bindings are immutable by default; adding `mut` after `let` makes them reassignable, so a later `name = new_value;` works; without `mut`, that reassignment fails with E0384 "cannot assign twice to immutable variable"
depends_on: 001-rustc-compile-and-run, 002-fn-main-entry-point, 003-read-rustc-diagnostic, 004-statements-in-order, 005-let-binding, ordinary computer-use (editor)
unlocks: future "shadowing" moves, future "type annotations on let" moves, future "constants" moves, future "&mut references" moves, future "format-string DSL" moves
evidence:
  - output/docs/rust/book/ch03-01-variables-and-mutability.md
  - output/docs/rust/error_codes/E0384.md
  - experimental/eduratchet2/runs/rust-moves/observations/006-mut-binding.rs
status: accepted
```

```text
id: 007-shadowing
move: rebind an existing name with a second `let name = ...;` to shadow the old binding
main_concept: a second `let` with the same name does not reassign the existing binding — it creates a new binding that shadows the old one; the name then refers to the new value; shadowing requires repeating `let` and does NOT require `mut` (different mechanism from lesson 006's reassignment)
depends_on: 001-rustc-compile-and-run, 002-fn-main-entry-point, 004-statements-in-order, 005-let-binding, 006-mut-binding, ordinary computer-use (editor)
unlocks: future "scope" moves, future "type annotations on let" moves, future "arithmetic / operators" moves, future "type-changing shadowing" moves
evidence:
  - output/docs/rust/book/ch03-01-variables-and-mutability.md
  - output/docs/rust/error_codes/E0384.md
  - experimental/eduratchet2/runs/rust-moves/observations/007-shadowing.rs
  - experimental/eduratchet2/runs/rust-moves/lessons/006-mut-binding.md (E0384 cross-reference)
status: accepted
```

```text
id: 008-define-and-call-function
move: define a second function with `fn name() { ... }` and call it from `main` with `name();`
main_concept: a Rust file can contain multiple functions; `fn name() { ... }` defines one; the statement `name();` calls it, transferring control to its body and returning to the caller; without a definition, `name();` fails with E0425 "cannot find function in this scope" (same E-code as lesson 005's missing-value)
depends_on: 001-rustc-compile-and-run, 002-fn-main-entry-point, 003-read-rustc-diagnostic, 004-statements-in-order, ordinary computer-use (editor)
unlocks: future "function parameters" moves, future "function return values" moves, future "expression vs statement" moves, future "recursion" moves, future "modules / pub" moves
evidence:
  - output/docs/rust/book/ch03-03-how-functions-work.md
  - output/docs/rust/error_codes/E0425.md
  - experimental/eduratchet2/runs/rust-moves/observations/008-define-and-call-function.rs
status: accepted
```

```text
id: 009-arithmetic-on-integers
move: combine integer values with `+ - * /` on the right of a `let`, then print each bound result
main_concept: Rust's binary operators `+` `-` `*` `/` combine two integer values into a new integer value; the resulting expression fits on the right of `let`; integer division truncates toward zero so `5 / 3` is `1`
depends_on: 001-rustc-compile-and-run, 002-fn-main-entry-point, 004-statements-in-order, 005-let-binding, ordinary computer-use (editor)
unlocks: future "remainder operator %" moves, future "operator precedence" moves, future "types / i32 / floats" moves, future "shadowing with arithmetic" moves
evidence:
  - output/docs/rust/book/ch03-02-data-types.md
  - experimental/eduratchet2/runs/rust-moves/observations/009-arithmetic-on-integers.rs
status: accepted
```

```text
id: 010-line-comments
move: add `//` somewhere on a line so rustc ignores everything from `//` to the end of the line
main_concept: `//` starts a line comment; rustc ignores everything from `//` to end of line; works at the start of a line, trailing after code, or to comment-out a `;`-terminated statement so it does not run
depends_on: 001-rustc-compile-and-run, 002-fn-main-entry-point, 004-statements-in-order, ordinary computer-use (editor)
unlocks: future "doc comments ///" moves, future "block comments /* */" moves, future "annotated probes use //" moves
evidence:
  - output/docs/rust/book/ch03-04-comments.md
  - experimental/eduratchet2/runs/rust-moves/observations/010-line-comments.rs
status: accepted
```

```text
id: 011-println-positional-args
move: print multiple values by writing one `{}` positional placeholder per slot in the println! format string and supplying matching extra arguments
main_concept: each `{}` in a println! format string is a positional placeholder consumed by one extra argument from the comma-separated list after the format string; placeholders match arguments left-to-right; you can pass an expression like `a + b` as an argument directly without binding it first
depends_on: 001-rustc-compile-and-run, 002-fn-main-entry-point, 004-statements-in-order, 005-let-binding, 009-arithmetic-on-integers, ordinary computer-use (editor)
unlocks: future "format specifiers" moves, future "Debug formatting {:?}" moves, future "format! and eprintln!" moves, future "named + positional mixing" moves
evidence:
  - output/docs/rust/std/macro.println.md
  - experimental/eduratchet2/runs/rust-moves/observations/011-println-positional-args.rs
status: accepted
```

```text
id: 012-bool-literals
move: bind a name to `true` or `false` and print it
main_concept: Rust has two boolean literal values, `true` and `false`, written as bare keywords; you can bind them with `let` and print them with the named or positional placeholder; the printed text is the word `true` or `false`
depends_on: 001-rustc-compile-and-run, 002-fn-main-entry-point, 004-statements-in-order, 005-let-binding, ordinary computer-use (editor)
unlocks: future "comparison operators" moves, future "if/else expressions" moves, future "logical operators && || !" moves, future "type annotations on let" moves
evidence:
  - output/docs/rust/book/ch03-02-data-types.md
  - experimental/eduratchet2/runs/rust-moves/observations/012-bool-literals.rs
status: accepted
```

```text
id: 013-comparison-operators
move: combine two integer values with `==`, `!=`, `<`, `<=`, `>`, or `>=` to produce a boolean, then bind and print it
main_concept: Rust's six comparison operators (`==`, `!=`, `<`, `<=`, `>`, `>=`) take two values of the same kind and produce a `true` or `false` (a value of the boolean kind from lesson 012); the result fits on the right of `let` and prints as the word `true` or `false`
depends_on: 001-rustc-compile-and-run, 002-fn-main-entry-point, 004-statements-in-order, 005-let-binding, 009-arithmetic-on-integers, 012-bool-literals, ordinary computer-use (editor)
unlocks: future "if/else expressions" moves, future "logical operators && || !" moves, future "comparing other kinds of values" moves, future "PartialEq / PartialOrd traits" moves
evidence:
  - output/docs/rust/book/appendix-02-operators.md
  - output/docs/rust/reference/expressions/operator-expr.md
  - experimental/eduratchet2/runs/rust-moves/observations/013-comparison-operators.rs
status: accepted
```

```text
id: 014-if-else
move: write `if condition { ... } else { ... }` to run one of two blocks based on a boolean condition
main_concept: `if condition { ... } else { ... }` evaluates the condition, which must produce a `true` or `false`; if `true`, the first block runs and the `else` block is skipped; if `false`, the `else` block runs instead
depends_on: 001-rustc-compile-and-run, 002-fn-main-entry-point, 004-statements-in-order, 005-let-binding, 012-bool-literals, 013-comparison-operators, ordinary computer-use (editor)
unlocks: future "else if chains" moves, future "if as expression in let" moves, future "loops (loop/while/for)" moves, future "match expressions" moves, future "if let" moves
evidence:
  - output/docs/rust/book/ch03-05-control-flow.md
  - experimental/eduratchet2/runs/rust-moves/observations/014-if-else.rs
status: accepted
```

```text
id: 015-logical-operators
move: combine boolean values with `&&`, `||`, or `!` to produce a new boolean, then bind and print
main_concept: Rust's logical operators act on booleans: `&&` is true when both sides are true, `||` is true when either side is true, `!` flips a boolean; the result is a boolean (lesson 012) that fits on the right of `let` and prints as the word `true` or `false`
depends_on: 001-rustc-compile-and-run, 002-fn-main-entry-point, 004-statements-in-order, 005-let-binding, 012-bool-literals, 013-comparison-operators, ordinary computer-use (editor)
unlocks: future "short-circuit evaluation semantics" moves, future "bitwise operators & | ^" moves, future "operator precedence" moves, future "if conditions with logical operators" moves
evidence:
  - output/docs/rust/book/appendix-02-operators.md
  - experimental/eduratchet2/runs/rust-moves/observations/015-logical-operators.rs
status: accepted
```

```text
id: 016-else-if-chain
move: extend an `if`/`else` with one or more `else if condition { ... }` arms; first matching condition wins
main_concept: `else if` arms add extra branches to lesson 014's two-armed shape; Rust checks conditions in source order and runs the first block whose condition is `true`, skipping all later arms; the optional final `else` catches the case where every earlier condition was `false`
depends_on: 001-rustc-compile-and-run, 002-fn-main-entry-point, 004-statements-in-order, 005-let-binding, 012-bool-literals, 013-comparison-operators, 014-if-else, ordinary computer-use (editor)
unlocks: future "match expressions" moves, future "if as expression in let" moves, future "no-else-arm chains" moves
evidence:
  - output/docs/rust/book/ch03-05-control-flow.md
  - experimental/eduratchet2/runs/rust-moves/observations/016-else-if-chain.rs
status: accepted
```

```text
id: 017-while-loop
move: write `while condition { ... }` to repeat a block as long as the condition is `true`
main_concept: `while condition { ... }` checks the condition before each pass; if `true`, the block runs, then the condition is checked again; if `false`, the loop exits and execution continues with the next statement; the body must update something the condition depends on, otherwise the loop never ends
depends_on: 001-rustc-compile-and-run, 002-fn-main-entry-point, 004-statements-in-order, 005-let-binding, 006-mut-binding, 009-arithmetic-on-integers, 013-comparison-operators, 014-if-else, ordinary computer-use (editor)
unlocks: future "loop / break / continue" moves, future "for loops" moves, future "while let" moves, future "loop labels" moves, future "compound assignment operators" moves
evidence:
  - output/docs/rust/book/ch03-05-control-flow.md
  - experimental/eduratchet2/runs/rust-moves/observations/017-while-loop.rs
status: accepted
```

```text
id: 018-block-comments
move: write a block comment with `/* ... */` that rustc ignores
main_concept: a block comment opens with `/*` and runs until the matching `*/`; it can span multiple lines or sit inline between code on a single line; block comments are the second non-doc comment form alongside lesson 010's `//` line comments
depends_on: 001-rustc-compile-and-run, 002-fn-main-entry-point, 004-statements-in-order, 010-line-comments, ordinary computer-use (editor)
unlocks: future "doc comments ///" moves, future "doc block comments /** */" moves, future "nested block comments" moves
evidence:
  - output/docs/rust/reference/comments.md
  - experimental/eduratchet2/runs/rust-moves/observations/018-block-comments.rs
status: accepted
```

```text
id: 019-type-annotation-i32
move: write `let name: i32 = value;` to annotate a binding with the type i32
main_concept: every Rust value has a type; rustc usually infers it from the value, but you can write it explicitly with `let name: TYPE = value;` (a type annotation between the name and the `=`); for plain integer literals the default and most common annotation is `i32`
depends_on: 001-rustc-compile-and-run, 002-fn-main-entry-point, 004-statements-in-order, 005-let-binding, 009-arithmetic-on-integers, ordinary computer-use (editor)
unlocks: future "other integer types i8/u32/etc" moves, future "floating-point types f32/f64" moves, future "type errors E0308" moves, future "function parameters with types" moves, future "function return types" moves
evidence:
  - output/docs/rust/book/ch03-02-data-types.md
  - experimental/eduratchet2/runs/rust-moves/observations/019-type-annotation-i32.rs
status: accepted
```

```text
id: 020-function-with-parameter
move: define `fn name(p: i32) { ... }` and call it with `name(value);` to pass an argument to a function
main_concept: a function definition can take parameters in the form `name: TYPE` between its parentheses; inside the body the parameter behaves like a `let` binding holding the value the caller passed; at the call site, `name(value);` supplies the value as an argument; in function signatures the type of each parameter is mandatory — calling rustc on a parameter without a type produces a parse error
depends_on: 001-rustc-compile-and-run, 002-fn-main-entry-point, 003-read-rustc-diagnostic, 008-define-and-call-function, 019-type-annotation-i32, ordinary computer-use (editor)
unlocks: future "multiple parameters" moves, future "function return values" moves, future "&T reference parameters" moves, future "&mut T parameters" moves, future "mut parameters" moves, future "generic parameters" moves
evidence:
  - output/docs/rust/book/ch03-03-how-functions-work.md
  - experimental/eduratchet2/runs/rust-moves/observations/020-function-with-parameter.rs
status: accepted
```

```text
id: 021-function-return-value
move: define `fn name(p: i32) -> i32 { return value; }` and use the call as a value, e.g. on the right of `let`
main_concept: a function can declare a return type after `->`; inside the body `return value;` sends a value back to the caller and exits the function; at the call site, `name(args)` is an expression whose value is what was returned, so it fits anywhere a value of that type can sit (most directly on the right of `let`); without `-> RTYPE`, the function returns nothing and cannot be used as a value
depends_on: 001-rustc-compile-and-run, 002-fn-main-entry-point, 005-let-binding, 008-define-and-call-function, 009-arithmetic-on-integers, 019-type-annotation-i32, 020-function-with-parameter, ordinary computer-use (editor)
unlocks: future "implicit final-expression return" moves, future "statement vs expression" moves, future "unit type ()" moves, future "tuple returns" moves, future "Result/Option returns" moves, future "early return semantics" moves
evidence:
  - output/docs/rust/book/ch03-03-how-functions-work.md
  - experimental/eduratchet2/runs/rust-moves/observations/021-function-return-value.rs
status: accepted
```

```text
id: 022-for-range
move: write `for var in 0..N { ... }` to repeat the body N times, binding `var` to each value in the range
main_concept: `for var in 0..N { ... }` runs the body once for each number in the exclusive range `0..N`; on each pass `var` is bound to that pass's number; the range `0..N` produces 0, 1, ..., N-1 and does NOT include N; unlike `while`, the loop variable is auto-advanced (no `mut` and no manual `+ 1` needed)
depends_on: 001-rustc-compile-and-run, 002-fn-main-entry-point, 004-statements-in-order, 005-let-binding, 017-while-loop, ordinary computer-use (editor)
unlocks: future "arrays and for-element-in-a" moves, future "inclusive ranges 0..=N" moves, future "range methods .rev() / .step_by()" moves, future "iterators and Iterator trait" moves, future "for with break/continue" moves
evidence:
  - output/docs/rust/book/ch03-05-control-flow.md
  - experimental/eduratchet2/runs/rust-moves/observations/022-for-range.rs
status: accepted
```

```text
id: 023-compound-add-assign
move: use `n += value;` to add a value to a `mut` binding in place; analogous shorthands `-=`, `*=`, `/=` exist
main_concept: `n += value;` is shorthand for `n = n + value;` (lesson 006 reassignment + lesson 009 arithmetic); requires `n` to be declared with `let mut`; the same shape works for `-=`, `*=`, `/=` with their corresponding operators
depends_on: 001-rustc-compile-and-run, 002-fn-main-entry-point, 004-statements-in-order, 005-let-binding, 006-mut-binding, 009-arithmetic-on-integers, ordinary computer-use (editor)
unlocks: future "remainder compound %=" moves, future "bitwise compound &= |= ^= <<= >>=" moves, future "AddAssign and other compound-assignment traits" moves
evidence:
  - output/docs/rust/book/appendix-02-operators.md
  - experimental/eduratchet2/runs/rust-moves/observations/023-compound-add-assign.rs
status: accepted
```

```text
id: 024-statement-vs-expression
move: write `let x: i32 = { let inner: i32 = 2; inner + 3 };` to bind `x` to the value of a block; the block's value is the value of its final inner expression, written without a trailing `;`
main_concept: Rust distinguishes statements (instructions that perform an action and do not return a value) from expressions (evaluate to a value); a `;` after an expression turns it into a statement and discards the value; a block `{ ... }` is itself an expression whose value is the value of its final inner expression, written without a trailing `;`
depends_on: 001-rustc-compile-and-run, 002-fn-main-entry-point, 003-read-rustc-diagnostic, 004-statements-in-order, 005-let-binding, 009-arithmetic-on-integers, 019-type-annotation-i32, ordinary computer-use (editor)
unlocks: future "implicit final-expression return" moves, future "if/else as expression in let" moves, future "match as expression" moves, future "loop with break value" moves, future "unit type ()" moves, future "block scope" moves
evidence:
  - output/docs/rust/book/ch03-03-how-functions-work.md
  - experimental/eduratchet2/runs/rust-moves/observations/024-statement-vs-expression.rs
  - broken-contrast probe transcript inline in lesson `## Evidence` (not committed)
status: accepted
```

```text
id: 025-implicit-return
move: drop `return` and the trailing `;` from a returning function body — write `fn add_one(n: i32) -> i32 { n + 1 }` instead of `fn add_one(n: i32) -> i32 { return n + 1; }`
main_concept: a function body is itself a block; lesson 024's rule applies — the block's value is its tail expression (no `;`), and `-> RTYPE` makes that value the function's return value, so `return value;` and a bare `value` on the last line are equivalent in this position; the bare form is idiomatic Rust; adding a stray `;` to the tail line drops the block to `()` and rustc reports E0308 with the body annotation `implicitly returns () as its body has no tail or return expression` plus a `help: remove this semicolon to return this value` line
depends_on: 001-rustc-compile-and-run, 002-fn-main-entry-point, 003-read-rustc-diagnostic, 005-let-binding, 008-define-and-call-function, 009-arithmetic-on-integers, 019-type-annotation-i32, 020-function-with-parameter, 021-function-return-value, 024-statement-vs-expression, ordinary computer-use (editor)
unlocks: future "if/else as expression in function body" moves, future "match as function body expression" moves, future "unit type ()" moves, future "early return inside if/else arms" moves, future "mixing implicit return with explicit return" moves
evidence:
  - output/docs/rust/book/ch03-03-how-functions-work.md
  - experimental/eduratchet2/runs/rust-moves/observations/025-implicit-return.rs
  - broken-contrast probe transcript inline in lesson `## Evidence` (not committed)
status: accepted
```

```text
id: 026-if-as-expression
move: put `if condition { value_a } else { value_b }` on the right of `let` and bind to whichever arm's value the condition selects (e.g. `let category: i32 = if n > 5 { 100 } else { -100 };`)
main_concept: lesson 014's `if`/`else` is itself an expression, not just a control-flow statement; each arm `{ ... }` is a block (lesson 024) whose value is its tail expression (no `;`); the whole `if`/`else` evaluates to the value of whichever arm ran, so it sits anywhere a value can — most directly on the right of `let`; the two arms must produce the same type, otherwise rustc emits the specialized headline `error[E0308]:` plus the words `if` and `else` have incompatible types (same E-code as lessons 024/025, different headline tailored to the two-arms situation)
depends_on: 001-rustc-compile-and-run, 002-fn-main-entry-point, 003-read-rustc-diagnostic, 005-let-binding, 009-arithmetic-on-integers, 012-bool-literals, 013-comparison-operators, 014-if-else, 019-type-annotation-i32, 024-statement-vs-expression, ordinary computer-use (editor)
unlocks: future "else if chains as expressions" moves, future "if/else as expression in function body" moves, future "if without else evaluating to ()" moves, future "match as expression" moves, future "loop with break value" moves, future "if let / while let" moves
evidence:
  - output/docs/rust/book/ch03-05-control-flow.md
  - experimental/eduratchet2/runs/rust-moves/observations/026-if-as-expression.rs
  - broken-contrast probe transcript inline in lesson `## Evidence` (not committed)
status: accepted
```

```text
id: 027-loop-and-break
move: write `loop { ... }` to repeat a block forever, and `break;` inside the body to exit it
main_concept: `loop { ... }` is Rust's unconditional-repetition construct — it runs the body, then runs it again, forever, with no built-in exit condition; `break;` inside the body terminates the loop and resumes execution at the next statement after the closing `}`; `loop` and `break;` are inseparable in practice — a `loop` without a `break` (or other early exit) never returns; together with `while` (lesson 017) and `for` (lesson 022) they form Rust's three loop constructs
depends_on: 001-rustc-compile-and-run, 002-fn-main-entry-point, 005-let-binding, 006-mut-binding, 013-comparison-operators, 014-if-else, 017-while-loop, 019-type-annotation-i32, 022-for-range, 023-compound-add-assign, ordinary computer-use (editor)
unlocks: future "`break value;` carrying a value out of `loop` (loop-as-expression, fourth surface of lesson 024's expression rule)" moves, future "`continue;` skip-to-next-pass" moves, future "loop labels `'name: loop { ... }`" moves, future "`while let` and `Option`-driven exits" moves, future "nested loops and inner-loop scope" moves
evidence:
  - output/docs/rust/book/ch03-05-control-flow.md
  - experimental/eduratchet2/runs/rust-moves/observations/027-loop-and-break.rs
status: accepted
```

```text
id: 028-break-value
move: add a value after `break` — `break value;` instead of lesson 027's `break;` — so the whole `loop { ... }` becomes an expression that produces `value`, bindable by `let`
main_concept: `break value;` does two things at once: terminates the loop exactly as `break;` does in lesson 027, AND carries `value` out of the loop so the whole `loop { ... }` is itself an expression whose value is the `value` passed to whichever `break` exited it; this is the *fourth surface* on which lesson 024's tail-expression rule applies — after let-block (024), function body (025), and `if`/`else` arms (026); without `break VALUE` (or another value-producing exit), a `loop { ... }` cannot be used as a value-producing expression — rustc rejects it with E0308 mismatched types, naming the unit type `()` as what the loop produces and emitting `help: give the break a value of the expected type` with a literal `break 42;` source-diff suggestion; closing lesson of the expression-rule arc 024→025→026→027→028
depends_on: 001-rustc-compile-and-run, 002-fn-main-entry-point, 003-read-rustc-diagnostic, 005-let-binding, 006-mut-binding, 009-arithmetic-on-integers, 013-comparison-operators, 014-if-else, 019-type-annotation-i32, 023-compound-add-assign, 024-statement-vs-expression, 027-loop-and-break, ordinary computer-use (editor)
unlocks: future "`continue;` skip-to-next-pass" moves, future "loop labels `'name: loop { ... break 'name value; }`" moves, future "`return` from inside a loop body" moves, future "`while let` and `Option`-driven exits" moves, future "`match` as expression" moves, future "the unit type `()`" moves
evidence:
  - output/docs/rust/book/ch03-05-control-flow.md
  - experimental/eduratchet2/runs/rust-moves/observations/028-break-value.rs
  - broken-contrast probe transcript inline in lesson `## Evidence` (not committed)
status: accepted
```

```text
id: 029-unit-type
move: name the recurring "nothing useful" type from prior lessons — it is *the unit type*, written `()`; its one value is also written `()`; bind it explicitly with `let _name: () = ();` (or via an empty block, or a no-`->` function call)
main_concept: `()` is a real Rust type with exactly one value, also written `()`; three places it shows up in code already accepted in this run: (1) the literal `()` itself; (2) any block `{ ... }` whose final inner line ends in `;`, including the empty block `{}` (lesson 024); (3) a call to a function declared without `-> RTYPE` (lesson 021); the corpus name is the *unit type* with the *unit value* spelled the same way; this lesson resolves the calibration debt accrued in lessons 021, 024, 025, 026, 027, and 028, all of which referred to `()` as "nothing useful / no value"
depends_on: 001-rustc-compile-and-run, 002-fn-main-entry-point, 003-read-rustc-diagnostic, 005-let-binding, 008-define-and-call-function, 019-type-annotation-i32, 021-function-return-value, 024-statement-vs-expression, 025-implicit-return, 026-if-as-expression, 027-loop-and-break, 028-break-value, ordinary computer-use (editor)
unlocks: future "tuple types with non-zero arity (i32,i32) etc." moves, future "the `_` binding-name convention and `_` wildcard pattern" moves, future "rustc warnings vs errors as a category distinction" moves, future "`if`-without-`else` evaluating to `()`" moves, future "`Result<(), E>` as a return type for `fn main`" moves, future "pattern-matching `()` and tuple destructuring" moves
evidence:
  - output/docs/rust/book/ch03-02-data-types.md
```

```text
id: 030-match-on-bool
move: write `match value { pattern => arm_value, pattern => arm_value }` to compare a value against a list of patterns and produce the value of the matching arm; the smallest useful surface is `match` on a `bool` with `true` and `false` as the two arms
main_concept: `match` is a Rust expression with a *scrutinee* (the value matched against) and a list of *arms* of the form `pattern => arm_expression`, separated by `,`; the matching arm's expression becomes the value of the whole `match`; all arm expressions must share a type; **exhaustiveness is mandatory** — every possible value of the scrutinee's type must be covered by some arm or rustc emits a new E-code, **E0004 non-exhaustive patterns**, naming exactly which pattern is missing; on a `bool` scrutinee, both `true` and `false` must appear; this is the *fifth surface* on which lesson 024's tail-expression rule applies (after let-block, function body, if/else arms, loop+break) — with new syntax (`=>` plus a bare expression, no `;`) and exhaustiveness enforced
depends_on: 001-rustc-compile-and-run, 002-fn-main-entry-point, 003-read-rustc-diagnostic, 005-let-binding, 012-bool-literals, 014-if-else, 019-type-annotation-i32, 024-statement-vs-expression, 026-if-as-expression, 029-unit-type, ordinary computer-use (editor)
unlocks: future "match with integer-literal patterns and `_` wildcard" moves, future "the `_` wildcard pattern in `let` and `match`" moves, future "match arms with multi-statement blocks `pattern => { ...; tail }`" moves, future "match guards `pattern if condition => arm`" moves, future "or-patterns `p1 | p2 => arm`" moves, future "range patterns `1..=5 => arm`" moves, future "`if let` / `while let`" moves, future "match against enum variants" moves
evidence:
  - output/docs/rust/reference/expressions/match-expr.md
  - output/docs/rust/book/ch06-02-match.md
  - output/docs/rust/error_codes/E0004.md
  - output/docs/rust/reference/patterns.md
  - experimental/eduratchet2/runs/rust-moves/observations/030-match-on-bool.rs
  - broken-contrast probe transcript inline in lesson `## Evidence` (not committed)
status: accepted
```

```text
id: 031-match-integer-and-wildcard
move: extend lesson 030's `match` to an integer scrutinee with `match n { 1 => 10, 2 => 20, 3 => 30, _ => 99, }`, using the `_` wildcard pattern as the final arm to catch every integer value not named by an earlier arm
main_concept: lesson 030's `match` machine carries over unchanged to integer scrutinees (matching arm wins, arms share a type, exhaustiveness enforced via E0004); two new pattern forms install together — any *integer literal* (`1`, `2`, `3`, ...) is a pattern that matches that exact integer value (the same general rule lesson 030 used for `true`/`false`-as-patterns), and the `_` *wildcard pattern* matches any value of any type; the pair is inseparable in practice because integer types like `i32` are unbounded — listing every literal is impossible, so a `match` on an integer scrutinee needs `_` (or another catch-all) as the final arm
depends_on: 001-rustc-compile-and-run, 002-fn-main-entry-point, 003-read-rustc-diagnostic, 005-let-binding, 019-type-annotation-i32, 030-match-on-bool, ordinary computer-use (editor)
unlocks: future "range patterns `1..=5 => arm`" moves, future "or-patterns `p1 | p2 => arm`" moves, future "match guards `pattern if condition => arm`" moves, future "match arms with multi-statement blocks" moves, future "match against enum variants" moves, future "negative integer literals as patterns" moves, future "named binding patterns `other => use(other)`" moves, future "`_` binding-name convention vs `_` wildcard pattern" moves, future "if let / while let" moves
evidence:
  - output/docs/rust/reference/patterns.md
  - output/docs/rust/error_codes/E0004.md
  - output/docs/rust/book/ch06-02-match.md
  - experimental/eduratchet2/runs/rust-moves/observations/031-match-integer-and-wildcard.rs
```

```text
id: 032-cargo-new-and-run
move: create a Rust package with `cargo new <name>` and run its program with `cargo run`
main_concept: cargo is Rust's package manager and build tool; `cargo new <name>` scaffolds a directory <name>/ containing a `Cargo.toml` manifest (with [package] name/version/edition and [dependencies]) and a `src/main.rs` source file holding a default `fn main` that prints "Hello, world!"; `cargo run` from inside that directory compiles `src/main.rs` and runs the resulting executable in one command, replacing lesson 001's two-step `rustc file.rs` + `./file` workflow; lesson 002's `fn main` entry-point rule still applies — cargo is a higher-level wrapper around rustc plus a project-layout convention
depends_on: 001-rustc-compile-and-run, 002-fn-main-entry-point, ordinary computer-use (terminal, editor, cargo on PATH)
unlocks: future "Cargo.toml manifest format" moves, future "cargo build" moves, future "cargo check" moves, future "cargo test" moves, future "cargo add / dependencies" moves, future "cargo new --lib (libraries vs binaries)" moves, future "editions" moves, future "crates.io" moves, future "release vs dev profiles (--release)" moves
```

```text
id: 033-f64-floats
move: bind a floating-point number with `let x: f64 = 5.0 / 3.0;`; the result is `1.6666666666666667`, not `1` -- float division does not truncate the way integer division does
main_concept: `f64` is Rust's floating-point type (numbers with decimal points); a float literal is written with a `.` (e.g. `5.0`, `3.14`); the default float type for a literal like `5.0` is `f64`; lesson 009's `+ - * /` operators all work on floats too, but `/` does NOT truncate when both operands are floats -- `5.0 / 3.0` evaluates to roughly `1.6666666666666667`; integer and float types are distinct, so rustc rejects `let x: f64 = 3;` (integer literal in `f64` slot) with E0308 plus `help: use a float literal` source-diff suggesting `3.0`
depends_on: 001-rustc-compile-and-run, 002-fn-main-entry-point, 003-read-rustc-diagnostic, 005-let-binding, 009-arithmetic-on-integers, 019-type-annotation-i32, ordinary computer-use (editor)
unlocks: future "f32 (32-bit float)" moves, future "as casts between numeric types" moves, future "other integer types i64/u32/usize" moves, future "IEEE 754 / NaN / infinity" moves, future "float comparison gotchas" moves, future "format specifiers like {:.2}" moves, future "float literal suffixes / scientific notation" moves
evidence:
  - output/docs/rust/book/ch03-02-data-types.md
  - experimental/eduratchet2/runs/rust-moves/observations/033-f64-floats.rs
  - broken-contrast probe transcript inline in lesson `## Evidence` (not committed)
status: accepted
```

```text
id: 034-as-cast-i32-to-f64
move: convert an `i32` value to `f64` with `value as f64` so it can mix with float arithmetic, e.g. `let avg: f64 = (count as f64) / 2.0;` where `count: i32`
main_concept: Rust does not implicitly convert between numeric types -- an `i32` and an `f64` cannot share an arithmetic operator directly; the way to bridge them is an explicit type cast, written with the binary operator `as`: the expression `value as TARGET_TYPE` produces a new value of `TARGET_TYPE` with the same numeric value as `value`; this lesson installs only the `i32 as f64` direction (always exact for `i32` values, since every `i32` fits in `f64`'s 52-bit significand); once converted, lesson 033's float arithmetic rules apply unchanged
depends_on: 001-rustc-compile-and-run, 002-fn-main-entry-point, 003-read-rustc-diagnostic, 005-let-binding, 009-arithmetic-on-integers, 019-type-annotation-i32, 033-f64-floats, ordinary computer-use (editor)
unlocks: future "`f64 as i32` (truncating float-to-integer cast)" moves, future "integer-width casts (`i32 as i64`, `i32 as u8`)" moves, future "signed/unsigned reinterpretation `-1_i32 as u32`" moves, future "`as` on non-numeric types (raw pointers, function pointers, `bool`/`char`)" moves, future "operator precedence" moves, future "`From`/`Into` and `TryFrom`/`TryInto` traits" moves, future "traits and generics" moves
evidence:
  - output/docs/rust/reference/expressions/operator-expr.md
  - output/docs/rust/rust-by-example/types/cast.md
  - experimental/eduratchet2/runs/rust-moves/observations/034-as-cast-i32-to-f64.rs
  - broken-contrast probe transcript inline in lesson `## Evidence` (not committed)
status: accepted
```

```text
id: 035-continue
move: write `continue;` inside a loop body to skip the rest of the current pass and immediately start the next pass; the loop's stop condition still applies
main_concept: `continue;` is a sibling of lesson 027's `break;` — both interrupt the loop body, but `break;` terminates the loop while `continue;` skips the rest of *this* pass and returns to the loop head; in `for var in 0..N` (lesson 022) the head advances `var` to the next value, in `while condition` (lesson 017) the head re-checks the condition, in `loop { ... }` (lesson 027) the head re-runs the body from the top; the natural shape is `if condition { continue; }`; together with `break;` and `break value;` (lesson 028) this completes the loop-control trio; unlike `break value;`, there is no `continue value;` form
depends_on: 001-rustc-compile-and-run, 002-fn-main-entry-point, 005-let-binding, 006-mut-binding, 013-comparison-operators, 014-if-else, 019-type-annotation-i32, 022-for-range, 023-compound-add-assign, 027-loop-and-break, ordinary computer-use (editor)
unlocks: future "labeled `continue 'name;` for nested loops" moves, future "`continue;` outside a loop body — diagnostic E0268" moves, future "`while let` and `Option`-driven exits" moves, future "iterator adapter `.filter(|x| ...)`" moves, future "nested loops and inner-loop scope" moves
evidence:
  - output/docs/rust/book/ch03-05-control-flow.md
  - output/docs/rust/reference/expressions/loop-expr.md
  - experimental/eduratchet2/runs/rust-moves/observations/035-continue.rs
status: accepted
```

```text
id: 036-multiple-parameters
move: define a function with more than one parameter — `fn add(a: i32, b: i32) -> i32 { a + b }` — and call it with the matching number of comma-separated arguments — `add(2, 3)`
main_concept: a function signature can take multiple parameters separated by `,`, each in lesson 020's `name: TYPE` shape; at the call site you supply that many comma-separated argument values, matched positionally; rustc enforces *arity* (number of arguments == number of parameters) and a mismatch is a new E-code, **E0061** with `note: function defined here` cross-referencing the definition via a secondary `-->` (the dual-`-->` pattern lesson 003 first observed); Rust has no default arguments, no named arguments, no variadic functions in safe code
depends_on: 001-rustc-compile-and-run, 002-fn-main-entry-point, 003-read-rustc-diagnostic, 005-let-binding, 008-define-and-call-function, 009-arithmetic-on-integers, 019-type-annotation-i32, 020-function-with-parameter, 021-function-return-value, 025-implicit-return, ordinary computer-use (editor)
```

```text
id: 037-remainder-operator
move: use `%` between two integer values to produce the *remainder* after integer division, e.g. `let r: i32 = 10 % 3;` binds `r` to `1`
main_concept: `%` is the *remainder* operator, the fifth member of Rust's basic arithmetic family alongside lesson 009's `+ - * /`; for integer values `a` and `b` (`b` non-zero), `a % b` produces the integer left over after dividing `a` by `b`, satisfying `a == (a / b) * b + (a % b)`; lesson 009's integer `/` truncates toward zero so `10 / 3` is `3` and `10 % 3` is `1`; `%` is *remainder*, not mathematical *modulo* — for negative dividends the result has the sign of the dividend, but this lesson uses positive operands only
depends_on: 001-rustc-compile-and-run, 002-fn-main-entry-point, 005-let-binding, 009-arithmetic-on-integers, 019-type-annotation-i32, ordinary computer-use (editor)
unlocks: future "compound remainder-assign `%=`" moves, future "operator precedence" moves, future "negative-dividend behavior and `rem_euclid`" moves, future "`%` on floats" moves, future "even/odd checks via `n % 2 == 0`" moves, future "runtime panics (e.g. divide-by-zero)" moves
evidence:
  - output/docs/rust/book/ch03-02-data-types.md
  - output/docs/rust/book/appendix-02-operators.md
  - output/docs/rust/reference/expressions/operator-expr.md
  - experimental/eduratchet2/runs/rust-moves/observations/037-remainder-operator.rs
status: accepted
```

```text
id: 038-else-if-chain-as-expression
move: put a whole `if cond_a { ... } else if cond_b { ... } else if cond_c { ... } else { ... }` chain on the right of `let` and bind to the value of whichever arm's tail expression runs first
main_concept: lesson 016's multi-arm `if`/`else if`/`else` chain is *also* a value-producing expression: combining lesson 016 (first matching condition wins) with lesson 026 (`if`/`else` is itself an expression on the right of `let`), the whole chain evaluates to the value of whichever arm's tail expression ran first; lesson 026's same-type-arms rule generalizes — *every* arm's tail expression must produce the same type — and the trailing `else` is mandatory for a value-producing chain because the no-block fallback is `()` (lesson 029); no new mechanism — pure composition of lessons 016 + 024 + 026 + 029, licensed by the Reference's recursive `IfExpression → if Conditions Block ( else ( Block | IfExpression ) )?` grammar plus `[[expr.if.result]]` "an `if` expression evaluates to the same value as the executed block, or `()` if no block is evaluated" and `[[expr.if.type]]` "an `if` expression must have the same type in all situations"
depends_on: 001-rustc-compile-and-run, 002-fn-main-entry-point, 005-let-binding, 013-comparison-operators, 014-if-else, 016-else-if-chain, 019-type-annotation-i32, 024-statement-vs-expression, 026-if-as-expression, 029-unit-type, ordinary computer-use (editor)
```

```text
id: 039-inclusive-range
move: write `0..=N` (with `=` between the dots and `N`) instead of lesson 022's `0..N` to make a `for` loop also iterate the upper bound `N`
main_concept: Rust has two range syntaxes — lesson 022's `0..N` is the *exclusive* range producing `0, 1, ..., N-1`, and the new `0..=N` is the *inclusive* range producing `0, 1, ..., N-1, N`; both fit the same `for var in range { ... }` shape from lesson 022, the only syntactic delta is the `=` after the `..`, and the inclusive form runs the body exactly one more time (the extra pass binds `var` to `N` itself)
depends_on: 001-rustc-compile-and-run, 002-fn-main-entry-point, 005-let-binding, 006-mut-binding, 019-type-annotation-i32, 022-for-range, 023-compound-add-assign, ordinary computer-use (editor)

  - output/docs/rust/reference/expressions/range-expr.md
  - output/docs/rust/std/ops/struct.RangeInclusive.md
```

```text
id: 040-method-call-syntax
move: call a method on a value with `value.method(args)` syntax — concretely `let m: i32 = n.abs();` to compute the absolute value of an `i32`
main_concept: Rust has a method-call form `receiver.method(args)` distinct from lesson 008's free-function call form `name(args)`; many functions in the standard library are *associated with a specific type* and the natural way to call them is dot-notation on a value of that type; the dot-form does not introduce a new control-flow or scoping rule — it is a different *syntax* for calling a function; writing the free-function form `abs(n)` for a method-only name fires E0425 (same E-code as lessons 005 and 008) and rustc's `help:` block names the dot-form fix in plain English ("use the `.` operator to call the method ...") with a source-diff suggesting `n.abs()`
depends_on: 001-rustc-compile-and-run, 002-fn-main-entry-point, 003-read-rustc-diagnostic, 005-let-binding, 008-define-and-call-function, 019-type-annotation-i32, 020-function-with-parameter, 021-function-return-value, ordinary computer-use (editor)
unlocks: future "associated-function form `Type::name(args)` (the `::` form)" moves, future "`String::new()` and the `String` type" moves, future "method chaining `a.b().c()`" moves, future "methods that take a reference receiver `&self` / `&mut self`" moves, future "generic method calls `.parse::<u32>()`" moves, future "methods on `Result`/`Option` (`.expect`, `.unwrap`, `.is_ok`)" moves, future "iterator adapters `it.filter(...).map(...)`" moves, future "trait-defined methods and `impl Trait for Type`" moves, future "the `self` parameter and `impl` blocks" moves

  - output/docs/rust/reference/expressions/method-call-expr.md
  - output/docs/rust/book/ch05-03-method-syntax.md
  - output/docs/rust/std/primitive.i32.md
```

```text
id: 041-qualified-method-call
move: call a method using the qualified form `Type::method(receiver, args)` — concretely `let m: i32 = i32::abs(n);` is equivalent to lesson 040's `let m: i32 = n.abs();`
main_concept: Rust offers a second call shape for methods, the *qualified form* `Type::method(receiver, args)`, parallel to lesson 040's *dot form* `receiver.method(args)`; for the receiver-bearing methods of lesson 040, the two forms desugar to the same call (per the Reference's "all function calls are sugar for a more explicit fully-qualified syntax") and produce the same value, so `i32::abs(n)` and `n.abs()` evaluate to the same `i32`; the qualified form names the type explicitly and lists the receiver as the first argument; writing `i32::abs()` with zero arguments fires E0061 "this function takes 1 argument but 0 arguments were supplied" (same E-code lesson 036 installed; the `defined here` noun shifts from `function` to `method` to match the kind of item), confirming that the receiver is mandatory in the qualified form
depends_on: 001-rustc-compile-and-run, 002-fn-main-entry-point, 003-read-rustc-diagnostic, 005-let-binding, 019-type-annotation-i32, 020-function-with-parameter, 021-function-return-value, 036-multiple-parameters, 040-method-call-syntax, ordinary computer-use (editor)
unlocks: future "associated functions without a receiver — `String::new()`, `i32::MAX`, `Vec::with_capacity(10)`" moves, future "the `String` type" moves, future "nested paths `std::cmp::min(a, b)`" moves, future "`use` declarations to shorten paths" moves, future "trait-defined methods via the qualified form" moves, future "the trait-disambiguation form `<T as Trait>::method(...)`" moves, future "`Self::method` form inside `impl` blocks" moves, future "method-resolution autoref / autoderef" moves
evidence:
  - output/docs/rust/reference/expressions/call-expr.md
```

```text
id: 042-string-new
move: create a fresh empty `String` value with `let s: String = String::new();` and print it with `println!("empty: [{s}]");`
main_concept: lesson 041's qualified call form `Type::method(receiver, args)` had a receiver; some functions attached to a type take **no receiver at all** and are reachable *only* via the qualified path `Type::name(args)` with no value-side dot form (because there is no value to put a dot on); the standard-library convention is an associated function named `new` returning a fresh value of that type (per the Reference: "a common example is an associated function named `new` that returns a value of the type with which it is associated"), and the smallest concrete instance is `String::new()` returning an empty `String` (the standard heap-allocated growable text type, per the std page); writing `new()` instead of `String::new()` fires E0425 "cannot find function `new` in this scope" — same E-code as lessons 005, 008, 040 — because there is no global free function named `new`; unlike lesson 040's E0425 broken probe, rustc here does not auto-suggest the qualified path
depends_on: 001-rustc-compile-and-run, 002-fn-main-entry-point, 003-read-rustc-diagnostic, 005-let-binding, 019-type-annotation-i32, 040-method-call-syntax, 041-qualified-method-call, ordinary computer-use (editor)
unlocks: future "`String::from(\"literal\")` and `.to_string()` constructors" moves, future "`String` mutation `.push_str(\"...\")` / `.push(c)`" moves, future "string literal type `&str`" moves, future "`Vec<T>::new()` with explicit type" moves, future "`String::with_capacity(n)`" moves, future "ownership and `String` as a heap value" moves, future "`Default` trait and `Default::default()`" moves, future "`io::stdin().read_line(&mut s)` — first non-trivial method call on `String`" moves
evidence:
  - output/docs/rust/alloc/string/struct.String.md
  - output/docs/rust/reference/items/associated-items.md
  - output/docs/rust/book/ch02-00-guessing-game-tutorial.md
```

```text
id: 043-nested-module-paths
move: call a free function that lives in a nested module by writing the full path `module::submodule::name(args)` — concretely `let smaller: i32 = std::cmp::min(3, 5);` binds `smaller` to `3`
main_concept: the qualified path syntax `A::B::name` from lessons 041 and 042 was demonstrated with one type segment in front of `::` (`i32::abs`, `String::new`); the same `::`-separated path grammar generalizes to multi-segment paths whose leading segments are **modules** rather than types; a *module* is a namespace that holds functions, types, constants, and other items (per the Reference's "A module is a container for zero or more items" and the Book chapter 7-02's "Modules can also hold definitions for other items, such as structs, enums, constants, traits, ... functions"); `std` is the standard library's root module (per the std crate page: "the standard library can be accessed ... through the path `std`"), `std::cmp` is one of its submodules (per the std crate page: "divided into a number of focused modules ... mighty names like `std::slice` and `std::cmp`"), and `std::cmp::min` is a free function in that submodule that takes two values of the same kind and returns the smaller one (per the std page's `pub fn min<T>(v1: T, v2: T) -> T where T: Ord` signature, presented to the audience without surfacing the generic `T` or the `Ord` bound); the path is read left-to-right with `::` separating each segment; the final segment names the function and every earlier segment names a module; writing `min(3, 5)` without the path fires E0425 "cannot find function `min` in this scope" — same E-code as lessons 005, 008, 040, 042 — and rustc's `help:` block here suggests `use std::cmp::min;` (a future move not installed by this lesson; the same fix is achievable with the full qualified path that this lesson installs)
depends_on: 001-rustc-compile-and-run, 002-fn-main-entry-point, 003-read-rustc-diagnostic, 005-let-binding, 019-type-annotation-i32, 020-function-with-parameter, 021-function-return-value, 041-qualified-method-call, 042-string-new, ordinary computer-use (editor)
unlocks: future "`use` declarations to shorten nested paths" moves, future "`std::io::stdin()` and the io module" moves, future "the standard prelude" moves, future "defining your own modules with `mod`" moves, future "external crates and Cargo dependencies" moves, future "trait-defined functions and generics" moves, future "the `crate::` / `self::` / `super::` path roots" moves
evidence:
  - output/docs/rust/std/cmp/fn.min.md
  - output/docs/rust/std/cmp/fn.max.md
  - output/docs/rust/std/index.md
  - output/docs/rust/reference/items/modules.md
  - output/docs/rust/reference/paths.md
  - output/docs/rust/book/ch07-02-defining-modules-to-control-scope-and-privacy.md
  - output/docs/rust/error_codes/E0425.md
  - experimental/eduratchet2/runs/rust-moves/observations/043-nested-module-paths.rs
  - experimental/eduratchet2/runs/rust-moves/evidence/043-nested-module-paths.md
status: accepted
```

```text
id: 044-use-declaration
move: write `use std::cmp::min;` at the top of the source file so the rest of the file can call `min(3, 5)` by its final segment alone, instead of writing the full nested path `std::cmp::min(3, 5)` every time
main_concept: a `use` declaration at the top level of a `.rs` file (the keyword `use`, then a path, then `;`, sitting outside `fn main`) brings a name from a nested path into the *current scope*, so the rest of the file can use it by its final segment; per the Reference, the `use` "creates one or more local name bindings synonymous with some other path"; after `use std::cmp::min;`, the bare name `min` resolves to the same function `std::cmp::min` that lesson 043 reached via the full path; the full path still works after the `use` (the `use` adds the short alias, it does not remove the long form); a `use` line brings in *only the items it names* — calling a sibling like bare `max(3, 5)` after `use std::cmp::min;` fires E0425 with `help: consider importing this function` suggesting a separate `use std::cmp::max;`, the same E-code lessons 005, 008, 040, 042, and 043 installed
depends_on: 001-rustc-compile-and-run, 002-fn-main-entry-point, 003-read-rustc-diagnostic, 005-let-binding, 019-type-annotation-i32, 043-nested-module-paths, ordinary computer-use (editor)
unlocks: future "`use ... as alias;`" moves, future "glob imports `use ...::*;`" moves, future "nested-group imports `use std::{io, cmp::min};`" moves, future "`pub use` re-exports" moves, future "`use std::io;` then `io::stdin()` for the guessing-game read line" moves, future "import shadowing and conflicts" moves, future "the standard prelude" moves, future "relative path roots `use crate::` / `use self::` / `use super::`" moves, future "`use` inside functions or modules" moves
evidence:
  - output/docs/rust/reference/items/use-declarations.md
```

```text
id: 045-shared-reference
move: take a shared reference to a value with the prefix operator `&` — `let n: i32 = 42; let r: &i32 = &n;` binds `r` to a value of type `&i32` referring to `n`'s `i32`, and `println!("n = {n}, r = {r}");` prints `n = 42, r = 42`
main_concept: Rust has a *shared reference type* spelled `&T` (per the Reference's *Shared references* section: "A shared reference type is written `&type`") and a *prefix-`&` operator* (per the Reference's *Borrow operators* section: a unary prefix operator that "produces a reference (pointer) to the location that the value refers to"); given a binding holding a value of type `T`, the expression `&value` produces a value of type `&T` that refers to the original without copying or owning it; the reference is itself a value, bindable by `let` and carrying its own type annotation, so the lesson-019 slot accepts `: &i32`; `T` and `&T` are *distinct types* — putting a `T` value in a `&T` slot fires E0308 *mismatched types* (same E-code lessons 024, 025, 026, 028, 033 installed) with caret labels `expected `&i32`, found `i32`` and `expected due to this`, plus `help: consider borrowing here` source-diff inserting `&`; the `{}` placeholder in `println!` formats `&T` and the underlying `T` identically, so the working probe prints `42` twice (per the std `Display` page line 542 blanket impl `impl<T> Display for &T where T: Display + ?Sized`); deliberately not installed: the `*` dereference operator, `&mut T`, ownership/move semantics, the borrow checker (E0382/E0502/E0505/E0596), lifetimes, references as function parameters, the `Copy` trait, `&str`, smart pointers, the `Display` trait by name, generics, blanket impls
depends_on: 001-rustc-compile-and-run, 002-fn-main-entry-point, 003-read-rustc-diagnostic, 005-let-binding, 019-type-annotation-i32, 026-if-as-expression, ordinary computer-use (editor)
unlocks: future "`&mut T` mutable references" moves, future "`*r` dereference operator" moves, future "ownership and the `Copy` trait" moves, future "borrow checker E0382/E0502/E0505/E0596" moves, future "references as function parameters `fn f(r: &i32)`" moves, future "method-resolution autoref" moves, future "lifetimes `'a` and lifetime elision" moves, future "the `Deref` trait" moves, future "string slice type `&str`" moves, future "`io::stdin().read_line(&mut s)`" moves, future "the `Display` trait and blanket impls" moves
evidence:
  - output/docs/rust/reference/types/pointer.md
  - output/docs/rust/reference/expressions/operator-expr.md
  - output/docs/rust/book/ch04-02-references-and-borrowing.md
  - output/docs/rust/std/fmt/trait.Display.md
  - output/docs/rust/error_codes/E0308.md
```

```text
id: 046-reference-function-parameter
move: declare a function parameter typed `&i32` — `fn show(r: &i32) { ... }` — and call it by passing a borrow `show(&n)`; the function reads through the reference without consuming the caller's value
main_concept: lesson 020's parameter-type slot accepts any type, including a reference type from lesson 045; with `fn show(r: &i32)` the call site supplies a value of type `&i32` (typically by writing `&binding`), so `show(&n)` works while bare `show(n)` fires E0308 *mismatched types* at the call site with `expected `&i32`, found `i32`` and `help: consider borrowing here` suggesting the `&` (the diagnostic also names the function definition via a second `-->`, the dual-`-->` pattern lesson 036 first observed); pure composition of lessons 020 + 045 with no new mechanism, plus the empirical observation that after `show(&n);` the binding `n` is still usable in `main` (the structural reason — ownership / `Copy` — is deferred)
```

```text
id: 047-mutable-reference
move: take a mutable reference with the prefix operator `&mut` (binding the source as `let mut`), then write through it with `*r = newval;` — concretely `let mut n: i32 = 1; let r: &mut i32 = &mut n; *r = 99;` leaves `n` equal to `99`, and `println!("n = {n}");` prints `n = 99`
main_concept: Rust has a *mutable reference type* spelled `&mut T`, parallel to lesson 045's `&T` (per the Reference's *Mutable references* section: "A mutable reference type is written `&mut type`"), built by the *prefix `&mut` operator* (per the Reference's *Borrow operators* section: a unary prefix operator that "evaluates its operand in a mutable place expression context"); the source binding must be `let mut`-bound (lesson 006) for `&mut` to apply; through a `&mut T` you can WRITE to the original value with the *deref-assign* form `*r = newval;` (the prefix `*` on the *left* of `=`, per the Reference's *Dereference operator* clause: "If the expression is of type `&mut T` ... the resulting memory location can be assigned to"); `&T` and `&mut T` are *distinct types* — putting a `&T` value in a `&mut T` slot fires E0308 *mismatched types* (same E-code lessons 024, 025, 026, 028, 033, 045, 046 installed) with caret label `types differ in mutability` and `note: expected mutable reference &mut _ / found reference &_`; the working probe corroborates the write-through claim empirically (`n` reads `99` after `*r = 99;`); deliberately not installed: the borrow checker (E0382/E0499/E0502/E0505), E0596 (`&mut` on non-`mut` binding), E0594 (`*r = v` through `&T`), `*r` as a read-through, `&mut T` parameters, methods with `&mut self`, reborrowing, lifetimes, the `Copy` and `DerefMut` traits, raw borrows `&raw mut`
depends_on: 001-rustc-compile-and-run, 002-fn-main-entry-point, 003-read-rustc-diagnostic, 005-let-binding, 006-mut-binding, 019-type-annotation-i32, 045-shared-reference, ordinary computer-use (editor)

  - output/docs/rust/reference/types/pointer.md
```

```text
id: 048-mutable-reference-parameter
move: declare a function parameter typed `&mut i32` — `fn set_to_99(r: &mut i32) { *r = 99; }` — and call it by passing a mutable borrow `set_to_99(&mut n)`; the function writes through the parameter and the caller's `let mut`-bound `n` reads the new value after the call returns
main_concept: pure composition of lesson 046 ("the parameter-type slot accepts a reference type, and the call site supplies it via a prefix-borrow") with lesson 047 ("`&mut T` exists, the prefix `&mut` operator builds it from a `let mut`-bound place, and `*r = newval;` writes through it") — `fn set_to_99(r: &mut i32)` is the lesson-020 shape with `&mut i32` in the type slot; `set_to_99(&mut n)` is the lesson-047 prefix-`&mut` operator in argument position; `*r = 99;` is the lesson-047 deref-assign form inside a function body; the empirical observation is that after the call returns the caller's `n` reads `99` (structural reason — ownership, `Copy` — deferred); passing `&n` instead of `&mut n` fires E0308 *mismatched types* with caret label `types differ in mutability` (lesson 047's exact label) plus a second `-->` reading `note: function defined here` (lesson 046's exact pattern) and `= note: expected mutable reference &mut _ / found reference &_` (lesson 047's exact note); same E-code family lessons 024, 025, 026, 028, 033, 045, 046, 047 installed; no `help:` block (matching lesson 047's broken probe — rustc does not auto-insert `mut` once mutability disagrees); deliberately not installed: `*r` as read-through (right of `=`), the borrow checker (E0382/E0499/E0502/E0505), E0596 (`&mut` on non-`mut` binding), E0594 (`*r = v` through `&T`), lifetimes, methods with `&mut self` and autoref, reborrowing `&mut *r`, multiple parameters mixing `&T` and `&mut T`, reference return types `fn f() -> &mut T`, the `Copy` trait, ownership semantics
```

```text
id: 049-method-chaining
move: write a *method chain* `String::new().is_empty()` — two method-call expressions joined directly, with the result of the first becoming the receiver of the second — and bind the final result with `let chained: bool = ...;` (no intermediate `let`)
main_concept: lesson 040's method-call grammar `MethodCallExpression → Expression . PathExprSegment ( CallParams? )` names the receiver an *Expression*; that grammar is recursive — the leading `Expression` slot accepts any expression that produces a value of the right type, including another method-call expression or a no-receiver associated-function call (lesson 042); so `String::new().is_empty()` parses as `(String::new()).is_empty()` (left-associative), with `String::new()` (lesson 042, returning a fresh empty `String`) supplying the receiver of `.is_empty()`; the chain is equivalent in value to a two-step `let s = String::new(); s.is_empty();` rewrite — same call sequence, fewer bindings — and both forms produce `true` (since `pub const fn is_empty(&self) -> bool` returns `true` when the `String` has length zero, per `alloc/string/struct.String.md` line 1313); pure composition of lessons 040 + 042 with no new syntactic mechanism — the grammar is the same, but the receiver slot is filled with a call instead of a binding name
depends_on: 001-rustc-compile-and-run, 002-fn-main-entry-point, 005-let-binding, 012-bool-literals, 019-type-annotation-i32, 040-method-call-syntax, 042-string-new, ordinary computer-use (editor)
unlocks: future "longer chains `a.b().c().d()`" moves, future "iterator chains `it.filter(...).map(...).collect()`" moves, future "the builder pattern `Foo::new().a(...).b(...).build()`" moves, future "method resolution autoref/autoderef" moves, future "`?` operator in chain context" moves, future "`io::stdin().read_line(&mut buf).expect(...)` as a three-step chain" moves, future "`.parse::<T>()` and turbofish in chain context" moves, future "chains where intermediate methods return `()` (e.g. `s.push_str(\"x\").push_str(\"y\")` failure)" moves
evidence:
  - output/docs/rust/reference/expressions/method-call-expr.md
  - output/docs/rust/reference/expressions/call-expr.md
  - output/docs/rust/alloc/string/struct.String.md
```

```text
id: 050-io-stdin-handle
move: obtain a standard-input handle by calling the no-receiver free function `std::io::stdin()` in the standard library's `io` submodule, and bind it — `let _stdin = std::io::stdin();` compiles cleanly and `println!("got stdin handle");` runs to completion (no input is read)
main_concept: pure composition of (a) lesson 042's no-receiver associated-function call shape `Type::name(args)` generalized to a no-receiver *free function* in a module — `module::submodule::name(args)` — and (b) lesson 043's nested-module-path form (three `::`-separated segments, leading segments are modules, final segment names the function); the concrete path is `std::io::stdin` where `std` is the standard library's root module (per `std/index.md` lines 17-19, already cited in lesson 043), `std::io` is its input/output submodule (per `std/index.md` line 14's `[I/O](...) "mod std::io"` link target text and the existence of items at `std/io/...`), and `std::io::stdin` is a free function in that submodule whose signature is `pub fn stdin() -> Stdin` (per `std/io/fn.stdin.md` line 7) returning a value of type `std::io::Stdin` — a *handle to the standard input stream of a process* (per `std/io/struct.Stdin.md` line 12); the Book chapter 2 explicitly licenses the full-path call form `std::io::stdin` over the chapter's preferred `use std::io;` + `io::stdin()` shortened form ("we could still use the function by writing this function call as `std::io::stdin`", lines 276-278); the lesson installs only that the function exists, the path resolves, the call returns a value, and the type is named — *every* method on `Stdin` (`.read_line(&mut buf)`, `.lock()`, `.lines()`, etc.), the `Read`/`BufRead` traits, threading and the internal mutex, stdin singleton-ness, the `io::Result<T>` alias, the rest of the `io` module (`stdout()`, `stderr()`, `Stdout`, `Stderr`, `BufReader`, `BufWriter`), type-path syntax `: std::io::Stdin`, and `use std::io;` are all explicitly deferred; binding name `_stdin` uses lesson 029's gloss for the underscore-prefix convention to silence the unused-variable warning rustc would otherwise emit; no broken-contrast probe (central claim is *introduction*, not contrast — the working probe demonstrates the claim directly), but a positive *underscore-elision* side probe is captured in the evidence appendix to back the small calibration about the warning
```

```text
id: 051-ordering-enum-and-variant-match
move: bind a value of the standard-library enum type `std::cmp::Ordering` to a name and `match` it against its three variants `Ordering::Less`, `Ordering::Greater`, `Ordering::Equal` to choose one of three arms — concretely `let direction: Ordering = Ordering::Less; let label = match direction { Ordering::Less => "less", Ordering::Greater => "greater", Ordering::Equal => "equal" };` produces `"less"`
main_concept: composite move installing three closely-coupled facts. (1) An *enum* is a type whose values come from a fixed list of named alternatives called *variants*; per the Reference's *Enumerations* page (lines 25-27, "An *enumeration*, also referred to as an *enum*, is a simultaneous definition of a nominal enumerated type as well as a set of *constructors*, that can be used to create or pattern-match values of the corresponding enumerated type"); the standard library's `std::cmp::Ordering` is the canonical three-variant enum, declared as `pub enum Ordering { Less = -1, Equal = 0, Greater = 1 }` (per `std/cmp/enum.Ordering.md` lines 6-14) with each variant unit-like (no payload). (2) A variant is named by the qualified path `Type::Variant` — same `::` separator as lesson 042's `String::new()` and lesson 043's `std::cmp::min`, but the right-hand side names a *variant* rather than a function or sub-module; the path produces a value of the enum type, bindable by `let` and usable as a pattern in `match` (per `reference/patterns.md` lines 1146-1175 *Path patterns* "patterns that refer either to constant values or to structs or enum variants that have no fields"; "Unqualified path patterns can refer to: enum variants, structs, constants, associated constants"). (3) Lesson 030's `match` form generalizes to an enum scrutinee unchanged: arms are still `pattern => arm_expression,`, the matching arm's value is the value of the whole `match`, all arms share a type, and exhaustiveness is still enforced by E0004 — but for an enum the diagnostic names the missing *variant* by its full path (e.g. `\`std::cmp::Ordering::Equal\` not covered`) instead of a literal value (lesson 030's `\`false\`` shape) or a range (lesson 031's `i32::MIN..=0_i32` shape). The lesson's working probe uses `use std::cmp::Ordering;` (lesson 044's shape) so the rest of the file can write `Ordering` and `Ordering::Less` instead of the full `std::cmp::Ordering::Less`. The annotation `let direction: Ordering = ...;` extends lesson 019's `: TYPE` slot to a third concrete type (after `i32` in 019 and `String` in 042). String-literal arm values (`"less"`, etc.) have type `&str`; the lesson uses inference (`let label = match ...;`) and explicitly defers `&str` as a typed name. Deliberately not installed: defining your own enum with the `enum` keyword; variants with payload (`Some(T)`, `None`, `Ok(T)`, `Err(E)`); methods on `Ordering` (`is_eq`, `is_lt`, `reverse`, `then`, etc.); the `Ord` and `PartialOrd` traits; generic enums (`Option<T>`, `Result<T, E>`); the `cmp(&other)` method on `i32`; match guards `pattern if cond => arm`; `if let` / `while let`; the glob form `use std::cmp::Ordering::*;`; `#[derive(...)]`; discriminants; `&str` as a typed name; memory layout / niche optimization
depends_on: 001-rustc-compile-and-run, 002-fn-main-entry-point, 003-read-rustc-diagnostic, 005-let-binding, 019-type-annotation-i32, 030-match-on-bool, 031-match-integer-and-wildcard, 042-string-new, 043-nested-module-paths, 044-use-declaration, ordinary computer-use (editor)
unlocks: future "defining your own enum `enum Color { Red, Green, Blue }`" moves, future "variants with payload `Foo(i32)` / `Some(T)` / `None` / `Ok(T)` / `Err(E)`" moves, future "the `Option<T>` enum" moves, future "the `Result<T, E>` enum" moves, future "match guards `pattern if cond => arm`" moves, future "methods on `Ordering` (`is_eq`, `is_lt`, `reverse`, `then`)" moves, future "the `cmp(&other)` method on `i32` returning `Ordering`" moves, future "`if let` / `while let`" moves, future "`#[derive(...)]` on enums" moves, future "discriminants and explicit `Variant = N` values" moves, future "the glob form `use std::cmp::Ordering::*;`" moves, future "`&str` as a typed name and string-literal type" moves
evidence:
  - output/docs/rust/std/cmp/enum.Ordering.md
  - output/docs/rust/reference/items/enumerations.md
  - output/docs/rust/reference/patterns.md

status: accepted
```

```text
id: 052-result-enum-and-is-ok
move: define a function returning `Result<i32, i32>` that branches to `Ok(n)` for success or `Err(n)` for failure, call it, and inspect the returned value with `.is_ok()` — concretely `fn parity(n: i32) -> Result<i32, i32> { if n % 2 == 0 { Ok(n) } else { Err(n) } }` followed by `let a = parity(4); let b = parity(7); println!("a is ok: {}", a.is_ok()); println!("b is ok: {}", b.is_ok());` prints `a is ok: true` and `b is ok: false`
main_concept: composite move installing three closely-coupled facts. (1) Lesson 051 installed unit-only enums where each variant is a bare name; today generalizes to *variants with payload*: a variant can carry a value, written `Variant(T)` in the declaration (per the Reference's enum-variant grammar `EnumVariant → IDENTIFIER ( EnumVariantTuple | EnumVariantStruct )?` with `EnumVariantTuple → ( TupleFields? )` at `reference/items/enumerations.md` lines 15-19) and called as a function-like *constructor* `Variant(value)` at use sites that wraps `value` to produce a value of the enum type (per `reference/items/enumerations.md` lines 113-115 "A tuple-like variant can be instantiated with a call expression" and the `Examples::TupleLike(123) // Call expression` annotation at lines 117-137). (2) The standard library's `Result<T, E>` is the canonical two-variant payload-bearing enum, declared `pub enum Result<T, E> { Ok(T), Err(E) }` (per `std/result/enum.Result.md` lines 6-11) where `Ok(T)` carries the *success value* (line 23) and `Err(E)` carries the *error value* (line 29); `<T, E>` are *type parameters* — placeholders the user fills in at the use site (per Book ch09-02 lines 23-30 "The `T` and `E` are generic type parameters: ... `T` represents the type of the value that will be returned in a success case within the `Ok` variant, and `E` represents the type of the error that will be returned in a failure case within the `Err` variant"), so `Result<i32, i32>` says "Ok carries an `i32` and Err also carries an `i32`"; constructors `Ok(value)` and `Err(value)` build values of `Result<T, E>` by wrapping the payload (per Book ch09-02 line 87 "the `Result` enum and its variants have been brought into scope by the prelude, so we don't need to specify `Result::` before the `Ok` and `Err` variants" — no `use` line is needed). (3) `.is_ok()` is the simplest inspection method on `Result<T, E>`, with signature `pub const fn is_ok(&self) -> bool` (per `std/result/enum.Result.md` line 41); it returns `true` if the value is `Ok` and `false` if it is `Err` (per the same page's worked example at lines 47-52 with `Ok(-3).is_ok() == true` and `Err("...").is_ok() == false`); the working probe corroborates the truth-table empirically (`parity(4)` builds `Ok(4)` and prints `true`, `parity(7)` builds `Err(7)` and prints `false`). The probe uses lesson 026's `if`-as-expression for the function body, lesson 037's `%` and lesson 013's `==` for the parity check, lesson 021's return-type slot for `-> Result<i32, i32>`, lesson 040's dot-form for `.is_ok()`, and `bool`'s `{}` formatting (lessons 012, 013) for the println. Optional broken contrast: `let n: i32 = Ok(7);` fires E0308 *mismatched types* with `expected \`i32\`, found \`Result<{integer}, _>\`` and `note: ... found enum \`Result<{integer}, _>\``, confirming `Result<T, E>` is a distinct type from its payload (same E-code lessons 024-034, 045-048 installed). Deliberately not installed: match on payload variants (`match r { Ok(v) => ..., Err(e) => ... }`); `.expect("msg")` and `.unwrap()` (panic-on-Err); `Option<T>` and its `Some(T)` / `None` variants; generics in general (`<T>` on functions, where-clauses, trait bounds — today surfaces only the type-expression form `Result<i32, i32>`); the `?` operator; other Result methods (`.is_err()` mentioned only, plus `.unwrap_or`, `.map`, `.and_then`, `.is_ok_and`, `.ok()`, `.err()`); runtime panics (no panic path is exercised today — `.is_ok()` returns `bool`); custom error types and `Box<dyn Error>`; `Display` / `Debug` formatting on `Result`; the `io::Result<T>` type alias; trait machinery (`Eq`, `Debug` impls); `&str` as a typed name; the `&self` receiver shape; type-inference variables `{integer}` and `_`
depends_on: 001-rustc-compile-and-run, 002-fn-main-entry-point, 003-read-rustc-diagnostic, 005-let-binding, 008-define-and-call-function, 012-bool-literals, 013-comparison-operators, 019-type-annotation-i32, 020-function-with-parameter, 021-function-return-value, 025-implicit-return, 026-if-as-expression, 037-remainder-operator, 040-method-call-syntax, 051-ordering-enum-and-variant-match, ordinary computer-use (editor)
unlocks: future "match on payload variants `Ok(v) => ..., Err(e) => ...`" moves, future "`.expect(\"msg\")` and `.unwrap()` panic-on-Err methods" moves, future "the `Option<T>` enum with `Some(T)` / `None`" moves, future "the `?` operator for error propagation" moves, future "other Result methods (`.unwrap_or`, `.map`, `.and_then`, `.is_ok_and`, `.ok()`, `.err()`)" moves, future "generic functions `fn f<T>(x: T) -> T` and where-clauses" moves, future "custom error types `enum MyError { ... }` and `Box<dyn Error>`" moves, future "`io::Result<T>` type alias" moves, future "`Stdin::read_line(&mut buf)` returning `io::Result<usize>`" moves, future "Debug/Display formatting on enums (`{:?}`)" moves, future "defining your own enum with payload variants" moves, future "trait machinery on `Result` (`Eq`, `Debug`, `From`, `Into`)" moves, future "`&str` as a typed name and string-literal type" moves, future "the `&self` receiver shape" moves
evidence:
  - output/docs/rust/std/result/enum.Result.md
```
