```text
id: 053-result-expect-and-panic
move: call `.expect("msg")` on a `Result<T, E>` value to extract the `Ok` payload as a plain `T`, OR — if the Result is `Err` — terminate the program with a *runtime panic* whose message is `msg: <Err payload printed>` — concretely `fn parity(n: i32) -> Result<i32, i32> { if n % 2 == 0 { Ok(n) } else { Err(n) } }` followed by `let v: i32 = parity(4).expect("expected even"); println!("v = {v}");` prints `v = 4` and exits 0; changing `parity(4)` to `parity(7)` still compiles cleanly but the executable panics at run time, prints `thread 'main' (<id>) panicked at demo.rs:10:28:` then `expected even: 7` then the `RUST_BACKTRACE=1` trailer to stderr, never runs `println!`, and exits with status 101
main_concept: composite move installing two closely-coupled facts. (1) `.expect(msg)` is a method on `Result<T, E>` whose signature `pub fn expect(self, msg: &str) -> T where E: Debug` (per `std/result/enum.Result.md` line 433) reads "give me the `Ok` payload, or crash with this message" — on `Ok(value)` it evaluates to `value` as a plain `T` (per the same page line 435 "Returns the contained `Ok` value, consuming the `self` value") and execution continues; on `Err(value)` it panics with a panic message *including the passed message and the content of the `Err`* (per the same page lines 444-445 "Panics if the value is an `Err`, with a panic message including the passed message, and the content of the `Err`"); the worked example at line 453 `x.expect("Testing expect"); // panics with \`Testing expect: emergency failure\`` shows the literal `<msg>: <Err payload>` format with `:` as the joiner. (2) A *runtime panic* is Rust's name for an aborting termination triggered at execution time, *categorically different* from compile-time errors (per Book ch09-01 lines 4-11 "Rust has the `panic!` macro. There are two ways to cause a panic in practice: by taking an action that causes our code to panic ... or by explicitly calling the `panic!` macro. ... By default, these panics will print a failure message, unwind, clean up the stack, and quit"); the canonical panic-output shape is `thread 'main' panicked at <file>:<line>:<col>:` then the message line then the `note: run with \`RUST_BACKTRACE=1\` ...` trailer, all on stderr (per Book ch09-01 lines 51-53); a panic in the main thread ends the program with exit code 101 (per `std/macro.panic.md` lines 67-69 "If the main thread panics it will terminate all your threads and end your program with code 101"); subsequent statements in the panicking function never execute. The probe corroborates all of this empirically: working probe `parity(4).expect("expected even")` prints `v = 4` and exits 0; panic probe `parity(7).expect("expected even")` *compiles cleanly* (rustc exit 0 — no compile error) but at run time exits 101 with stderr containing exactly the canonical panic-block (with `expected even: 7` as the message line) and stdout empty (the following `println!` never runs). The receiver of `.expect` here is the call expression `parity(4)` (lesson 049's receiver-is-any-expression generalization); the lesson does not chain two methods. The Book ch09-02 audience-level passage (lines 234-253) confirms `.expect` "calls the `panic!` macro" internally, and Book ch02 lines 350-358 give the audience-level both-outcomes summary. Deliberately not installed: `.unwrap()` (no-message sibling, direct successor); the `where E: Debug` clause itself (deferred since 052); `&str` as a typed name (cycle-045 reference type, but the typed name `&str` for string literals is uninstalled); ownership / consume semantics (`self`-by-value — for `Result<i32, i32>` both payloads are `Copy` so the consume is invisible); Err-side methods `.unwrap_err`, `.expect_err`, `.unwrap_or(default)`, `.unwrap_or_else`, `.unwrap_or_default`, `.map_err`; pattern-matching the payload `match r { Ok(v) => ..., Err(e) => ... }`; the `?` operator; the `panic!` macro itself as a thing you write; unwind vs abort panic strategies; `std::panic::catch_unwind`; custom panic hooks `std::panic::set_hook`; the `#[panic_handler]` attribute; no_std panic handling; stack unwinding mechanics; drop-on-panic; the `RUST_BACKTRACE=1` environment variable as a mechanism; the parenthesized OS thread id `(125950362)` in the captured panic line; the recommended `expect`-message style (mirrored informally as `"expected even"`, not as a rule); other Result methods deferred since 052 (`.is_err`, `.is_ok_and`, `.ok()`, `.err()`, `.as_ref`, `.map`, `.and_then`, `.and`, `.or`); `Option<T>::expect` and `::unwrap`
depends_on: 001-rustc-compile-and-run, 002-fn-main-entry-point, 003-read-rustc-diagnostic, 005-let-binding, 008-define-and-call-function, 012-bool-literals, 013-comparison-operators, 019-type-annotation-i32, 020-function-with-parameter, 021-function-return-value, 025-implicit-return, 026-if-as-expression, 037-remainder-operator, 040-method-call-syntax, 049-method-chaining, 052-result-enum-and-is-ok, ordinary computer-use (terminal, editor, knowing `echo $?` shows shell exit status, knowing program output goes to stdout while diagnostic output goes to stderr)
unlocks: future "`.unwrap()` no-message sibling of `.expect`" moves, future "`.unwrap_or(default)` graceful-default Result handling" moves, future "match on `Ok(v) => ..., Err(e) => ...` payload variants" moves, future "the `?` operator for error propagation" moves, future "the `panic!` macro as a thing you write" moves, future "the `unwind` vs `abort` panic strategies" moves, future "`std::panic::catch_unwind`" moves, future "custom panic hooks `std::panic::set_hook`" moves, future "the `Debug` trait and `{:?}` formatting" moves, future "the `where E: Debug` clause and trait bounds in general" moves, future "`&str` as a typed name and string-literal type" moves, future "ownership / move / `self` consume" moves, future "`Stdin::read_line(&mut buf).expect(\"msg\")` as the canonical guessing-game form" moves, future "`io::Result<T>` type alias" moves, future "`Option<T>::expect` and `::unwrap`" moves, future "`#[panic_handler]` and no_std panic handling" moves, future "stack unwinding and drop on panic" moves, future "Err-side methods (`.unwrap_err`, `.expect_err`, `.map_err`, `.unwrap_or_else`, `.unwrap_or_default`)" moves
evidence:
  - output/docs/rust/std/result/enum.Result.md
  - output/docs/rust/book/ch09-01-unrecoverable-errors-with-panic.md
  - output/docs/rust/book/ch09-02-recoverable-errors-with-result.md
```

```text
id: 054-read-line-from-stdin
move: read one line of standard input by chaining the established pieces — `io::stdin().read_line(&mut buf).expect("Failed to read line");` reads one newline-terminated line of text from stdin and *appends* it (including the trailing `\n`) to the `String` named by `buf`; concretely, with `use std::io;` at the top of the file, the program `fn main() { let mut buf = String::new(); io::stdin().read_line(&mut buf).expect("Failed to read line"); println!("got: {buf}"); }` compiled with `rustc demo.rs` and invoked as `echo "hello" | ./demo` prints `got: hello` followed by a blank line (the blank line is the `\n` `read_line` appended to `buf`, then `println!`'s own newline) and exits 0
main_concept: pure composition of seven prior cycles plus one new method. The new piece: `Stdin` has a method `pub fn read_line(&self, buf: &mut String) -> io::Result<usize>` (per `std/io/struct.Stdin.md` line 83) that "Locks this handle and reads a line of input, appending it to the specified buffer" (line 85), with two load-bearing semantics from lines 88-92: "Previous content of the buffer will be preserved" (read_line *appends*, does not overwrite) and "The trailing newline character, if any, is included in the buffer" (the `\n` from Enter ends up in `buf`). The full canonical guessing-game statement `io::stdin().read_line(&mut buf).expect("Failed to read line");` (per Book ch02 lines 268-270 and the one-line equivalent at line 329) is just `read_line` plus seven installed cycles: (a) `io::stdin()` from cycle 050, now reached as bare `io::stdin()` rather than `std::io::stdin()` because line 1's `use std::io;` (cycle 044's parent-module `use` form, deferred from cycle 050 and picked up today — cycle 044 covered `use Path::name;` for both function-name and module-name targets, with the Book ch02 itself using the parent-module form); (b) `String::new()` from cycle 042 supplies the buffer; (c) `let mut buf` from cycle 006 makes the binding mutably-borrowable (without it E0596 would fire — deferred); (d) `&mut buf` from cycle 048 supplies the `&mut String` argument; (e) the three-call chain `io::stdin().read_line(...).expect(...)` is cycle 049's receiver-is-any-expression generalization extended one level deeper than cycle 053 (053 chained two — call expression as receiver of `.expect`; 054 chains three — call as receiver of `.read_line`, then chained expression as receiver of `.expect`); (f) cycle 052 supplies the `Result<T, E>` enum that `read_line` returns; (g) cycle 053 supplies `.expect("msg")` consuming a `Result<T, E>`, yielding the `Ok` payload (here a byte count) or panicking on `Err`. The lesson establishes that running such a program REQUIRES providing stdin input (per `std/io/struct.Stdin.md` lines 109-114 "Pipe some text to it, e.g., `printf foo | path/to/executable`" or "Give it text interactively by running the executable directly, in which case it will wait for the Enter key to be pressed before continuing"); the lesson's recipe uses `echo "..." | ./demo`. Optional broken contrast: passing `&buf` (shared reference) instead of `&mut buf` to `read_line` fires `error[E0308]: mismatched types` with caret label `types differ in mutability` and `= note:` block reading `expected mutable reference \`&mut String\` / found reference \`&String\`` plus a second `-->` to library internals (`/rustc/.../library/std/src/io/stdio.rs:411:11`). Same E-code family as cycles 045-048, with `String` substituted for `i32` as the underlying type. Captured verbatim in the evidence appendix. The `Ok` payload of `read_line` is a `usize` byte count (per Book ch02 line 358 "the number of bytes in the user's input"); today's chain discards it by not binding the chain's value. Deliberately not installed: `io::Result<T>` type alias (deferred — `read_line`'s real return type is `io::Result<usize>` aliasing to `Result<usize, io::Error>` per `std/io/type.Result.md` lines 6-7; lesson treats it as "a `Result` with `usize` on the `Ok` side"); `io::Error` type; `usize` as a typed name; `.trim()` (named only); `.parse::<T>()`; `BufRead::read_line` trait machinery (deferred since cycle 040 — the std page itself defers detailed semantics to it); Stdin's internal mutex/locking (the std page mentions "Locks this handle"); empty-stdin/EOF behavior (`./demo < /dev/null` returns `Ok(0)` with empty buffer, no panic — verified by side probe; cycle's recipe avoids EOF); reading multiple lines / `io::stdin().lines()`; buffered vs unbuffered I/O; Windows `\r\n` vs Unix `\n`; `&self` autoref on `read_line` (carrying over from cycle 040); `String::clear()`; the `loop { ... }` driving repeated reads
depends_on: 001-rustc-compile-and-run, 002-fn-main-entry-point, 005-let-binding, 006-mut-binding, 042-string-new, 044-use-declaration, 045-shared-reference, 048-mutable-reference-parameter, 049-method-chaining, 050-io-stdin-handle, 052-result-enum-and-is-ok, 053-result-expect-and-panic, ordinary computer-use (terminal, editor, shell-piping with `echo "..." | ./demo` to provide stdin)
unlocks: future "`.trim()` on a `String` to strip trailing whitespace" moves, future "`.parse::<T>()` to convert text to a typed value" moves, future "type-changing shadowing `let guess: u32 = guess.trim().parse().expect(...)`" moves, future "the `io::Result<T>` type alias as a typed name" moves, future "the `io::Error` type and its variants (`BrokenPipe`, `Interrupted`, `NotFound`, etc.)" moves, future "`usize` as a typed name and the byte-count return value" moves, future "reading multiple lines, EOF handling (`Ok(0)` at EOF), buffered vs unbuffered I/O" moves, future "`BufRead::read_line` trait machinery" moves, future "`io::stdin().lines()` iterator" moves, future "interactive input with stdin closed / piped vs terminal" moves, future "`String::clear()` between reads" moves, future "the `loop { ... }` driving repeated reads" moves, future "`Stdin::lock()` and `StdinLock`" moves, future "Windows `\r\n` vs Unix `\n` line-ending portability" moves, future "the `panic!` macro called explicitly" moves
```

```text
id: 055-string-trim
move: call `.trim()` on a `String` value to get a *string slice* (`&str`) with leading and trailing whitespace stripped — concretely, after `read_line` reads `"hello\n"` into `buf`, the line `let trimmed: &str = buf.trim();` produces a `&str` containing exactly `"hello"` (no trailing newline); the working probe `use std::io; fn main() { let mut buf = String::new(); io::stdin().read_line(&mut buf).expect("Failed to read line"); let trimmed: &str = buf.trim(); println!("buf has {} bytes; trimmed = [{trimmed}]", buf.len()); }` invoked as `echo "hello" | ./demo` prints `buf has 6 bytes; trimmed = [hello]`
main_concept: composite move installing two tightly-coupled facts. (1) `.trim()` is a method that returns a value with leading and trailing whitespace removed; per the std page `std/primitive.str.md` line 1725 the canonical signature is `pub fn trim(&self) -> &str` with description "Returns a string slice with leading and trailing whitespace removed" (line 1727) where "'Whitespace' is defined according to the terms of the Unicode Derived Core Property `White_Space`, which includes newlines" (lines 1729-1730 — license for the central practical observation that `read_line`'s trailing `\n` disappears). The method is technically defined on `str` (`&self` receiver) but works on a `String` value via the dot-form (lesson 040), per Book ch02 line 925 "The `trim` method on a `String` instance will eliminate any whitespace at the beginning and end" — the lesson does NOT install or surface deref coercion (the underlying mechanism), only observes that the dot-form works. (2) The return type is `&str`, called a *string slice* — the typed name this lesson installs as the lesson's main concept. Per `std/primitive.str.md` lines 12-14: "The `str` type, also called a 'string slice', is the most primitive string type. It is usually seen in its borrowed form, `&str`. It is also the type of string literals, `&'static str`." Per Book ch04-03 line 264: "The type that signifies 'string slice' is written as `&str`." Per Book ch04-03 lines 379-381: a string literal like `"Hello, world!"` has type `&str`. The lesson treats `&str` as a unit typed name and does NOT distinguish `str` from `&str`, NOR install `'static` lifetimes, NOR unpack UTF-8 encoding details. The leading `&` in `&str` is cycle 045's same operator-turned-type-prefix applied to a different underlying type. Lesson 019's `let name: TYPE = value;` annotation slot extends to `&str`, alongside `i32` (019), `String` (042), `&i32` (045), and `Ordering` (051). `String` and `&str` are *distinct types* per Book ch08-02 lines 24-37 (audience-level distinction: "Rust has only one string type in the core language, which is the string slice `str` ... usually seen in its borrowed form, `&str`. ... The `String` type, which is provided by Rust's standard library rather than coded into the core language, is a growable, mutable, owned, UTF-8 encoded string type"); putting a `String` value into a `: &str` slot fires E0308 *mismatched types* with caret label `expected `&str`, found `String``, same E-code lessons 024, 025, 026, 028, 033, 045-048, 052, 054 installed. The rustc `help: consider borrowing here` block proposes `&String::new()` — a calibration probe captured in evidence confirms this *does* compile (via deref coercion, which the lesson defers); the lesson body acknowledges this honestly without unpacking the mechanism. Small collateral install: `.len()` on `String` (used in the working probe's `buf.len()` to show byte count); the lesson does not unpack its return type (`usize`, deferred from cycle 054). Deliberately not installed: `.trim_start()` / `.trim_end()` (siblings, named only); `.trim_matches(pat)` and the `Pattern` trait; `str` as a typed name on its own; `'a` / `'static` lifetimes; deref coercion / `Deref` trait; UTF-8 encoding details and byte-vs-character distinction; `String::from(&str)` / `.to_string()`; `String::push_str(&str)` / `.push(c)` / `.clear()` / `.replace()`; `+` on strings; `&buf[..5]` slicing syntax; `.as_str()` explicit conversion; `.parse::<T>()` (the natural follow-up); `usize` as a typed name; `.is_empty()` on `String` (parallel to `.len() == 0`)
depends_on: 001-rustc-compile-and-run, 002-fn-main-entry-point, 003-read-rustc-diagnostic, 005-let-binding, 006-mut-binding, 019-type-annotation-i32, 040-method-call-syntax, 042-string-new, 044-use-declaration, 045-shared-reference, 048-mutable-reference-parameter, 050-io-stdin-handle, 052-result-enum-and-is-ok, 053-result-expect-and-panic, 054-read-line-from-stdin, ordinary computer-use (terminal, editor, shell-piping with `echo "..." | ./demo` to provide stdin)
unlocks: future "`.parse::<T>()` on a `&str` to convert text to a typed value" moves, future "type-changing shadowing `let guess: u32 = guess.trim().parse().expect(...)`" moves, future "the chain `buf.trim().parse::<u32>()`" moves, future "`String::from(&str)` and `.to_string()` constructors" moves, future "`String::push_str(&str)` and `&str` as method argument" moves, future "`'a` / `'static` lifetimes and lifetime annotations on `&str`" moves, future "deref coercion and the `Deref` trait" moves, future "`&str` slicing `&s[..5]`" moves, future "UTF-8 encoding, byte-vs-character distinction, `chars()` iterator" moves, future "`.trim_start()` / `.trim_end()` / `.trim_matches(pat)` siblings" moves, future "`String::len()`, `String::is_empty()`, byte-vs-character distinction" moves, future "`usize` as a typed name and `.len()`'s return type" moves, future "`.as_str()` explicit `String → &str` conversion" moves, future "the `Pattern` trait and methods taking patterns" moves
```

```text
id: 056-str-parse-to-i32
move: convert a `&str` to an `i32` by calling `.parse()` on it and consuming the resulting `Result` with `.expect("msg")` — concretely `let n: i32 = "42".parse().expect("not a number");` binds `n` to the integer `42`; the target type `i32` is selected by *type inference* from the binding's `: i32` annotation (the right-hand side does not name `i32` anywhere)
main_concept: composite move installing two coupled facts. (1) `&str` has a method `.parse()` that attempts to convert the string slice's content into a value of some target type and returns `Result<TARGET, PARSE_ERROR>`; the canonical signature is `pub fn parse<F>(&self) -> Result<F, <F as FromStr>::Err> where F: FromStr` (per `std/primitive.str.md` line 2176) and the description is "Parses this string slice into another type" (line 2178); the method is "so general, it can cause problems with type inference" (line 2180), so the call alone does not pick a target type. (2) The target type is determined by *type inference* from how the call's result is *used* — typically a `let name: TARGET = ....parse()...;` annotation. Cycle 056 is the first cycle that EXPLICITLY relies on inference for a method's return type: the `: i32` annotation on the binding flows back through `.expect` (which doesn't change the type — `Ok(T) -> T`) into `.parse()` and pins down `F = i32`, which then resolves through the `FromStr for i32` impl (per `std/primitive.i32.md` lines 3781-3818) to a `Result<i32, ParseIntError>` return type. The Book ch03-02 lines 8-14 give the audience-level statement: "the compiler can usually infer what type we want to use ... in cases when many types are possible, such as when we converted a `String` to a numeric type using `parse` ..., we must add a type annotation." `.expect("not a number")` (cycle 053) consumes the Result, panicking on Err with `<msg>: <Err payload>`; the lesson is the second formal use of cycle 053's runtime-panic shape. Working probe `let n: i32 = "42".parse().expect("not a number"); println!("n = {n}");` compiles cleanly and prints `n = 42`. Broken-contrast probe (Shape A — invalid digits → runtime panic): changing `"42"` to `"abc"` still compiles (rustc-exit=0) but at runtime panics with stderr block `thread 'main' (...) panicked at broken.rs:2:32:` then `not a number: ParseIntError { kind: InvalidDigit }` then the `RUST_BACKTRACE=1` trailer; exit 101; stdout empty. The panic message exposes the type name `ParseIntError`, which the lesson treats as text rustc prints (not as a typed name to install). Calibration probe (Shape B — no annotation → compile-time refusal): removing the `: i32` annotation produces `error[E0284]: type annotations needed` with caret label `type must be known at this point`, note `cannot satisfy `<_ as FromStr>::Err == _``, and help block proposing `let n: /* Type */ = ...`; matches Book ch03-02 lines 23-44's reference diagnostic exactly. The lesson body avoids naming E0284 (per orchestrator directive — new E-code not yet installed); the transcript is captured in evidence only. Deliberately not installed: the `FromStr` trait itself (trait machinery deferred since cycle 040); generics and the turbofish form `"42".parse::<i32>()` (equivalent to today's annotation form); generic functions `<T>` on a function signature; type-inference variables `_` and partial annotations like `Result<i32, _>`; `ParseIntError` as a typed name (only acknowledged as text rustc prints); `.parse()` on other targets (`u32`, `f64`, `bool`); negative-number parsing (works for `i32`, not `u32`); whitespace handling (`.parse()` does *not* trim — explicitly per `std/primitive.i32.md` lines 3789-3793; this is *why* the Book chains `.trim().parse()`); type-changing shadowing `let guess: u32 = guess.trim().parse().expect(...)` (the natural next cycle, carrying cycle 007's deferral); match on the parse result `Ok(num) => num, Err(_) => continue`; the `?` operator; E0282/E0284 as named E-codes
depends_on: 001-rustc-compile-and-run, 002-fn-main-entry-point, 005-let-binding, 019-type-annotation-i32, 040-method-call-syntax, 049-method-chaining, 052-result-enum-and-is-ok, 053-result-expect-and-panic, 055-string-trim, ordinary computer-use (terminal, editor, knowing program output goes to stdout while diagnostic text goes to stderr)
unlocks: future "type-changing shadowing `let guess: u32 = guess.trim().parse().expect(...)`" moves, future "the chain `buf.trim().parse::<u32>()` for stdin-to-integer conversion" moves, future "match on `Ok(num) => num, Err(_) => continue` for graceful retry" moves, future "the `?` operator for error propagation" moves, future "turbofish syntax `.parse::<i32>()`" moves, future "the `FromStr` trait and trait bounds in general" moves, future "`u32` and other integer types as typed names" moves, future "`.parse::<f64>()` and float parsing" moves, future "type inference variable `_` in `Result<T, _>`" moves, future "`E0282` / `E0284` type-annotation-needed E-codes" moves, future "negative-number parsing on `i32` and the `+`/`-` sign rule" moves, future "whitespace handling on `.parse()` and the `.trim().parse()` composition" moves, future "`ParseIntError` and other named error types" moves, future "the `cmp(&other)` method composing parsed integers with cycle 051's Ordering" moves, future "generic functions `fn f<T>(x: T) -> T` and where-clauses" moves
evidence:
  - output/docs/rust/std/primitive.str.md
```

```text
id: 057-type-changing-shadowing
move: extend cycle 007's shadowing rule to a *different-type* second binding — concretely `let n = "42"; let n: i32 = n.parse().expect("not a number"); let doubled: i32 = n * 2; println!("n = {n}, doubled = {doubled}");` compiled with `rustc demo.rs` and run as `./demo` prints `n = 42, doubled = 84` and exits 0; the first `n` is a `&str` and the second `n` is an `i32`, with the third line's `n * 2` succeeding as the empirical witness that the new `n` is an `i32` (cycle 009's `*` works on `i32`)
main_concept: cycle 007 installed shadowing as a mechanic — a second `let` with the same name does not reassign the old binding but creates a new binding under the same name; from that statement onward the name refers to the new binding; no `mut` involved. Cycle 007's example used the same kind of value (an integer literal) on both sides. Cycle 057 extends the rule along *one* axis: the new binding's *type* may differ from the old's. Per Book ch03-01 lines 211-213 (load-bearing): "because we're effectively creating a new variable when we use the `let` keyword again, we can change the type of the value but reuse the same name." The Book's canonical example (lines 217-222) is `let spaces = "   "; let spaces = spaces.len();` (`&str` → number type); the lesson uses the structurally identical but cycle-056-grounded form `let n = "42"; let n: i32 = n.parse().expect("not a number");` (`&str` → `i32`). Two coupled observations the lesson surfaces: (a) the right-hand side of the shadow can use the *old* binding to compute the new one — `n.parse()` runs while `n` still refers to the old `&str`, because the shadow only takes effect once the whole `let n: i32 = ...;` statement has run; (b) writing the new type out (`: i32`) is what makes the type change legible AND drives `.parse()`'s target type via cycle 056's inference. The witness for the type change is the third line `let doubled: i32 = n * 2;` — cycle 009's `*` is integer multiplication, not defined on `&str`, so `n * 2` succeeding *proves* the new `n` is an `i32`. A type-ascription side probe (`let _: &str = n;` before the shadow, `let _: i32 = n;` after) confirms both bindings' types directly. Two contrast probes captured in evidence (not in main path — lesson's claim is positive "shadowing CAN change type", not a "with X works without X fails" form): (i) calling `.trim()` on the new `i32` `n` fires `error[E0599]: no method named `trim` found for type `i32`` with rustc's diagnostic explicitly naming both bindings' types ("earlier shadowed binding `n` of type `&'static str`" and "earlier `n` shadowed here with type `i32`") — strongest possible empirical evidence for the type change, but E0599 is a NEW E-code (not installed); (ii) directly assigning `let m: i32 = n;` while `n` is still `&str` fires E0308 *mismatched types* `expected `i32`, found `&str`` — already-installed E-code, grounds the implicit "`&str` and `i32` are distinct types" fact. Per orchestrator directive: lesson body uses positive demonstration only (option three — same pattern as cycles 049/050); contrast transcripts live in evidence appendix. Deliberately not installed: shadowing in nested `{ ... }` scopes (requires installing *scope*); the lifetime of the shadowed binding (moot for `&str` borrows; relevant for owned `String` — deferred); borrow-checker interactions with shadowing (heavy deferral); pattern shadowing (`if let Some(x) = ...` and `match` arm bindings); E0599 as a named E-code (described in *Check Yourself* (b) answer as text, not by code); `u32` and other typed names (the Book's actual guessing-game shadow is `let guess: u32 = guess.trim().parse().expect(...);` — same mechanic, `u32` not yet installed); the full `buf.trim().parse().expect(...)` chain on a `String` from `read_line` (the natural next cycle); design rationale for type-changing shadowing beyond the Book's "spares us from having to come up with different names"
depends_on: 001-rustc-compile-and-run, 002-fn-main-entry-point, 005-let-binding, 007-shadowing, 009-arithmetic-on-integers, 011-println-positional-args, 019-type-annotation-i32, 040-method-call-syntax, 049-method-chaining, 052-result-enum-and-is-ok, 053-result-expect-and-panic, 055-string-trim, 056-str-parse-to-i32, ordinary computer-use (terminal, plain-text editor, `rustc` on `PATH`)
unlocks: future "the full chain `let guess: u32 = guess.trim().parse().expect(...);` for stdin-to-integer conversion in the guessing-game shape" moves, future "shadowing in nested `{ ... }` scopes and the `until either it itself is shadowed or the scope ends` rule" moves, future "pattern shadowing in `if let Some(x) = ...` and `match` arm bindings" moves, future "borrow-checker interactions with shadowing — moves and aliases across a shadow" moves, future "the `?` operator producing different types in early-return positions" moves, future "tuple unpacking via `let (a, b) = ...` as a related multi-binding shape" moves, future "`u32` as a typed name and the Book's `: u32` choice in the guessing-game" moves, future "`error[E0599]: no method named X found for type Y`" moves, future "`match` on `Ok(num) => num, Err(_) => continue` for graceful retry over `.expect`-panic" moves, future "the lifetime of a shadowed owned binding (`String` shadows that drop on rebind)" moves
evidence:
  - output/docs/rust/book/ch03-01-variables-and-mutability.md
  - output/docs/rust/book/ch02-00-guessing-game-tutorial.md
  - experimental/eduratchet2/runs/rust-moves/observations/057-type-changing-shadowing.rs
  - experimental/eduratchet2/runs/rust-moves/evidence/057-type-changing-shadowing.md
status: accepted
```

```text
id: 058-match-result-payload-variants
move: scrutinize a `Result<i32, _>` value with `match` whose arms are *payload-variant patterns* — `Ok(num) => num` binds the `Ok` payload to the local name `num` for that arm's body, and `Err(_) => default` matches any `Err` and discards its payload via the wildcard subpattern; concretely `let good: i32 = match "42".parse() { Ok(num) => num, Err(_) => -1, };` binds `good` to `42`, while changing `"42"` to `"abc"` (with the same arms) binds the corresponding `bad` to `-1` because the `Err(_)` arm fires
main_concept: composite move installing two coupled facts. (1) An enum variant declared with a payload (`Ok(T)`, `Err(E)` per cycle 052) gets a *constructor-shaped pattern* in `match` arms: `Variant(subpattern)`, where the subpattern is itself a pattern — per the Reference's *Tuple struct patterns* grammar `TupleStructPattern → PathInExpression ( TupleStructItems? )` with `TupleStructItems → Pattern ( , Pattern )* ,?` (per `reference/patterns.md` lines 968-980 "Tuple struct patterns match tuple struct and enum values that match all criteria defined by its subpatterns"). The two subpattern shapes used today are: (a) a *binding pattern* — a bare identifier like `num` that matches any value and binds it to that name, available as a value in the arm's body (per `reference/patterns.md` lines 196-218 "Identifier patterns bind the value they match to a variable in the value namespace ... Patterns that consist of only an identifier ... match any value and bind it to that identifier"; the Book's `Coin::Quarter(state)` example at ch06-02 lines 138-141 "the `state` variable will bind to the value of that quarter's state" is the audience-level precedent); and (b) the wildcard `_` from cycle 031, now reused *inside* a variant constructor where it matches any payload but binds nothing (per `reference/patterns.md` lines 462-468 "the wildcard pattern ... matches any value ... used to ignore values when they don't matter"; the Book ch02 line 1209-1211 walkthrough "The underscore, `_`, is a catch-all value; in this example, we're saying we want to match all `Err` values, no matter what information they have inside them" is the audience-level precedent for `_` inside a payload variant). (2) Cycle 051's match machinery generalizes: arms still produce values (the matching arm's expression is the match's value), arm types must agree, exhaustiveness still applies via E0004 — but for a payload-bearing scrutinee the *missing-pattern label* rustc prints is also `Variant(subpattern)`-shaped. Empirically the broken-contrast probe (omitting the `Err` arm) fires `error[E0004]: non-exhaustive patterns: \`Err(_)\` not covered` with the missing pattern named in the new shape (probe transcript verbatim in evidence). Three new shapes of E0004 missing-pattern labels are now installed: cycle 030's literal (`false`), cycle 031's range (`i32::MIN..=0_i32`), cycle 051's unit-variant path (`std::cmp::Ordering::Equal`), and now cycle 058's payload-variant `Variant(subpattern)` (`Err(_)`). Cycle 056's inference path is reused: the `: i32` annotation on each binding constrains both arms to produce `i32` (cycle 030's all-arms-share-type rule), forcing `num: i32` and selecting `.parse()`'s target as `i32` (cycle 056's flow-back). The Book ch02 lines 1171-1174 canonical `match guess.trim().parse() { Ok(num) => num, Err(_) => continue, }` shape is now legible structurally except for the `continue` arm body (deferred — composes once `loop` is involved). Working probe corroborates the truth-table empirically: `"42".parse()` → `Ok(_)` arm fires, `good = 42`; `"abc".parse()` → `Err(_)` arm fires, `bad = -1`; no panic. Calibration probes captured for (a) Shape B "no `: i32` annotation" — the integer-literal default makes the program *still* compile, an honest disclosure that the annotation is not the only inference channel here; and (b) Shape C "reference `num` in the wrong arm" — fires `error[E0425]: cannot find value `num` in this scope`, grounding the *Check Yourself (c)* answer that bindings are arm-local (per `reference/patterns.md` lines 212-214 "the scope of the new binding depends on the context of where the pattern is used (such as a `let` binding or a `match` arm)"). Deliberately not installed: `continue` in arm body (`Err(_) => continue` — the Book's actual guessing-game form, deferred until `loop` joins); match guards (`Ok(n) if n > 0 => ...`); or-patterns (`Ok(0) | Ok(1) => ...`); range patterns inside variants (`Ok(0..=10) => ...`); nested payload patterns (`Ok(Some(x))`, `Ok((a, b))`); `if let` and `while let` shorthand for one-variant match; the `Option<T>` enum (`Some(T)` / `None` — sibling generic enum, same payload-variant pattern shape); defining your own payload-bearing enum; arm ordering and overlap (cycle 031's source-order rule reused; full overlap / unreachable-arm / E0001 story deferred); pattern-binding move/copy semantics (for `i32` Copy makes the question invisible; for owned payloads ownership rules apply); `@`-bindings (`Ok(num @ 0..=10) => ...`); `mut` and `ref` bindings in patterns; the `ParseIntError` typed name (the actual `Err` payload — visible in the broken-contrast diagnostic but NOT installed; the wildcard `_` is what lets the lesson handle `Err` without naming it); the `FromStr` trait and trait machinery in general (deferred since cycle 040)
depends_on: 001-rustc-compile-and-run, 002-fn-main-entry-point, 005-let-binding, 011-println-positional-args, 019-type-annotation-i32, 030-match-on-bool, 031-match-integer-and-wildcard, 040-method-call-syntax, 049-method-chaining, 051-ordering-enum-and-variant-match, 052-result-enum-and-is-ok, 055-string-trim, 056-str-parse-to-i32, ordinary computer-use (terminal, plain-text editor, `rustc` on `PATH`)
unlocks: future "`continue` in match arm body for graceful retry — `Err(_) => continue` inside a `loop`" moves, future "match guards `Ok(n) if n > 0 => ...`" moves, future "or-patterns `Ok(0) | Ok(1) => ...`" moves, future "range patterns inside variants `Ok(0..=10) => ...`" moves, future "nested payload patterns `Ok(Some(x))` and `Ok((a, b))`" moves, future "`if let` / `while let` shorthand for one-variant match" moves, future "the `Option<T>` enum and `Some(T)` / `None` variants" moves, future "defining your own payload-bearing enum `enum Foo { Bar(i32), Baz }`" moves, future "`@`-bindings in patterns `Ok(num @ 0..=10) => ...`" moves, future "`mut` and `ref` bindings in patterns" moves, future "pattern-binding move/copy semantics on owned payloads (`String` etc.)" moves, future "the full chain `let guess: u32 = match guess.trim().parse() { Ok(num) => num, Err(_) => continue, };` from the Book's guessing-game ch02 listing 2-5" moves, future "`ParseIntError` and other named error types as typed names" moves, future "unreachable-arm warnings and E0001 (overlapping match arms)" moves
evidence:
  - output/docs/rust/reference/patterns.md
  - output/docs/rust/std/result/enum.Result.md
```

```text
id: 059-continue-in-match-arm
move: write a `match` arm whose body is `continue` (or another diverging control-flow expression) and observe that the match expression still type-checks against the value-arm's type — concretely `let v: i32 = match n % 2 { 0 => n, _ => continue };` inside a `for n in 1..=5` loop produces `total = 6` (sum of evens), where the `_ => continue` arm transfers control to the loop head without producing a value
main_concept: cycle 030's all-arms-share-type rule is refined: arms whose body is a *diverging* control-flow expression (`continue`, `break`, `return`) are exempt because control leaves the match before the value question matters; the match's type is decided by the *value-producing* arms; the Reference's match-expr.md line 161 grounds this precisely ("If either the scrutinee expression or all of the match arms diverge, then the entire `match` expression also diverges"); today's lesson uses the *partial* form — some arms diverge, others produce values, and the type-check sees only the value-producing arms; the audience-level framing is "control-flow keywords let a match arm escape without producing a value"; loop-expr.md line 457 confirms `continue` is "diverging and has a type of `!`" — the `!` typed name is deferred; the lesson installs the type-rule that makes the Book's guessing-game `Err(_) => continue` shape (ch02 lines 1171-1174) legal, but defers the full composition. Broken contrast (Shape A): replacing `_ => continue` with `_ => "skip"` (a non-diverging non-`i32` body) fires `error[E0308]: \`match\` arms have incompatible types`, naming both arms in the diagnostic and confirming the share-type rule still applies for value-producing arms. Calibration probe (Shape B): with a `1 => continue` arm and a `_ => "skip"` arm, rustc emits the *simpler* `error[E0308]: mismatched types` form pointing only at `"skip"` — strong evidence that the diverging arm has dropped out of the type-check entirely. Calibration probe (Shape C): `10 => break, _ => n` compiles cleanly, corroborating that `break` is also exempt (loop-expr.md line 350). Calibration probe (Shape D): `_ => println!("skip")` (unit type `()`, non-diverging) fires E0308, sharpening the boundary — the exemption is specifically for *diverging* expressions, not for non-`i32` bodies in general. Deliberately not installed: the never type `!` as a typed name; `break;` and `return;` in arm bodies (named only); `break value;` from arm bodies; `panic!()` and other diverging library calls; labeled loops `'outer: loop { ... continue 'outer; }`; nested match with control flow; the full guessing-game form `let guess: u32 = match guess.trim().parse() { Ok(num) => num, Err(_) => continue, };` (forward pointer); `let _ = match ... { ... continue }` without an annotation
depends_on: 001-rustc-compile-and-run, 002-fn-main-entry-point, 005-let-binding, 006-mut-binding, 019-type-annotation-i32, 022-for-range, 023-compound-add-assign, 030-match-on-bool, 031-match-integer-and-wildcard, 035-continue, 037-remainder-operator, 039-inclusive-range, ordinary computer-use (terminal, plain-text editor, `rustc` on `PATH`)
unlocks: future "`break` and `return` in match arm bodies — generalizing today's rule beyond `continue`" moves, future "the never type `!` and divergence as a typed concept" moves, future "the full guessing-game shape `let guess: u32 = match guess.trim().parse() { Ok(num) => num, Err(_) => continue, };` inside a `loop`" moves, future "labeled `continue 'label;` for nested loops and labeled-divergence in match arms" moves, future "`break value;` from match arm bodies" moves, future "`panic!()` as a divergent library call exempt from arm-type rule" moves, future "nested match plus control flow patterns" moves, future "type-inference of a match expression via a divergent arm with no annotation" moves
evidence:
  - output/docs/rust/reference/expressions/match-expr.md
  - output/docs/rust/reference/expressions/loop-expr.md
  - output/docs/rust/book/ch02-00-guessing-game-tutorial.md
  - output/docs/rust/error_codes/E0308.md
  - experimental/eduratchet2/runs/rust-moves/observations/059-continue-in-match-arm.rs
  - experimental/eduratchet2/runs/rust-moves/evidence/059-continue-in-match-arm.md
status: accepted
```

```text
id: 060-input-prompt-loop
move: prompt the user repeatedly until they enter a parseable integer, then print it once and exit — the canonical Book input-prompt loop pattern (`loop { fresh buf; read_line chain; match .trim().parse() { Ok(num) => num, Err(_) => continue, }; use the integer; break; }`); concretely the working probe `use std::io; fn main() { loop { let mut buf = String::new(); io::stdin().read_line(&mut buf).expect("Failed to read line"); let n: i32 = match buf.trim().parse() { Ok(num) => num, Err(_) => continue, }; println!("got: {n}"); break; } }` compiled with `rustc demo.rs` and run as `echo "42" | ./demo` prints `got: 42` (single-pass success); run as `printf 'abc\n42\n' | ./demo` also prints `got: 42` (one invalid then valid); run as `printf 'abc\nxyz\n  42  \n' | ./demo` also prints `got: 42` (two invalids then valid with surrounding whitespace stripped by `.trim()`). All three runs exit 0
main_concept: PURE COMPOSITION CYCLE — installs NO new Rust mechanic. The cycle's value is empirical: fifteen previously-installed cycles (when each surface is named distinctly) compose into one runnable program that recognizably does an interactive input-prompt task in the Book's exact shape. Cycle 060 is the second composition cycle in this run; cycle 054 was the first (seven cycles braided into one `read_line` statement). The line-by-cycle attribution is the lesson's pedagogical work: line 1 `use std::io;` is cycle 044's parent-module form; line 4 `loop { ... }` is cycle 027; line 5 `let mut buf = String::new();` is cycles 042 + 006 (without `mut` line 6's `&mut buf` would not type-check); line 6 `io::stdin().read_line(&mut buf).expect("Failed to read line");` is cycle 054 itself (which is cycles 050 + 048 + 049 + 052 + 053 + the `read_line` method); line 7 `let n: i32 = match buf.trim().parse() { ... };` is cycles 055 (.trim returning &str) + 056 (.parse with annotation-driven inference) + 058 (Ok(num)/Err(_) payload patterns) + 030 (match machine inherited via 058); line 9 `Err(_) => continue` is cycle 035's continue running in cycle 059's divergent-arm rule (the type-rule that lets the Err arm not produce an i32); line 11 `println!("got: {n}");` is cycle 011's {name} placeholder; line 12 `break;` is cycle 027's loop exit. The composition is *complete*: every line is licensed by an installed cycle and no syntax in the main path is unexplained. The Book ch02 listing 2-5 (lines 1147-1190) is the canonical audience-level form of this exact pattern with three deliberate audience-level deferrals: (1) the Book uses `: u32` (cycle 019 installed `i32`; `u32` is a deferred sibling typed name, mechanic identical); (2) the Book uses cycle 057's type-changing shadowing (`let guess: i32 = match guess.trim().parse() { ... }` shadowing the `guess: String` from line 5) — today uses two names `buf` and `n` for trace clarity, with the optional shadowing variant captured in the evidence appendix as Calibration Probe Shape A confirming identical observable behavior; (3) the Book's program continues looping with `break;` only on the `Ordering::Equal` win path (lines 1180-1188), today's `break;` after success is the smallest demonstration of "the composition has done its job and exits to whatever's next." Two consequence probes are captured for the *Check Yourself* answers: Shape B (lesson's *Check Yourself* (a)) hoists `let mut buf = ...;` outside the loop (still compiles; on the second multi-line input loops forever because cycle 054's *append* semantics corrupts the reused buffer — `buf` becomes `"abc\n42\n"`, `.trim()` produces `"abc\n42"` which never parses; the inside-the-loop placement matches Book ch02 line 1163 exactly); Shape C (lesson's *Check Yourself* (b)) removes line 12's `break;` (still compiles silently; on `echo "42"` succeeds once, then `read_line` returns `Ok(0)` at EOF with empty `buf`, `.trim().parse()` on `""` is `Err(ParseIntError { kind: Empty })`, `continue` fires forever). Per the orchestrator directive (positive demonstration only): no broken-contrast probe removes a single cycle from the working program, because removing each one would break the program differently and the working probe's empirical correctness across three distinct inputs is load-bearing on its own. Substrate note: the timeout wrapper is a perl `alarm` substitute because GNU `timeout(1)` from coreutils is not on the macOS substrate's PATH; the SIGKILL-after-2s observation is what proves the loop never exits. Deliberately not installed: `u32` as a typed name (Book's actual annotation, sibling of cycle 019's `i32`); type-changing shadowing the buffer name into the parsed value (cycle 057's pattern, observably equivalent — Calibration Shape A); `String::clear()` for buffer reuse across iterations (the natural alternative to today's "fresh `String::new()` each pass" — pairs with *Check Yourself* (b)); reading from a terminal vs. a pipe (terminal stdin is line-buffered); EOF handling (`read_line` returns `Ok(0)` at EOF, *not* `Err`; today sidesteps EOF via `break;` on first success — pairs with *Check Yourself* (b)); `break value;` from the input-prompt loop (cycle 028's loop-as-expression shape would let `let n: i32 = loop { ... break n; };` replace today's bound-inside form); `cmp(&other)` against a secret number (Book's next layer, composes cycles 040 + 045 + 051); the `rand` external crate (`rand::thread_rng().gen_range(1..=100)` for the Book's secret number — largest remaining deferral on the Book path, requires Cargo.toml `[dependencies]` and `cargo build`); the `println!("Please input your guess.");` prompt-print (Book ch02 line 1108, line 1161 — cycle 011 already installed; omitted today for brevity)
depends_on: 001-rustc-compile-and-run, 002-fn-main-entry-point, 005-let-binding, 006-mut-binding, 011-println-positional-args, 019-type-annotation-i32, 027-loop-and-break, 035-continue, 040-method-call-syntax, 042-string-new, 044-use-declaration, 048-mutable-reference-parameter, 049-method-chaining, 050-io-stdin-handle, 052-result-enum-and-is-ok, 053-result-expect-and-panic, 054-read-line-from-stdin, 055-string-trim, 056-str-parse-to-i32, 058-match-result-payload-variants, 059-continue-in-match-arm, ordinary computer-use (terminal, plain-text editor, `rustc` on `PATH`, shell-piping with `echo "..." | ./demo` and `printf '...' | ./demo` to provide stdin)
```

```text
id: 061-i32-cmp-returns-ordering
move: compare two `i32` values three-way by calling the `cmp` method — concretely `let a: i32 = 3; let b: i32 = 5; match a.cmp(&b) { Ordering::Less => println!("a < b"), Ordering::Greater => println!("a > b"), Ordering::Equal => println!("a == b") }` (with `use std::cmp::Ordering;` at the top) compiled with `rustc demo.rs` and run as `./demo` prints `a < b` and exits 0; re-running with `a = 3, b = 3` prints `a == b`; with `a = 7, b = 5` prints `a > b`; the three runs reach all three arms empirically
main_concept: PURE COMPOSITION CYCLE — installs no new Rust mechanic beyond the empirical fact that `i32` has a method `cmp` whose signature is `fn cmp(&self, other: &Self) -> Ordering` (per `std/cmp/trait.Ord.md` lines 6-19 declaring the trait method, plus `std/primitive.i32.md` lines 4066-4074 specializing the signature to `fn cmp(&self, other: &i32) -> Ordering` for the `impl Ord for i32` block). Three previously-installed cycles snap together: (a) the call `a.cmp(&b)` is cycle 040's *dot-form method call* — receiver `a`, dot, method name `cmp`, parenthesized argument list `(&b)`; (b) the argument `&b` is cycle 045's *prefix-`&` operator* applied to the binding `b`, producing a value of type `&i32` to match the `&i32` parameter the signature declares; (c) the whole call expression has type `Ordering`, fed into cycle 051's three-arm `match` against `Ordering::Less` / `Ordering::Greater` / `Ordering::Equal`. The std `Ord` page's convention sentence (lines 269-273) — "by convention, `self.cmp(&other)` returns the ordering matching the expression `self <operator> other` if true" — verbally ties today's `cmp` shape to cycle 013's two-way comparison operators (`<`, `==`, `>`): the same comparison, three-way as one `Ordering` value vs. three separate `bool`s. The Book ch02-00 lines 778-783 canonical `match guess.cmp(&secret_number) { Ordering::Less => ..., Ordering::Greater => ..., Ordering::Equal => ... }` is now fully readable; the Book's audience-level prose at lines 793-800 ("the `cmp` method compares two values ... it takes a reference to whatever you want to compare with ... it returns a variant of the `Ordering` enum") matches the lesson's framing. Three working probes captured (Less / Equal / Greater) corroborate the truth-table empirically across all three arms. Broken-contrast probe (drop the `&` and write `a.cmp(b)`) fires `error[E0308]: mismatched types` with caret label `expected `&i32`, found `i32``, sub-line `arguments to this method are incorrect`, dual-`-->` `note: method defined here` pointing into `core/src/cmp.rs:999:7` (cycle 036's pattern), and `help: consider borrowing here` source-diffing `&` in front of `b` — same E-code as cycles 045/046, identical `help:` wording, with the noun shifting from `function` (cycle 046) to `method` (today's case). Deliberately not installed: the `Ord` and `PartialOrd` traits as nouns; `PartialOrd::partial_cmp` returning `Option<Ordering>` for types with NaN like `f64`; `String::cmp` and `&str::cmp` (lexicographic on strings); `Ordering`'s methods (`is_eq`, `is_lt`, `reverse`, `then`, `then_with`); method dispatch via traits; generic functions taking `T: Ord`; `cmp` on tuples / structs / `Vec<T>`; `std::cmp::min` / `std::cmp::max` (cycle 043 already installed `min`); the FULL Book guessing-game program end-to-end (composes cycle 060's input loop with today's cycle, plus `u32` typed name plus the `rand` external crate)
depends_on: 001-rustc-compile-and-run, 002-fn-main-entry-point, 005-let-binding, 019-type-annotation-i32, 040-method-call-syntax, 044-use-declaration, 045-shared-reference, 051-ordering-enum-and-variant-match, ordinary computer-use (terminal, plain-text editor, `rustc` on `PATH`)
unlocks: future "the comparison-against-secret pattern from the Book guessing-game `match guess.cmp(&secret_number) { Ordering::Less => ..., Ordering::Greater => ..., Ordering::Equal => ... }`" moves, future "the `Ord` and `PartialOrd` traits as installed nouns" moves, future "`PartialOrd::partial_cmp` returning `Option<Ordering>` for types with NaN like `f64`" moves, future "`String::cmp` and `&str::cmp` for lexicographic ordering" moves, future "`Ordering`'s methods `is_eq`, `is_lt`, `reverse`, `then`, `then_with`" moves, future "method dispatch via traits — when `a.cmp(&b)` resolves through `Ord::cmp`" moves, future "generic functions with `T: Ord` bound" moves, future "`min`, `max` from `std::cmp` already installed in cycle 043 — sibling free functions" moves, future "`cmp` on tuples, structs, `Vec<T>` — element/field-wise lexicographic comparison" moves, future "`u32` as a typed name and the Book's `: u32` choice in the guessing-game" moves, future "the FULL Book guessing-game program end-to-end (Listing 2-5)" moves, future "the `rand` external crate for random-number generation, Cargo.toml `[dependencies]`, and `cargo build`" moves
```

```text
id: 062-u32-unsigned-integer
move: bind a value of type `u32` (unsigned 32-bit integer) by writing `let n: u32 = value;` — concretely the working probe `fn main() { let n: u32 = 42; let m: u32 = n + 1; println!("n = {n}, m = {m}"); }` compiled with `rustc demo.rs` and run as `./demo` prints `n = 42, m = 43` and exits 0; the broken-contrast probe `fn main() { let n: u32 = -1; println!("n = {n}"); }` fails to compile with `error[E0600]: cannot apply unary operator `-` to type `u32`` and the audience-level `note: unsigned values cannot be negated`
main_concept: cycle 019 installed `i32` as the integer-literal default and the `let name: TYPE = value;` annotation form; `u32` is `i32`'s *unsigned* sibling — same 32-bit width (per `std/primitive.u32.md` line 8 "The 32-bit unsigned integer type"), but values range `0..=4_294_967_295` (per Book ch03-02 lines 86-87 "unsigned variants can store numbers from 0 to 2^n − 1, so a `u8` can store numbers from 0 to 2^8 − 1, which equals 0 to 255" applied with n=32). The Book ch03-02 Table 3-1 line 69 places `i32` and `u32` in the same 32-bit row in the Signed and Unsigned columns respectively; lines 56-58 give the spelling rule "signed integer types start with `i` instead of `u`". `u32` arithmetic, comparison, and printing reuse cycles 009 and 011 unchanged for non-negative values; the only visible difference today is that *negative literals do not fit a `u32` slot* — `let n: u32 = -1;` fires `E0600` *cannot apply unary operator -* with the audience-level gloss `unsigned values cannot be negated`. The Book uses `let guess: u32 = "42".parse().expect("Not a number!");` (ch03-02 line 19) and `let guess: u32 = guess.trim().parse().expect("Please type a number!");` (ch02 line 897) as canonical examples; ch02 lines 937-939 give the audience-level prose "the `u32` seen here is an unsigned, 32-bit integer." The integer-literal *default* is still `i32` (cycle 019's installed default), so the annotation is required to override the default — without `: u32`, the literal `42` infers to `i32`. E0600 is mentioned by *number only* in the lesson body (the `note:` line is the load-bearing audience-level statement); the precise mechanism (`u32` doesn't implement `std::ops::Neg`) is uninstalled trait machinery and deferred. Deliberately not installed: the other ten integer types `i8`/`u8`/`i16`/`u16`/`i64`/`u64`/`i128`/`u128`/`isize`/`usize` (Book Table 3-1 lines 67-72); integer overflow (`u32::MAX + 1` panics debug / wraps release); type suffixes on literals (`42u32`, `1_000_u32`); the named associated constants `u32::MIN` and `u32::MAX` (rustc's `help:` block suggested `u32::MAX` in the broken-contrast probe); `as` casts between integer types (`i32 as u32`); the `Neg` trait and the trait-bound mechanism behind E0600; binary representation; `From`/`Into` between integer widths; checked/wrapping/saturating arithmetic methods (`u32::checked_sub`, `u32::wrapping_sub`, `u32::saturating_sub`)
depends_on: 001-rustc-compile-and-run, 002-fn-main-entry-point, 005-let-binding, 009-arithmetic-on-integers, 011-println-positional-args, 019-type-annotation-i32, ordinary computer-use (terminal, plain-text editor, `rustc` on `PATH`, Linux/macOS shell)
unlocks: future "the FULL Book guessing-game program with `: u32` as the input annotation (Listing 2-5)" moves, future "other integer types `i8`/`i16`/`i64`/`i128`/`isize`/`u8`/`u16`/`u64`/`u128`/`usize`" moves, future "integer overflow and `u32::MAX + 1` debug-panic vs release-wrap" moves, future "integer width and value range — `u32` holds 0..=4_294_967_295 by 2^n − 1" moves, future "type suffixes on literals `42u32` / `42i32`" moves, future "`u32::MAX` and `u32::MIN` associated constants" moves, future "`as` casts between integer types `i32 as u32`" moves, future "`From`/`Into` integer-width conversions" moves, future "checked/wrapping/saturating arithmetic for unsigned types — `u32::checked_sub` and the 0 - 1 problem" moves, future "the `Neg` trait and why unary `-` doesn't work on unsigned integer types" moves, future "the `rand` external crate for random-number generation, Cargo.toml `[dependencies]`, and `cargo build`" moves
```

```text
id: 063-capstone-guessing-game-no-rand
move: build, compile, and run the Book ch02 Listing 2-6 guessing-game program (lines 1256-1293, the Book's *final* code) end-to-end with one substitution — replace `let secret_number = rand::thread_rng().gen_range(1..=100);` with the hardcoded `let secret_number: u32 = 7;` — to avoid the `rand` external crate, Cargo.toml `[dependencies]`, and `cargo build`; concretely the working probe `use std::cmp::Ordering; use std::io; fn main() { println!("Guess the number!"); let secret_number: u32 = 7; loop { println!("Please input your guess."); let mut guess = String::new(); io::stdin().read_line(&mut guess).expect("Failed to read line"); let guess: u32 = match guess.trim().parse() { Ok(num) => num, Err(_) => continue, }; println!("You guessed: {guess}"); match guess.cmp(&secret_number) { Ordering::Less => println!("Too small!"), Ordering::Greater => println!("Too big!"), Ordering::Equal => { println!("You win!"); break; } } } }` compiled with `rustc demo.rs` (no Cargo project, no warnings) and run six times via piped stdin: (1) `printf '7\n' | ./demo` prints `Guess the number! / Please input your guess. / You guessed: 7 / You win!` and exits 0 (Ordering::Equal arm fires first try, `break;` exits the outer loop); (2) `printf '3\n7\n' | ./demo` prints `... You guessed: 3 / Too small! / Please input your guess. / You guessed: 7 / You win!` and exits 0 (Ordering::Less then Equal); (3) `printf '12\n7\n' | ./demo` prints `... You guessed: 12 / Too big! / ... You win!` and exits 0 (Ordering::Greater then Equal); (4) `printf 'abc\n7\n' | ./demo` prints `Guess the number! / Please input your guess. / Please input your guess. / You guessed: 7 / You win!` — `abc` triggers the `Err(_) => continue` arm with NO `You guessed:` line and NO error message printed (the wildcard `_` discards the `ParseIntError` payload per cycle 058) — exit 0; (5) `printf '3\n12\n7\n' | ./demo` walks all three Ordering arms in one run (Less / Greater / Equal) and exits 0; (6) `printf -- '-1\n7\n' | ./demo` — load-bearing — prints the same shape as transcript 4 (no `You guessed: -1` line, no error, just the next prompt then the win), confirming empirically that the `: u32` annotation on the shadowed `guess` makes negative input behave as parse-failure rather than as valid-but-too-small (with `: i32` instead, `-1` would parse as `-1i32` and Ordering::Less would print `Too small!`); exit 0
main_concept: CAPSTONE CYCLE — installs NO new Rust mechanic (parallel framing to cycle 060's PURE COMPOSITION CYCLE; cycle 063 is the third composition cycle in this run after cycles 054 and 060). The cycle's value is the empirical claim that 62 previously-installed cycles, composed end-to-end, produce a runnable program that recognizably does what the Book's guessing-game does (ask, validate, compare, retry on garbage, win on equal), modulo the random-number generator. The line-by-line license map is the lesson's pedagogical work and is the central capstone artifact: line 1 `use std::cmp::Ordering;` is cycle 044 + cycle 051; line 2 `use std::io;` is cycle 044; line 4 `fn main() {` is cycle 002; line 5 `println!("Guess the number!");` is cycle 011; line 7 `let secret_number: u32 = 7;` is cycle 019 (`let name: TYPE = value;`) + cycle 062 (`u32`) — *this is the capstone substitution*, replacing the Book's `rand::thread_rng().gen_range(1..=100)` call (ch02 line 1265 in Listing 2-6); line 9 `loop {` is cycle 027; line 10 `println!("Please input your guess.");` is cycle 011; line 12 `let mut guess = String::new();` is cycles 042 + 006 (without `mut` line 14's `&mut guess` would not type-check); lines 14-16 `io::stdin().read_line(&mut guess).expect("Failed to read line");` is exactly cycle 054's one-statement composition (cycle 050 + cycle 054 + cycle 048 + cycle 053 + cycle 049 + cycle 052); line 18 `let guess: u32 = match guess.trim().parse() { Ok(num) => num, Err(_) => continue, };` is the densest line — six cycles braided: cycle 057 (type-changing shadow `String → u32`, parallel to cycle 057's `&str → i32`), cycle 062 (`u32` annotation; Book uses this exact form at ch02 line 1276 in Listing 2-6), cycle 055 (`.trim()` returning `&str`), cycle 056 (`.parse()` with annotation-driven inference; the `: u32` flows back through the match and pins parse target), cycle 058 (`Ok(num)` binding pattern + `Err(_)` wildcard-payload pattern), cycle 059 (`continue` in match arm + divergent-arm rule that lets `Err(_) => continue` not produce a `u32`); line 23 `println!("You guessed: {guess}");` is cycle 011's `{name}` placeholder resolving to the *new* shadowed `u32` `guess`; lines 25-31 `match guess.cmp(&secret_number) { Ordering::Less => println!("Too small!"), Ordering::Greater => println!("Too big!"), Ordering::Equal => { println!("You win!"); break; } }` is cycle 061 (`cmp` returns `Ordering`; cycle 061 was on `i32`, today receiver is `u32` — sibling-type extension grounded by `output/docs/rust/std/primitive.u32.md` lines 4347-4355's `impl Ord for u32` block declaring `fn cmp(&self, other: &u32) -> Ordering`, identical signature to cycle 061's `i32::cmp`) + cycle 045 (`&secret_number` shared-ref) + cycle 051 (`Ordering` enum, three-arm match) + cycle 030 (match machinery) + cycle 027 (`break;` exits the outer loop from inside the `Ordering::Equal` arm body); the block-shaped arm body `{ println!("You win!"); break; }` is cycle 030 (an arm body may be any expression, including a block) + cycle 027 (`break;` is a statement; the block diverges before producing a value, generalizing cycle 059's divergent-arm rule from `continue` to `break;` — cycle 059's *What To Ignore* explicitly listed this generalization as an unlock and the capstone exercises it). The capstone leans on TWO sibling-type/sibling-statement extensions of installed mechanics: (a) `u32::cmp` extending cycle 061's `i32::cmp` (grounded by the `impl Ord for u32` block in `primitive.u32.md` lines 4347-4355 — same signature shape, only receiver type changes; calibration probe `let a: u32 = 3; let b: u32 = 5; match a.cmp(&b) { ... }` compiles and prints `a < b`, empirically confirming the sibling extension); (b) `break;` as a divergent-statement arm body extending cycle 059's `continue;` rule (grounded by cycle 059's *What To Ignore* listing this generalization as a future unlock — the capstone is the empirical witness). Neither extension introduces a new noun (no `Ord` trait, no `!` never-type, no method dispatch); both are described operationally as "same mechanic, sibling type/statement." Six transcripts cover the program's behavior end-to-end: win-first-guess, small-then-win, big-then-win, garbage-then-win, all-three-arms-in-one-run, and (load-bearing) negative-input-rejected-as-parse-failure. The negative-input transcript is the empirical witness for the lesson's "the `: u32` annotation does double duty" claim — it pins `parse()`'s target to `u32` (cycle 056's flow-back) AND it rejects negative input as a parse failure (cycle 062's unsigned-rule, surfacing at parse time because input is a string not a literal). The Book's program makes the same choice for the same reason (ch02 lines 935-947: "a good default choice for a small positive number" + "the comparison will be between two values of the same type"). Listing 2-6 (ch02 lines 1256-1293, the Book's *final* code) is bit-equivalent to the capstone modulo two deliberate deviations: removing the `use rand::Rng;` import line (1260), and replacing the `rand::thread_rng().gen_range(1..=100)` call (line 1265) with `let secret_number: u32 = 7;`; all other lines bit-identical. (Listing 2-5 at ch02 lines 1147-1190 is an earlier version of the same program that includes a debug `println!("The secret number is: {secret_number}");` line at 1158 as a temporary aid; Listing 2-6 already omits that debug print, so the capstone matches Listing 2-6 directly with no third "removed-debug-print" deviation.) Deliberately not installed: the `rand` external crate (Cargo.toml `[dependencies]`, `cargo build`, `rand::Rng` trait, `gen_range` method, `1..=100` inclusive-range argument — largest remaining piece on the Book path, the next move); the `Ord` and `PartialOrd` traits as installed nouns (cycle 061's deferral preserved); `String::clear()` for buffer reuse across iterations (cycle 060's deferral preserved); EOF handling — without `break;` the program would loop forever after consuming piped input (cycle 060's deferral preserved); the never type `!` (cycle 059's deferral preserved); restricting guesses to `1..=100` like the Book does (the `if guess < 1 || guess > 100 { continue; }` check is a future composition of cycles 014 + 013 + 015 + 035); the interactive `./demo`-typing-into-terminal behavior (capstone uses piped stdin for reproducibility); `ParseIntError` and other named error types (the wildcard `_` in `Err(_)` discards the payload, so the type name is not load-bearing — cycle 058's deferral preserved)
depends_on: 001-rustc-compile-and-run, 002-fn-main-entry-point, 005-let-binding, 006-mut-binding, 011-println-positional-args, 019-type-annotation-i32, 027-loop-and-break, 030-match-on-bool, 035-continue, 042-string-new, 044-use-declaration, 045-shared-reference, 048-mutable-reference-parameter, 049-method-chaining, 050-io-stdin-handle, 051-ordering-enum-and-variant-match, 052-result-enum-and-is-ok, 053-result-expect-and-panic, 054-read-line-from-stdin, 055-string-trim, 056-str-parse-to-i32, 057-type-changing-shadowing, 058-match-result-payload-variants, 059-continue-in-match-arm, 060-input-prompt-loop, 061-i32-cmp-returns-ordering, 062-u32-unsigned-integer, ordinary computer-use (terminal, plain-text editor, `rustc` on `PATH`, shell-piping with `printf '...' | ./demo` to provide stdin)
unlocks: future "the `rand` external crate (`rand::thread_rng().gen_range(1..=100)`), Cargo.toml `[dependencies]`, the `rand::Rng` trait, and `cargo build` — the substitution replaced today" moves, future "the FULL Book guessing-game program with `rand` (Listing 2-6 verbatim, no substitution)" moves, future "restricting guesses to `1..=100` via `if guess < 1 || guess > 100 { continue; }`" moves, future "EOF handling — `read_line` returns `Ok(0)` at EOF, distinguishing piped-stdin termination from interactive use" moves, future "`String::clear()` for buffer reuse across iterations as an alternative to fresh `String::new()` each pass" moves, future "`break value;` from a labeled or expression-shaped loop with the win value" moves, future "the `Ord` and `PartialOrd` traits as installed nouns and method dispatch via traits" moves, future "the never type `!` and divergence as a typed concept (covers `continue;`, `break;`, `return;`, `panic!()` uniformly)" moves, future "interactive program structure with TTY input vs piped stdin behavior (line-buffered terminal vs delivered-then-EOF pipe)" moves, future "future capstones — building on the cycle-063 substitution by adding the deferred pieces back (rand, range-check, EOF handling, etc.)" moves
```

```text
id: 064-cargo-build-standalone
move: from inside a Cargo package directory (the directory containing `Cargo.toml` and `src/`, scaffolded by `cargo new`), run `cargo build` to compile `src/main.rs` and write the executable to `target/debug/<name>` *without* executing it (where `<name>` is the package name from the manifest's `[package] name = "..."` line); then run the binary yourself with `./target/debug/<name>` — the same `./executable` shape as cycle 001, applied at a deeper path inside the package directory; concretely the working probe `cargo new --vcs none hello_build && cd hello_build && cargo build` from a `mktemp -d` scratch dir prints `Compiling hello_build v0.1.0 (...) / Finished `dev` profile [unoptimized + debuginfo] target(s) in 0.70s` (two lines, NO `Running` line) and exits 0; `./target/debug/hello_build` then prints `Hello, world!` and exits 0; after editing `src/main.rs` to `println!("Built but not run!");` a second `cargo build` recompiles (`Compiling` line present, time 0.20s) and `./target/debug/hello_build` prints the new output; a third `cargo build` with no source edit between prints `Finished` only (no `Compiling` line), exit 0
main_concept: `cargo build` is the *build half* of cycle 032's `cargo run`. Cycle 032's `cargo run` does compile-and-execute in one command (Compiling/Finished/Running + program output); today's `cargo build` does just the compile half (Compiling/Finished, NO Running line). The Book ch01-03 lines 119-127 grounds the two-line output shape verbatim ("```\n$ cargo build\n   Compiling hello_cargo v0.1.0 (file:///projects/hello_cargo)\n    Finished dev [unoptimized + debuginfo] target(s) in 2.85 secs\n```"); lines 129-131 grounds the binary's path ("This command creates an executable file in *target/debug/hello_cargo* ... rather than in your current directory"); lines 132-137 grounds the manual run step ("You can run the executable with this command: `$ ./target/debug/hello_cargo`"); lines 145-156 grounds the cargo-run-as-convenience-wrapper framing ("we just built a project with `cargo build` and ran it with `./target/debug/hello_cargo`, but we can also use `cargo run` to compile the code and then run the resultant executable all in one command"); lines 158-162 grounds the cache-hit observation ("Cargo figured out that the files hadn't changed, so it didn't rebuild but just ran the binary"). The two-step manual form (today's lesson) is the sibling of cycle 001's `rustc demo.rs && ./demo`; the one-step `cargo run` from cycle 032 is the convenience wrapper. Empirically `cargo run` is roughly `cargo build && ./target/debug/<name>`. Audience-level *why* of `cargo build` vs `cargo run`: when you want to compile but not start running yet (ship the binary, time the compile, run it later with input you'll provide separately). Calibration probe `cargo run` from the same package after step 11's clean cache prints `Finished` (cache hit) + `Running \`target/debug/hello_build\`` + `Built but not run!` (program output) + exit 0 — confirms empirically that the third `Running` line and program output are exactly what `cargo build` omits. Three reading-shapes of build output now installed: (a) fresh build = `Compiling` + `Finished`; (b) rebuilt after source edit = same shape, smaller time; (c) no source change since last build = `Finished` only, no `Compiling` (per Book lines 158-162). Deliberately not installed: build profiles (the `dev` substring is present in the literal output line but its meaning — vs `release`, `cargo build --release`, `[profile.dev]` / `[profile.release]` manifest sections, optimization levels — is deferred); `cargo check` (Book ch01-03 lines 172-187 pairs it with `cargo build`; sibling, deferred); `target/` internals beyond `target/debug/<name>` (the `target/debug/` listing also contains `build`, `deps`, `examples`, `hello_build.d`, `incremental` — all Cargo build-cache implementation detail, deferred); `Cargo.lock` (cycle 032's deferral preserved); the `[dependencies]` table beyond noting it exists in the default manifest (cycle 032's deferral preserved; with dependencies, `cargo build` would also fetch and build them — future move); external crates of any kind; `cargo new --lib`, library packages, multi-file projects, workspaces, modules; `cargo doc` / `cargo test` / `cargo clean` / `cargo fmt` / `cargo clippy`; crates.io and the public ecosystem; Windows path separators
depends_on: 001-rustc-compile-and-run, 002-fn-main-entry-point, 011-println-positional-args, 032-cargo-new-and-run, ordinary computer-use (terminal, plain-text editor, `cargo` on `PATH`, `cd` and `ls`)
unlocks: future "`cargo check` (typecheck only, no codegen) — the Book ch01-03 sibling of `cargo build`" moves, future "`cargo build --release` and the `dev` vs `release` profile distinction, optimization levels, `[profile.dev]` / `[profile.release]` manifest sections" moves, future "the `[dependencies]` table populated with at least one entry, and `cargo build` resolving and building those dependencies" moves, future "the `target/` directory's internal layout (`build/`, `deps/`, `examples/`, `incremental/`, `<name>.d`)" moves, future "`Cargo.lock` semantics — exact-version pinning for reproducible builds" moves, future "the `rand` external crate (`rand::thread_rng().gen_range(1..=100)`), Cargo.toml `[dependencies] rand = "..."`, and `cargo build` fetching and building it — final piece of the Book guessing-game (cycle 063's deferred substitution)" moves, future "`cargo new --lib` and library packages with `src/lib.rs`" moves, future "multi-file binary packages with modules" moves, future "`cargo clean` to reset the build cache and trigger a full rebuild" moves, future "`cargo doc`, `cargo test`, `cargo fmt`, `cargo clippy` as sibling Cargo subcommands" moves, future "Cargo workspaces — multiple packages sharing one build" moves, future "Rust editions and the `edition = "2024"` manifest line" moves
evidence:
  - output/docs/rust/book/ch01-03-hello-cargo.md
```
