```text
id: 065-cargo-toml-dependencies-entry
move: under the `[dependencies]` section header of a Cargo package's `Cargo.toml` (the empty section that cycles 032 and 064 named without filling), add a single line of the shape `<crate-name> = "<version-string>"` — today's exact line is `rand = "0.8.5"`, copied bit-identical from Book ch02-00 line 492 — then run `cargo build` from the package directory; concretely the working probe `cargo new --vcs none hello_dep && cd hello_dep`, edit `Cargo.toml` to add `rand = "0.8.5"` under `[dependencies]` (no other change; `src/main.rs` stays as the default `fn main() { println!("Hello, world!"); }` from cargo new), then `cargo build` from a `mktemp -d` scratch dir prints in order `Updating crates.io index / Locking 14 packages to latest Rust 1.95.0 compatible versions / Adding rand v0.8.6 (available: v0.10.1) / Downloading crates ... / Downloaded <crate> v<ver>` (eight lines: getrandom, rand_chacha, cfg-if, rand_core, ppv-lite86, rand v0.8.6, zerocopy, libc) / `Compiling <crate> v<ver>` (eight lines: libc, zerocopy, cfg-if, getrandom, rand_core, ppv-lite86, rand_chacha, rand v0.8.6) / `Compiling hello_dep v0.1.0 (/private/var/folders/...)` / `Finished \`dev\` profile [unoptimized + debuginfo] target(s) in 11.20s` and exits 0; `ls -la` then shows a NEW `Cargo.lock` file (3351 bytes) next to `Cargo.toml`, and `target/` from cycle 064; a second `cargo build` with no source/manifest change prints `Finished \`dev\` profile [...] in 0.04s` only — bit-identical cache-hit shape to cycle 064 step 11; calibration `cargo run` prints `Finished` (cache hit) + `Running \`target/debug/hello_dep\`` + `Hello, world!` and exits 0, empirically witnessing that `src/main.rs` is unchanged from cargo new's default and the lesson does NOT exercise rand's API
main_concept: the `[dependencies]` section in `Cargo.toml` (named-empty-only at cycle 032 and reaffirmed at cycle 064) takes lines of the form `<crate-name> = "<version-string>"`, where the entry tells Cargo which external crate to fetch and which version to require (Book ch02 lines 495-503 grounds the section's purpose; cargo reference `specifying-dependencies.md` lines 12-20 grounds the general shape); on the next `cargo build`, Cargo (a) `Updating crates.io index` — refreshes its local copy of the crates.io registry index; (b) `Locking N packages` and `Adding <crate> v<ver> (available: v<latest>)` — runs the resolver to pick concrete versions; (c) `Downloading crates ... / Downloaded <crate> v<ver>` — fetches crate sources; (d) `Compiling <crate> v<ver>` for each transitive dependency, then for the listed crate, then finally for *your* package — the package is built LAST, after all its dependencies; (e) the familiar `Finished \`dev\` profile` line from cycle 064 closes the build (Book ch02 lines 549-558 grounds the fetch-then-compile-deps-then-compile-package order, particularly "After downloading the crates, Rust compiles them and then compiles the project with the dependencies available"); on the resolving build Cargo *also* writes a new `Cargo.lock` file next to `Cargo.toml`, recording the exact versions chosen (Book ch02 lines 587-606 grounds the file's existence and one-sentence purpose; contents and reproducible-build mechanics deferred per cycle 032's preexisting deferral); a *transitive* dependency relationship is named operationally as "rand has its own dependencies; cargo handles them automatically" — the probe shows `libc`, `zerocopy`, `cfg-if`, `getrandom`, `rand_core`, `ppv-lite86`, `rand_chacha` getting compiled even though only `rand` was listed (Book ch02 lines 555-557 grounds this: "although we only listed `rand` as a dependency, Cargo also grabbed other crates that `rand` depends on"); a second `cargo build` with no source/manifest change reuses cycle 064's cache-hit shape unchanged — `Finished` only, no `Updating`/`Downloading`/`Compiling` (Book ch02 lines 560-565 grounds this: "If you immediately run `cargo build` again without making any changes, you won't get any output aside from the `Finished` line"). One honest deviation: the Book quotes `rand v0.8.5` in the manifest entry AND in the resolver output, but the live registry resolved `"0.8.5"` to `rand v0.8.6` because the bare version string is shorthand for the caret requirement `^0.8.5` ≡ `>=0.8.5, <0.9.0` (Book ch02 lines 502-503; cargo reference `specifying-dependencies.md` lines 35-49); the lesson states this in one sentence and defers SemVer mechanics. Two reading-shapes of build output now installed on top of cycle 064's three: (a) FIRST resolving build with new dependency = head section (`Updating` + `Locking` + `Adding` + `Downloading`/`Downloaded`) + many `Compiling` lines (one per transitive dep, the listed crate, finally your package) + `Finished` with a long elapsed time; (b) cache-hit rebuild after a resolving build = unchanged from cycle 064: `Finished` only. Cargo.lock is named-and-deferred a second time (cycle 032 first deferred it; cycle 065 minimally extends to "this is the file that just appeared and here is its one-sentence purpose"). Audience-level *why*: "to use someone else's Rust code, list it under `[dependencies]` in `Cargo.toml`. The next `cargo build` does the work of fetching and building that code for you." Today's program does NOT import or call rand; `src/main.rs` is bit-identical to `cargo new`'s default. The dependency is *resolved and built* and would be available to import — but importing is the next cycle's move, explicitly named in `unlocks`. Deliberately not installed: any `use rand::...;` import line; any actual call to rand's API (`rand::thread_rng`, `gen_range`, etc.); SemVer / version operators / version requirement syntax beyond naming `"0.8.5"` as "the version we're using"; `Cargo.lock`'s contents and TOML structure (cycle 032's deferral preserved; cycle 065 names the file's existence only); `cargo update` for upgrading dependencies; `cargo add <crate>` as a sibling (today edits the manifest by hand to make the entry visible); path/git dependencies (`{ path = "..." }`, `{ git = "..." }`); `[dev-dependencies]` and `[build-dependencies]` sections; feature flags (`features = [...]`); workspace-level dependencies (`[workspace.dependencies]`); alternate registries; vendoring; `cargo build --offline` and other resolver-control flags; transitive-dependency theory beyond "rand has its own deps; cargo handles them"; crates.io's account/publishing/ownership model beyond "Cargo's default place to download public Rust crates"; build profiles (cycle 064's deferral preserved); `cargo check` as a sibling (cycle 064's deferral preserved); multi-file projects, modules, library packages, workspaces. Network-access caveat: the FIRST resolving build requires network reach to crates.io; the cache-hit rebuild does not. Listed in `depends_on` as an ordinary computer-use assumption.
depends_on: 001-rustc-compile-and-run, 002-fn-main-entry-point, 011-println-positional-args, 032-cargo-new-and-run, 064-cargo-build-standalone, ordinary computer-use (terminal, plain-text editor that saves UTF-8, `cargo` on `PATH`, `cd`/`ls`/`cat`, internet access — Cargo's first resolving build must reach crates.io to download)
unlocks: future "import the now-resolved external crate with `use rand::Rng;` and call its API like `rand::thread_rng().gen_range(1..=100)` — the immediate next cycle, completing the deferred substitution from cycle 063" moves, future "the FULL Book guessing-game program with `rand` (Listing 2-6 verbatim, no substitution from cycle 063)" moves, future "SemVer / caret requirements / tilde requirements / wildcard requirements / version-requirement syntax — the rule that `\"0.8.5\"` is shorthand for `^0.8.5` ≡ `>=0.8.5, <0.9.0`" moves, future "`Cargo.lock`'s internal TOML structure (`[[package]]` blocks, `name`, `version`, `source`, `checksum`, `dependencies`) and reproducible-build semantics" moves, future "checking `Cargo.lock` into source control for binary projects vs ignoring it for library projects" moves, future "`cargo update` for upgrading dependency versions and the `--package <name>` selector" moves, future "`cargo add <crate>` and `cargo remove <crate>` as sibling Cargo subcommands that edit Cargo.toml automatically" moves, future "path dependencies `{ path = \"...\" }` for local crates" moves, future "git dependencies `{ git = \"...\", branch = \"...\", rev = \"...\", tag = \"...\" }` for crates fetched from a git repo" moves, future "`[dev-dependencies]` for test-only and example-only crates" moves, future "`[build-dependencies]` and build scripts (`build.rs`)" moves, future "feature flags (`features = [\"...\"]`, `default-features = false`, `optional = true`) and conditional compilation" moves, future "`[workspace.dependencies]` and workspace-shared dependency versions" moves, future "alternate registries — private crate registries beyond crates.io" moves, future "vendoring dependencies (`cargo vendor`) and offline/air-gapped builds (`cargo build --offline`, `--frozen`, `--locked`)" moves, future "the `target/` cache's `deps/` and `build/` subdirectories now containing other crates' artifacts" moves, future "transitive-dependency theory — Cargo's resolver, dependency graphs, version unification, the lockfile's role" moves, future "publishing your own crate to crates.io (`cargo publish`, `[package] description / license / repository / readme`, semantic-version discipline)" moves
```

```text
id: 066-rand-gen-range
move: in a Cargo package whose `[dependencies]` lists `rand = "0.8.5"` (cycle 065's installed move), write `use rand::Rng;` at the top of `src/main.rs` and call `rand::thread_rng().gen_range(1..=100)` inside `fn main` to get a random integer in the inclusive range `[1, 100]` — concretely the working probe `cargo new --vcs none guessing_random && cd guessing_random` from a `mktemp -d` scratch dir, edit `Cargo.toml` to add `rand = "0.8.5"` under `[dependencies]` (cycle 065's edit, bit-identical), edit `src/main.rs` to `use rand::Rng;\n\nfn main() {\n    let n: u32 = rand::thread_rng().gen_range(1..=100);\n    println!("Got: {n}");\n}`, then `cargo run` three times produces three different random numbers in [1, 100] (probe captured 71, 22, 62; calibration restore captured 96 — four distinct values total) — first build is the same resolving-build shape from cycle 065 step 8 (`Updating crates.io index / Locking 14 packages / Adding rand v0.8.6 (available: v0.10.1) / Compiling libc v0.2.186 / ... eight transitive deps ... / Compiling rand v0.8.6 / Compiling guessing_random v0.1.0 (...) / Finished \`dev\` profile [unoptimized + debuginfo] target(s) in 7.64s / Running \`target/debug/guessing_random\` / Got: 71`), runs 2-3 are cache-hit shape (`Finished` only + `Running` + program output) bit-identical to cycle 064 step 11 / cycle 065 step 10; the load-bearing broken-contrast probe deletes the `use rand::Rng;` line, runs `cargo build`, and fires `error[E0599]: no method named \`gen_range\` found for struct \`ThreadRng\` in the current scope` with caret on `gen_range` (column 37 of line 2), cross-file `:::` pointer to `rand-0.8.6/src/rng.rs:129:8` showing the method definition `fn gen_range<T, R>(&mut self, range: R) -> T` with the under-line annotation "the method is available for `ThreadRng` here", top-level `= help: items from traits can only be used if the trait is in scope`, and source-diff `help: trait \`Rng\` which provides \`gen_range\` is implemented but not in scope; perhaps you want to import it` proposing `1 + use rand::Rng;`; restoring the line, `cargo build` succeeds in 0.19s; this is the empirical witness that the trait import is what makes the method visible
main_concept: with a crate listed in `[dependencies]` (cycle 065), the `use Path::final;` shape from cycle 044 *extends* to external crates — `use rand::Rng;` is the same syntactic form as `use std::io;`, only the path's first segment swaps from `std` (the standard library) to `rand` (an external crate from `Cargo.toml`); the full call `rand::thread_rng().gen_range(1..=100)` composes (a) `rand::thread_rng()` as a free function call via the cycle 040 dot-form's path-prefixed sibling — Book ch02 lines 690-693 grounds it as "the `rand::thread_rng` function that gives us the particular random number generator we're going to use: one that is local to the current thread of execution and is seeded by the operating system" — the lesson surfaces only "the random-number-generator handle for this thread" and defers thread-local-storage / OS-seeding internals; (b) `.gen_range(1..=100)` as cycle 040's dot-form method call, with cycle 039's inclusive range `1..=100` now used as a *method argument* rather than a `for`-loop range (cycle 039's *What To Ignore* explicitly listed "`gen_range(1..=100)` and other range arguments to library functions" as a future move — today is that move) — Book ch02 lines 693-699 grounds it as "the `gen_range` method takes a range expression as an argument and generates a random number in the range. The kind of range expression we're using here takes the form `start..=end` and is inclusive on the lower and upper bounds, so we need to specify `1..=100` to request a number between 1 and 100"; (c) the chain itself is cycle 049's left-associative method-chain shape — `(rand::thread_rng()).gen_range(1..=100)` parsed implicitly; (d) the binding `let n: u32 = ...;` is cycle 019 + cycle 062, the Book's exact `: u32` choice (matches cycle 063 capstone's substituted `let secret_number: u32 = 7;` — today's call is the substitution back). The audience-level *why* of `use rand::Rng;`: Book ch02 lines 686-688 gives the canonical statement — "First, we add the line `use rand::Rng;`. The `Rng` trait defines methods that random number generators implement, and this trait must be in scope for us to use those methods. Chapter 10 will cover traits in detail" — the Book itself defers trait machinery; the lesson treats `use rand::Rng;` operationally as "a required line whose absence fires E0599" without unpacking what a trait *is* (cycle 040's deferral preserved). The broken-contrast probe (E0599 with `= help: items from traits can only be used if the trait is in scope` and the source-diff `help:` proposing `1 + use rand::Rng;`) is the empirical witness that the trait import is *load-bearing for method visibility* — rustc itself states the lesson's load-bearing claim in the diagnostic and proposes the fix verbatim. The randomness witness is three distinct numbers in `[1, 100]` across three `cargo run` invocations — Book ch02 lines 743-744 grounds the audience-level claim "You should get different random numbers, and they should all be numbers between 1 and 100." Two reading-shapes already installed by cycles 064/065 reused unchanged: first resolving build (`Updating` + `Locking` + `Adding` + many `Compiling` lines + `Finished`), and cache-hit rebuild (`Finished` only). Today adds NO new Cargo-output reading-shape; the only delta from cycle 065 is the `src/main.rs` content. Deliberately not installed: trait machinery (what a trait *is*, `impl Rng for ThreadRng`, method dispatch via traits — cycle 040's deferral preserved); `ThreadRng` internals (thread-local storage, OS-seeding, generator state); other rand methods (`gen`, `gen_bool`, `random`, `fill`, `sample`); exclusive ranges as method arguments (`gen_range(1..100)`); SemVer / version-requirement syntax (cycle 065's deferral preserved); `Cargo.lock` internals (cycles 032/065 deferral preserved); seeded / reproducible RNGs (`SeedableRng`, `ChaCha8Rng`); probability / distribution semantics; `rand::Rng::gen_range(...)` qualified-path form (cycle 041's `Type::method` applied to a trait); `cargo doc --open`; multi-file projects, modules, library packages, workspaces, feature flags, dev/build/path/git dependencies.
depends_on: 001-rustc-compile-and-run, 002-fn-main-entry-point, 005-let-binding, 011-println-positional-args, 019-type-annotation-i32, 039-inclusive-range, 040-method-call-syntax, 044-use-declaration, 049-method-chaining, 062-u32-unsigned-integer, 063-capstone-guessing-game-no-rand, 065-cargo-toml-dependencies-entry, ordinary computer-use (terminal, plain-text editor that saves UTF-8, `cargo` on `PATH`, `cd`/`ls`/`cat`, internet access for the first resolving build per cycle 065)
```

```text
id: 067-capstone-guessing-game-with-rand
move: build, compile, and run the Book ch02 Listing 2-6 guessing-game program (lines 1256-1293, the Book's *complete* code) end-to-end *verbatim* — no substitutions, no deviations — by composing cycle 063's capstone program shape with cycle 066's `use rand::Rng;` import and `rand::thread_rng().gen_range(1..=100)` call (replacing cycle 063's hardcoded `let secret_number: u32 = 7;`), all wrapped in a Cargo package whose `Cargo.toml` lists `rand = "0.8.5"` under `[dependencies]` (cycle 065); concretely the working probe `cargo new --vcs none guessing_game && cd guessing_game` from a `mktemp -d` scratch dir, edit `Cargo.toml` to add `rand = "0.8.5"` (cycle 065's edit, bit-identical), edit `src/main.rs` to be bit-identical to Book ch02 lines 1257-1292 (verified by `diff` returning exit 0 with empty output against the Book's verbatim listing), then `cargo build` produces the same eight-transitive-dep resolving-build shape from cycle 065 step 8 / cycle 066 step 7 (`Updating crates.io index / Locking 14 packages / Adding rand v0.8.6 (available: v0.10.1) / Compiling libc v0.2.186 / Compiling zerocopy v0.8.48 / Compiling cfg-if v1.0.4 / Compiling getrandom v0.2.17 / Compiling rand_core v0.6.4 / Compiling ppv-lite86 v0.2.21 / Compiling rand_chacha v0.3.1 / Compiling rand v0.8.6 / Compiling guessing_game v0.1.0 (...) / Finished \`dev\` profile [unoptimized + debuginfo] target(s) in 7.82s`), exit 0, no warnings. ```text
```
```text
id: 068-let-binding-scope
move: predict that a `let` binding introduced inside an inner `{ ... }` block (here, the body of an `if`) is unusable on a line *after* that block's closing `}`; observe `error[E0425]: cannot find value ... in this scope` with the caret on the use site outside the block, and a `help:` block pointing back at the original `let` labelled "available in a different scope in the same function"; fix by moving the use back inside the block
main_concept: a name introduced by `let` is *in scope* (available) from just after the `let` line down to the closing `}` of the enclosing `{ ... }` block; outside that region the name is not in scope and rustc fires E0425; the phrase "in this scope" in E0425 messages (read since lesson 003) names exactly that region; scope is a compile-time decision, not a runtime one — the contrast fails to compile regardless of whether the inner block would have run
depends_on: 001-rustc-compile-and-run, 002-fn-main-entry-point, 003-read-rustc-diagnostic, 005-let-binding, 014-if-else, ordinary computer-use (terminal, plain-text editor, rustc on PATH, Linux/macOS shell)
unlocks: future "block expressions as values" moves, future "shadowing across nested scopes" moves, future "match-arm binding scope" moves, future "function-parameter scope as a named rule" moves, future "lifetimes / borrow-checker region rules" moves

  - output/docs/rust/reference/names/scopes.md
  - output/docs/rust/book/ch03-01-variables-and-mutability.md

  - output/docs/rust/rust-by-example/variable_bindings/scope.md
```

```text
id: 069-rustc-warnings
move: compile a program containing an unused `let` binding (`fn main() { let x = 5; }`) with `rustc`, observe that rustc prints a `warning:`-headlined diagnostic block (lesson 003 shape) followed by a `warning: 1 warning emitted` trailer instead of `error: aborting due to N previous error(s)`, AND that rustc still exits 0 and writes an executable next to the source; run the executable and confirm it succeeds (exits 0, no output)
main_concept: rustc emits two categories of diagnostic, distinguished by the first word of the headline — `error:` and `warning:`; errors abort the build (no executable, non-zero exit), warnings are advisory and do NOT stop compilation by themselves (executable is produced, exit 0); the four-part diagnostic shape from lesson 003 (headline / `-->` location / source excerpt with caret / help+note) is shared between categories — only the headline word and the trailer sentence change; warnings are emitted by lint name (e.g. `unused_variables`), not by an enumerated `W####` code parallel to the `E####` system
depends_on: 001-rustc-compile-and-run, 002-fn-main-entry-point, 003-read-rustc-diagnostic, 005-let-binding, ordinary computer-use (terminal, plain-text editor, rustc on PATH, Linux/macOS shell)
```

```text
id: 071-macro-invocation-syntax
move: distinguish a macro invocation `name!(...)` from a function call `name(...)` by the syntactic `!` between the name and the `(`; concretely the working probe `fn greet() { println!("hi"); } fn main() { greet(); println!("from a macro"); }` compiled with `rustc demo.rs` and run as `./demo` prints `hi` then `from a macro` and exits 0; the broken-A contrast probe replaces `greet();` with `greet!();` and fires `error: cannot find macro \`greet\` in this scope` (uncoded, with the `note: \`greet\` is in scope, but it is a function, not a macro` line stating the namespace separation in plain prose); the broken-B contrast probe restores `greet()` and replaces `println!("from a macro");` with `println("from a macro");` and fires `error[E0423]: expected function, found macro \`println\`` with `help: use \`!\` to invoke the macro` and a source-diff `+` proposing the original working line
main_concept: Rust has two call shapes — `name(...)` (function call, lesson 008's form) and `name!(...)` (macro invocation, the `!` is the syntactic mark). The two shapes resolve names in separate namespaces in rustc: a function `greet` is not findable as a macro, and a macro `println` is not findable as a function. The broken-A probe witnesses the function-as-macro direction (uncoded "cannot find macro" with the audience-level note "X is in scope, but it is a function, not a macro"); the broken-B probe witnesses the macro-as-function direction (coded `error[E0423]` "expected function, found macro" with a `help: use \`!\` to invoke the macro` block proposing the fix). `println!` (used operationally since lesson 001 and named-macro deferred there, in lesson 003, in lesson 008's *What To Ignore*, and in lesson 011's *What To Ignore*) is the canonical example named today; other macros (`vec!`, `format!`, `assert_eq!`, `panic!`) are listed for orientation but not installed. Today is *shallow operational*: the lesson installs only the syntactic distinction and the namespace separation that diagnostics surface. Deliberately not installed: what macro expansion does at compile time (Book defers to ch20); `macro_rules!` and macro definition; procedural macros (function-like, attribute, derive); token trees, capture variables (`$x:expr`), TT munchers, hygiene; the full set of std macros; macros in patterns / types / items beyond statement position; macro paths (`crate::mod::name!(...)`); the bracketing alternatives `name![...]` and `name!{...}`; the never type `!` (same character, unrelated); trait machinery (the prerequisite list deliberately cites no trait-related lesson).
depends_on: 001-rustc-compile-and-run, 002-fn-main-entry-point, 003-read-rustc-diagnostic, 008-define-and-call-function, 011-println-positional-args, ordinary computer-use (terminal, plain-text editor, `rustc` on `PATH`, Linux/macOS shell)
unlocks: future "what macro expansion actually does at compile time — rustc rewriting `name!(...)` into other Rust code before the rest of compilation" moves, future "`macro_rules!` and declarative macro definition" moves, future "procedural macros — function-like, attribute, derive" moves, future "token trees, capture variables (`$x:expr`), TT munchers" moves, future "macro hygiene" moves, future "`format!` macro for building a `String` from a format string and arguments" moves, future "`vec!` macro for building a `Vec<T>` from a list of values" moves, future "`assert!` and `assert_eq!` macros for runtime assertions" moves, future "`panic!` macro for explicit aborts" moves, future "`dbg!` macro for printing values with file/line context" moves, future "`eprintln!` macro for printing to stderr" moves, future "macros in non-statement positions — patterns, types, items" moves, future "the bracketing alternatives `name![...]` and `name!{...}`" moves, future "macro paths (`crate::mod::name!(...)`) and macros behind `use`" moves, future "the never type `!` and divergence as a typed concept (cycle 059's deferral; same character, unrelated to today's syntactic mark)" moves
evidence:
  - output/docs/rust/book/ch01-02-hello-world.md
  - output/docs/rust/reference/macros.md
  - output/docs/rust/error_codes/E0423.md
  - experimental/eduratchet2/runs/rust-moves/observations/071-macro-invocation-syntax.rs
  - experimental/eduratchet2/runs/rust-moves/evidence/071-macro-invocation-syntax.md
status: accepted
```

```text
id: 072-tuple-type-and-index
move: construct a non-zero-arity tuple as a value with `(v1, v2, ...)`, optionally annotate its type with `(T1, T2, ...)` in lesson 019's `let name: TYPE = ...` slot, and read individual fields by numeric position with `expr.0`, `expr.1`, etc.; concretely the working probe `fn main() { let pair: (i32, i32) = (3, 7); let triple = (10, 20, 30); let mixed: (i32, f64) = (5, 2.5); let first = pair.0; let second = pair.1; println!("pair = ({}, {})", first, second); println!("triple.2 = {}", triple.2); println!("mixed = ({}, {})", mixed.0, mixed.1); }` compiled with `rustc demo.rs` and run as `./demo` prints `pair = (3, 7)`, `triple.2 = 30`, `mixed = (5, 2.5)` and exits 0; the broken-contrast probe replaces `let first = pair.0;` with `let bad = pair.2;` (asking for a third field on a 2-tuple) and fires `error[E0609]: no field \`2\` on type \`(i32, i32)\`` with the source-excerpt caret under the literal `2` annotated `unknown field` and the `= note: available fields are: \`0\`, \`1\`` line enumerating the legal field names of the tuple's type, plus the coded trailer `For more information about this error, try \`rustc --explain E0609\`.`
main_concept: a *tuple type* is a parenthesized comma-separated list of types `(T1, T2, ...)` — a structural compound type with a *fixed arity* equal to the length of the list and *heterogeneous* element types (the element types do not have to match). Each field has a numeric *name* matching its position in the list of types: the first field is `0`, the second is `1`, and so on. Lesson 029's unit type `()` is the 0-arity case of this same family; today extends the family to non-zero arity. A *tuple expression* `(v1, v2, ...)` constructs a value of the corresponding tuple type (the first written operand initializes field `0`, the second field `1`, etc.); the value and the type are written with the same parens-and-commas shape. A *tuple indexing expression* `expr.N` reads field `N` — the syntax is the operand expression, then `.`, then a plain decimal number with no leading zeros, no underscores, and no type suffix. The index must be the name of an actual field of the tuple's type; asking for a field number that does not exist fails *at compile time* (not at runtime) with `error[E0609]: no field \`N\` on type \`(...)\``, where the `= note: available fields are: ...` line enumerates the legal field names — rustc knows the field names from the type itself. Today's lesson is *shallow operational*: it installs construction with `(...)` and access with `.N` on tuples of arity 2 and 3 with element types already installed (`i32`, `f64`). Deliberately not installed: pattern destructuring on the left of `let` (`let (a, b) = pair;` — explicit next move, deferred-queue Q06, Book ch03-02 lines 277-285); 1-ary tuples and the trailing-comma disambiguation rule (`(5)` vs `(5,)` — Reference `type.tuple.restriction` and `expr.tuple.unary-tuple-restriction`); tuple structs (`struct Point(f32, f32)` and the `Point(...)` constructor — same `.N` access shape, different declaration); tuple patterns in `match`; tuple indexing as a place expression (`pair.0 = 9;` on a `mut` binding, nested `pairs.0.1` access); tuples larger than three fields and the upper-arity-limit policy that std uses (depends on trait machinery, not yet installed); whether `pair.0 + pair.1` works (it does, by lesson 009; held off the probe to keep one move per lesson); method calls on tuples (none useful at audience level — no `.len()` because the length is baked into the type).
depends_on: 001-rustc-compile-and-run, 002-fn-main-entry-point, 003-read-rustc-diagnostic, 005-let-binding, 019-type-annotation-i32, 029-unit-type, 011-println-positional-args (cited), 033-f64-floats (cited), ordinary computer-use (terminal, plain-text editor, `rustc` on `PATH`, Linux/macOS shell)
unlocks: future "pattern destructuring on the left of `let` — `let (a, b) = pair;` (deferred-queue Q06, the explicit next move)" moves, future "1-ary tuples `(T,)` and the trailing-comma disambiguation rule" moves, future "tuple structs `struct Point(f32, f32)` and the `Point(...)` tuple-struct constructor" moves, future "tuple patterns in `match` arms — `match pair { (0, _) => ..., ... }`" moves, future "tuple indexing as a place expression — assigning into one field of a `mut` tuple, nested `.0.1` field access on tuples-of-tuples" moves, future "arrays `[T; N]` as the homogeneous fixed-length sibling of tuples" moves, future "the upper-arity policy for std-trait impls on tuples (depends on trait machinery)" moves, future "the `Termination` trait and `main() -> Result<(), E>` (deferred-queue Q07; uses `()` from lesson 029 in `Result<(), E>`)" moves
evidence:
  - output/docs/rust/book/ch03-02-data-types.md
  - output/docs/rust/reference/types/tuple.md
  - output/docs/rust/reference/expressions/tuple-expr.md
  - experimental/eduratchet2/runs/rust-moves/observations/072-tuple-type-and-index.rs
  - experimental/eduratchet2/runs/rust-moves/evidence/072-tuple-type-and-index.md
status: accepted
```

```text
id: 073-let-tuple-destructure
move: write a *tuple pattern* on the LEFT of `let` — a parenthesized comma-separated list of names with the same parens-and-commas shape as a tuple value — to introduce one binding per field in a single statement; concretely the working probe `fn main() { let pair = (3, 7); let (a, b) = pair; println!("a = {}", a); println!("b = {}", b); let (x, y, z) = (10, 20, 30); println!("x y z = {} {} {}", x, y, z); let (m, n) = (5, 2.5); println!("m = {}, n = {}", m, n); }` compiled with `rustc demo.rs` and run as `./demo` prints `a = 3`, `b = 7`, `x y z = 10 20 30`, `m = 5, n = 2.5` and exits 0; the broken-contrast probe replaces the destructure with `let (a, b, c) = (3, 7);` (3-name pattern against a 2-tuple) and fires `error[E0308]: mismatched types` with the caret under the *pattern* `(a, b, c)`, the right-hand annotation `this expression has type \`({integer}, {integer})\``, the inline annotation under the caret `expected a tuple with 2 elements, found one with 3 elements`, and a `= note:` block `expected tuple \`({integer}, {integer})\`` / `found tuple \`(_, _, _)\``, plus the coded trailer `For more information about this error, try \`rustc --explain E0308\`.`
main_concept: the LEFT of `let` is a *pattern*, not just a name. Lesson 005's bare-name form `let name = value;` is the simplest pattern (an *identifier pattern* in Reference terms; today does not name it). Today installs one new pattern shape — the *tuple pattern* — on the left of `let`. A tuple pattern is a parenthesized comma-separated list of names with the same parens-and-commas shape as a tuple value; given a value of the matching tuple type, the `let` introduces one binding per field, in order — the first name takes field `0`, the second takes field `1`, etc. (the field-numbering rule from lesson 072). The shape works on a previously-bound tuple (`let pair = (3, 7); let (a, b) = pair;`) and directly on a tuple expression (`let (a, b) = (3, 7);`); both are the same form. The pattern's name count must equal the tuple's field count; a mismatch fails *at compile time* with `error[E0308]: mismatched types` and the inline annotation `expected a tuple with N elements, found one with M elements`. The Book (`ch03-02-data-types.md` lines 287-291) calls the construct *destructuring* and "a pattern with `let`"; the Reference (`patterns.md` lines 99-105 in the *Destructuring* section, plus lines 1043-1066 in *Tuple patterns*) names the tuple pattern explicitly and gives the canonical example `let pair = (10, "ten"); let (a, b) = pair;`. Today is *shallow operational*: install only the tuple pattern, only on the left of `let`, only with bare names (no wildcards, no rest pattern, no nested patterns, no `mut`). Deliberately not installed: tuple patterns in `match` arms (separate move; the run has used patterns informally on the right of `=>` since lesson 030); the wildcard `_` inside a tuple pattern; the rest pattern `..` inside a tuple pattern; refutable vs. irrefutable patterns as terminology (the Reference's lines 137-154 call today's shape *irrefutable*; today only operationally observes that the shape works); destructuring of structs or enums (`let Point { x, y } = ...;`, `let Some(n) = ...;` — different syntax, separate moves); nested tuple patterns (`let ((a, b), c) = nested;`); type annotation on the destructured pattern (`let (a, b): (i32, i32) = pair;` — grammatical, deferred); `mut` inside a tuple pattern (`let (mut a, b) = pair;`); identifier-pattern terminology for the bare-name case lesson 005 already installed.
depends_on: 001-rustc-compile-and-run, 002-fn-main-entry-point, 003-read-rustc-diagnostic, 005-let-binding, 072-tuple-type-and-index, 011-println-positional-args (cited), 033-f64-floats (cited), ordinary computer-use (terminal, plain-text editor, `rustc` on `PATH`, Linux/macOS shell)
unlocks: future "tuple patterns inside `match` arms — `match pair { (0, _) => ..., ... }`" moves, future "the wildcard pattern `_` (in `let` and `match`) for ignoring a field or arm" moves, future "the rest pattern `..` in tuple/struct/slice patterns" moves, future "destructuring of structs — `let Point { x, y } = point;` and the named-field shorthand" moves, future "destructuring of enums — `let Some(n) = maybe;` and the connection to `if let` / `while let`" moves, future "nested tuple patterns and pattern composition rules" moves, future "type annotations on the destructured pattern — `let (a, b): (i32, i32) = pair;`" moves, future "`mut` inside a tuple pattern — `let (mut a, b) = pair;`" moves, future "refutable vs. irrefutable patterns as a typed concept; the rule that `let` outside `let-else` requires an irrefutable pattern" moves, future "function-parameter patterns — `fn f((a, b): (i32, i32)) { ... }`" moves
evidence:
  - output/docs/rust/book/ch03-02-data-types.md
  - output/docs/rust/reference/patterns.md
  - experimental/eduratchet2/runs/rust-moves/observations/073-let-tuple-destructure.rs
  - experimental/eduratchet2/runs/rust-moves/evidence/073-let-tuple-destructure.md
status: accepted
```

```text
id: 074-char-type
move: bind a value of Rust's primitive `char` type using a single-quoted character literal — `let c = 'z';` infers the type, `let letter: char = 'A';` plugs `char` into the lesson-019 `: TYPE` annotation slot — and observe that the single-vs-double-quote distinction is type-load-bearing: `let c: char = "z";` fires `error[E0308]: mismatched types` with `expected \`char\`, found \`&str\`` and a `help:` line "if you meant to write a `char` literal, use single quotes"; the working probe `fn main() { let c = 'z'; let letter: char = 'A'; let math: char = 'ℤ'; println!("c = {}", c); println!("letter = {}", letter); println!("math = {}", math); }` compiled with `rustc demo.rs` and run as `./demo` prints `c = z`, `letter = A`, `math = ℤ` and exits 0
main_concept: `char` is one of Rust's primitive scalar types — Book Ch3-2 names it alongside the integer types (`i32`, `u32`), the float types (`f64`), and `bool`. A `char` literal is one character between *single* quotation marks: `'z'`, `'A'`, `'ℤ'`. The single-quote form is what tells rustc the value is a `char`, so a bare `let c = 'z';` infers `char` without any annotation; an explicit form is `let name: char = 'X';`, the lesson-019 `let name: TYPE = value;` shape with `char` in the `TYPE` slot. Single quotes and double quotes are *not* interchangeable: `'z'` is a `char`, but `"z"` (double quotes) is a string-literal value of a different type — lesson 055 incidentally named that type `&str`, and today reuses that gloss without re-installing `&str` as a centered concept. Putting a double-quoted literal in a `: char` slot fails *at compile time* with `error[E0308]: mismatched types` and the inline annotation `expected \`char\`, found \`&str\``, plus a rustc `help:` block literally suggesting single quotes (the source-diff `let c: char = "z";` → `let c: char = 'z';`). The Book (`ch03-02-data-types.md` lines 222, 228-234, 236-241) frames `char` as "the language's most primitive alphabetic type", states the single-quote-vs-double-quote rule directly ("we specify `char` literals with single quotation marks, as opposed to string literals, which use double quotation marks"), and frames the value space as "a Unicode scalar value, which means it can represent a lot more than just ASCII" — license for the working probe's non-ASCII example `'ℤ'`. The Reference (`reference/types/char.md` lines 8 and 31) corroborates with the formal definition "represents a single Unicode scalar value (i.e., a code point that is not a surrogate)" and the layout claim "`char` is guaranteed to have the same size and alignment as `u32` on all platforms" — corroborating the Book's "4 bytes in size" framing without the lesson treating size as load-bearing for any compile-or-run claim. Today is *shallow operational*: install only the type name, only the single-quote literal form, only the `: char` annotation slot, only the contrast against double-quoted string literals. Deliberately not installed: byte literals `b'A'` (Book Table 3-2; queue item H, separate move); `&str` as a centered typed name (lesson 055 incidentally; not re-installed); escape sequences inside `char` literals (`'\n'`, `'\u{1F600}'`); `char` methods (`.is_alphabetic()`, `.to_lowercase()`, `.is_ascii()`); integer-`char` casting (`'A' as u32`, `97u8 as char`); the `chars()` iterator on strings; the exact Unicode-scalar-value range as a typed bound (`U+0000`-`U+D7FF` and `U+E000`-`U+10FFFF`); UTF-8 encoding details (the Book defers to chapter 8); the "4 bytes" size as load-bearing.
depends_on: 001-rustc-compile-and-run, 002-fn-main-entry-point, 003-read-rustc-diagnostic, 005-let-binding, 019-type-annotation-i32, 011-println-positional-args (cited), 055-string-trim (cited — `&str` gloss for the contrast diagnostic), ordinary computer-use (terminal, plain-text editor, `rustc` on `PATH`, Linux/macOS shell, UTF-8-capable editor for the non-ASCII literal)
unlocks: future "byte literals `b'A'` — a separate integer-literal form whose value type is `u8`, not `char`" moves (Book Ch1-3 closure queue item H), future "escape sequences inside `char` and string literals — `'\n'`, `'\t'`, `'\\'`, `'\''`, `'\u{1F600}'`" moves, future "`char` methods — `.is_alphabetic()`, `.is_ascii()`, `.to_lowercase()`" moves, future "integer-`char` casting — `'A' as u32` and `97u8 as char` extending lesson 034" moves, future "the `chars()` iterator on strings — `\"abc\".chars()`" moves, future "the exact Unicode-scalar-value range as a typed bound — surrogates and the `U+0000`-`U+D7FF` / `U+E000`-`U+10FFFF` split" moves, future "function parameters typed `char` — Ch3-3 `print_labeled_measurement(value: i32, unit_label: char)` example" moves, future "`&str` as a centered typed name beyond the lesson-055 gloss" moves
evidence:
  - output/docs/rust/book/ch03-02-data-types.md
  - output/docs/rust/reference/types/char.md
  - experimental/eduratchet2/runs/rust-moves/observations/074-char-type.rs
  - experimental/eduratchet2/runs/rust-moves/evidence/074-char-type.md
status: accepted
```

```text
id: 075-const-declaration
move: declare a constant with `const NAME: TYPE = value;` — keyword `const` not `let`, `: TYPE` annotation *required* (no bare `const NAME = value;` form), right side a *constant expression* rustc evaluates at compile time, no `mut`, may sit at global scope outside every `fn` or inside a `fn` body, name in SCREAMING_SNAKE_CASE by convention; concretely the working probe `const THREE_HOURS_IN_SECONDS: u32 = 60 * 60 * 3; fn main() { const MAX_POINTS: u32 = 100; println!("THREE_HOURS_IN_SECONDS = {}", THREE_HOURS_IN_SECONDS); println!("MAX_POINTS = {}", MAX_POINTS); }` compiled with `rustc demo.rs` and run as `./demo` prints `THREE_HOURS_IN_SECONDS = 10800`, `MAX_POINTS = 100` and exits 0; the primary contrast probe replaces `const X: i32 = 5;` with `const X = 5;` (missing annotation) and fires `error: missing type for \`const\` item` (uncoded) with the caret between the name and `=` and the inline `help: provide a type for the constant: \`: i32\``; the auxiliary contrast probes fire `error: const globals cannot be mutable` on `const mut X: u32 = 5;` (with rustc's `help:` block proposing `static mut`) and `error[E0015]: cannot call non-const function \`add_one\` in constants` on `const X: u32 = add_one(5);` where `add_one` is not declared `const fn`
main_concept: `const NAME: TYPE = value;` is a second name-introduction form, sibling to lesson 005's `let name = value;`, with five differences from `let` all stated in Book Ch3-1 *Declaring Constants* (lines 109-157): (1) the keyword changes — `const` instead of `let`; (2) `mut` is rejected — constants are *always* immutable, not just immutable-by-default ("Constants aren't just immutable by default — they're always immutable", line 115-117); (3) the `: TYPE` annotation is *required*, not optional as it is on `let` ("the type of the value *must* be annotated", line 117-118; lesson 019 had it optional); (4) the right side must be a *constant expression* — an expression rustc can evaluate at compile time using only literals, other constants, and basic arithmetic — not "the result of a value that could only be computed at runtime" (lines 125-126); (5) a `const` may be declared "in any scope, including the global scope" (lines 122-123) — outside every `fn` body, where a `let` cannot live. Plus a naming convention from the same Book section: SCREAMING_SNAKE_CASE — "all uppercase with underscores between words" (lines 140-141). The Book's canonical example `const THREE_HOURS_IN_SECONDS: u32 = 60 * 60 * 3;` (line 133) is used verbatim in the working probe, placed at global scope to additionally exercise the lines-122-123 claim; rustc evaluates `60 * 60 * 3` at compile time to `10800` (lines 142-146 corroborate the limited-compile-time-evaluation framing). Today is *shallow operational*: install the form, the convention, and the five-fact difference list. Deliberately not installed: `static` items (`static NAME: T = ...;` — sibling form rustc surfaces in the `const mut` contrast probe's `help:` block; separate mechanics, separate move); `const fn` (declaring a function evaluable at compile time, so it *can* sit on the right of a `const`; the runtime-call contrast probe fires E0015 *because* the called function is not `const fn`); const generics (`fn f<const N: usize>() { ... }` — same keyword, different role); the full set of operations allowed in constant expressions (the Book defers this to the Reference's `const_eval.md` chapter); compile-time function evaluation (CTFE) as a typed concept; constant propagation as a named optimization (Reference: "essentially inlined wherever they are used"); why the missing-type `help:` suggests `: i32` (rustc inferred the literal `5` would be `i32` per lesson 019; the suggestion is a hint about the inferred type, not a default for `const` itself); integer overflow inside a constant expression.
depends_on: 001-rustc-compile-and-run, 002-fn-main-entry-point, 003-read-rustc-diagnostic, 005-let-binding, 006-mut-binding, 019-type-annotation-i32, 062-u32-unsigned-integer, 011-println-positional-args (cited), 068-let-binding-scope (cited — extends "enclosing `{ ... }` block" rule to include the *global scope*), ordinary computer-use (terminal, plain-text editor, `rustc` on `PATH`, Linux/macOS shell)
unlocks: future "`static NAME: T = ...;` items — the sibling form rustc surfaces in the `const mut` contrast probe's `help:` block; fixed memory location, distinct mechanics from `const`'s inlining" moves, future "`const fn` — declaring a function evaluable at compile time so it *can* sit on the right of a `const`" moves, future "const generics — `fn f<const N: usize>() { ... }`" moves, future "the full set of operations allowed in constant expressions per the Reference's `const_eval.md`" moves, future "compile-time function evaluation (CTFE) as a typed concept" moves, future "constant propagation as a named optimization" moves, future "associated constants on traits / impls — `impl T { const N: u32 = 5; }`" moves, future "integer overflow inside constant expressions" moves, future "the `non_upper_case_globals` lint when a `const` violates the SCREAMING_SNAKE_CASE convention" moves, future "`pub const` for crate-visible constants" moves
evidence:
  - output/docs/rust/book/ch03-01-variables-and-mutability.md
  - output/docs/rust/reference/items/constant-items.md
  - experimental/eduratchet2/runs/rust-moves/observations/075-const-declaration.rs
  - experimental/eduratchet2/runs/rust-moves/evidence/075-const-declaration.md
status: accepted
```

```text
id: 076-array-literal-and-type
move: build a Rust array three ways — the *array literal* `[v1, v2, ...]` (square brackets, comma-separated values, all of one type), the *array type* `[T; N]` (square brackets, element type, semicolon, length) plugged into lesson 019's `: TYPE` annotation slot, and the *repeat-init* expression `[v; N]` (square brackets, one element value, semicolon, length, equivalent to writing the value out N times) — and read each array's length with `.len()` (lesson 040's `value.method()` shape); concretely the working probe `fn main() { let nums = [1, 2, 3, 4, 5]; let typed: [i32; 5] = [10, 20, 30, 40, 50]; let zeros = [0; 4]; println!("nums.len() = {}", nums.len()); println!("typed.len() = {}", typed.len()); println!("zeros.len() = {}", zeros.len()); }` compiled with `rustc demo.rs` and run as `./demo` prints `nums.len() = 5`, `typed.len() = 5`, `zeros.len() = 4` and exits 0; the broken-contrast probe replaces the literal with `let a = [1, 2.5];` (mixing an integer literal and a floating-point literal in one array) and fires `error[E0308]: mismatched types` with the source-excerpt caret under the literal `2.5` and the inline annotation `expected integer, found floating-point number` plus the coded trailer `For more information about this error, try \`rustc --explain E0308\`.`; the auxiliary arity-mismatch probe `let a: [i32; 3] = [1, 2];` fires the same E0308 headline with the inline annotation `expected an array with a size of 3, found one with a size of 2` and a `help:` block proposing `consider specifying the actual array length: \`2\``
main_concept: an *array* is Rust's second primitive compound type (Book Ch3-2 *Compound Types* lines 247-251 pairs it with the tuple) — a *fixed-length, homogeneous* sequence of values, sibling to lesson 072's *fixed-length, heterogeneous* tuple. The Book draws the contrast directly at line 322: "Unlike a tuple, every element of an array must have the same type. Unlike arrays in some other languages, arrays in Rust have a fixed length." Today co-installs three syntactic shapes that are not observable in isolation: (1) the *array literal* `[v1, v2, ...]` — Reference `expr.array.array-syntax` "a comma-separated list of expressions of uniform type enclosed in square brackets"; (2) the *array type* `[T; N]` — Reference `type.array.intro` "An array is a fixed-size sequence of `N` elements of type `T`. The array type is written as `[T; N]`" — plugged into lesson 019's `: TYPE` slot, the same slot lessons 033/062/074 extended with `f64`/`u32`/`char` but now filled with a *parameterized* type expression rather than a bare type name; (3) the *repeat-init* form `[v; N]` — Book line 384 "the same as writing `let a = [3, 3, 3, 3, 3];` but in a more concise way," Reference `expr.array.repeat-behavior` "`[a; b]` creates an array containing `b` copies of the value of `a`." The length `N` is part of the *type*: `[i32; 5]` and `[i32; 6]` are different types (Reference `type.array.intro`), so the homogeneous-elements rule and the fixed-length rule are both compile-time facts. The lesson uses `.len()` (std `primitive.slice.md` lines 794-805 — `pub const fn len(&self) -> usize`, with the canonical example `let a = [1, 2, 3]; assert_eq!(a.len(), 3);` literally calling the method on an array literal) to surface the length without installing element access; `.len()` is reachable on arrays via the array-to-slice coercion (std `primitive.array.md` lines 41-42 "Arrays coerce to slices ([T]), so a slice method may be called on an array"). The contrast probe witnesses the homogeneous-elements rule operationally: `[1, 2.5]` fires `error[E0308]: mismatched types` with `expected integer, found floating-point number` — rustc fixes the element type from the first element and rejects the second. Today is *shallow operational*: install only the three syntactic shapes, the `.len()` call, and the homogeneous contrast against tuples. Deliberately not installed: array element access `a[i]` (Book Ch1-3 closure queue item D, the explicit next move); `usize` as a centered typed name (used here only as the return type of `.len()` and the type of the `N` slot — queue item D's sibling, or queue item G's full integer family); out-of-bounds runtime panic (queue item E; needs D first); `for element in array` iteration (queue item F); slices (`[T]` / `&[T]` — `.len()` actually lives there, arrays coerce); `Vec<T>` (Book lines 340-344 contrast); `Debug` formatting `{:?}` (the lesson uses `.len()` because arrays do not implement `Display` for `{}`); stack-vs-heap as a typed concept (Book lines 336-338 forward to chapter 4); multidimensional arrays `[[T; N]; M]`; array patterns in `match`; the `.iter()` method; the full constant-expression rule for `N` (Reference `type.array.constraint` says `N` must be a constant expression of type `usize` — today's lengths are plain integer literals, so the rule does not bite).
depends_on: 001-rustc-compile-and-run, 002-fn-main-entry-point, 003-read-rustc-diagnostic, 005-let-binding, 019-type-annotation-i32, 040-method-call-syntax, 072-tuple-type-and-index, 011-println-positional-args (cited), 029-unit-type (cited — primes the *Compound Types* family with `()` as the 0-arity tuple), 033-f64-floats (cited — the contrast probe's `2.5` is an `f64` literal and the diagnostic names "floating-point number"), ordinary computer-use (terminal, plain-text editor, `rustc` on `PATH`, Linux/macOS shell)
unlocks: future "array element access `a[i]` reading an element by zero-based position with square-bracket indexing (Book Ch1-3 closure queue item D, the explicit next move)" moves, future "`usize` as a centered typed name — the natural index type, return type of `.len()`, second slot of `[T; N]` (queue item D's sibling, or queue item G's full integer family)" moves, future "out-of-bounds runtime panic when `a[i]` has `i >= a.len()` — queue item E" moves, future "`for element in array` iteration over an array's elements — queue item F" moves, future "the integer type family `i8`/`u8`/.../`i128`/`u128`/`isize`/`usize` (Book Ch3-2 Table 3-1) — queue item G" moves, future "slices `[T]` and `&[T]` as a separate type with a runtime length, plus the array-to-slice coercion that makes `.len()` callable on arrays" moves, future "`Vec<T>` as the heap-allocated growable cousin of arrays (Book lines 340-344 contrast)" moves, future "the `Debug` formatting trait and the `{:?}` placeholder for printing arrays and other compound types directly" moves, future "stack-vs-heap allocation as a typed concept (Book lines 336-338 forward this to chapter 4)" moves, future "multidimensional arrays `[[T; N]; M]` (Reference `array-expr.md` line 92 example)" moves, future "array patterns in `match` arms — slice patterns" moves, future "the `.iter()` method and array-to-iterator conversion" moves, future "the full constant-expression rule for the array length `N` (Reference `type.array.constraint`)" moves
```

```text
id: 077-array-indexing-and-usize
move: read an element of an array with `a[i]` (square brackets after the array value, containing one index expression), where the index expression has type `usize`; for literal indexes like `nums[0]` rustc infers `usize` from the index slot, and for a *named* binding in the index slot the binding's type matters — `let i: usize = 2; nums[i]` compiles, while `let i: i32 = 2; nums[i]` fires `error[E0277]: the type \`[{integer}]\` cannot be indexed by \`i32\`` with the inline gloss `slice indices are of type \`usize\` or ranges of \`usize\``; concretely the working probe `fn main() { let nums = [10, 20, 30, 40, 50]; let first = nums[0]; let second = nums[1]; let i: usize = 2; let middle = nums[i]; println!("first = {}", first); println!("second = {}", second); println!("middle = {}", middle); }` compiled with `rustc demo.rs` and run as `./demo` prints `first = 10`, `second = 20`, `middle = 30` and exits 0; the primary contrast probe replaces line 4 with `let i: i32 = 2;` and fires the E0277 diagnostic above; auxiliary contrast probes fire `error: this operation will panic at runtime` with `index out of bounds: the length is 5 but the index is 10` on `nums[10]` (deny-by-default `unconditional_panic` lint — queue item E neighborhood) and `error: negative integers cannot be used to index on a \`[{integer}; 5]\`` on `nums[-1]`
main_concept: two co-installed concepts. (1) *Array element access* `a[i]`: square brackets follow an array-typed value and contain one index expression; the result is a value of the array's element type `T` from `[T; N]`; the brackets-after-value shape rhymes with lesson 040's dot-after-receiver. Indices are *zero-based* — Reference `expr.array.index.zero-index` line 118 "Indices are zero-based for arrays and slices," so `a[0]` is the first element and the last slot of an N-element array is `a[N - 1]`. (2) *`usize` as a centered typed name*: the natural index type, the third typed integer this run installs after `i32` (lesson 019) and `u32` (lesson 062). The Reference at `expr.array.index.array` line 110 (load-bearing) states the rule: "Array and slice-typed values can be indexed by writing a square-bracket-enclosed expression of type `usize` (the index) after them." For literal indexes rustc infers `usize` from the index slot — Book Ch3-2 line 110 "Integer types default to `i32`" does *not* apply here because the index *position* in `a[i]` forces `usize`. For a named binding in the index slot, the binding's type matters: Probe 2 changes `let i: usize = 2;` to `let i: i32 = 2;` and rustc fires `error[E0277]: the type \`[{integer}]\` cannot be indexed by \`i32\`` with the inline gloss `slice indices are of type \`usize\` or ranges of \`usize\``. Two facts about `usize`: (a) it is *unsigned* (the `u`-prefix family from lesson 062, range 0 upward); (b) it is *architecture-dependent* — Book Ch3-2 lines 89-91 "the `isize` and `usize` types depend on the architecture of the computer your program is running on: 64 bits if you're on a 64-bit architecture and 32 bits if you're on a 32-bit architecture." Book Ch3-2 lines 109-112 (load-bearing for the *why* `usize`) "the primary situation in which you'd use `isize` or `usize` is when indexing some sort of collection." Co-installation is forced because indexing alone with literal indexes does not surface usize as load-bearing — type inference handles literals; the type-load-bearing claim only emerges when the index is a *variable*, at which point its type matters. Today is *shallow operational*: install the indexing form, the zero-based rule, and `usize` as the centered typed name. Deliberately not installed: out-of-bounds runtime panic (queue item E; needs today first — Probe 3 captures the constant-out-of-bounds case as `error: this operation will panic at runtime` with the deny-by-default `unconditional_panic` lint, which lands as a hard error on rustc 1.95.0, not a warning); negative-literal indexing (`a[-1]` fires its own diagnostic — auxiliary, not centered); `for element in array` iteration (queue item F); slice indexing with a range (`&a[1..3]` returns a slice — the index slot also accepts *ranges of `usize`* per Probe 2's gloss, but ranges and slices are separate moves); the `Index` and `IndexMut` traits (Probe 2's E0277 trailer exposes `SliceIndex<[T]>`, `Index<i32>` — trait machinery deferred since lesson 040; today reads E0277 by headline + inline gloss only); multidimensional indexing `b[1][2]` (Reference line 133 example); mutable element assignment `a[i] = v;` (needs `let mut`, separate move); `isize` as a centered typed name (signed sibling, Table 3-1 same row; indexing wants `usize`); the full integer family (queue item G); the `usize` literal suffix `0usize`.
depends_on: 001-rustc-compile-and-run, 002-fn-main-entry-point, 003-read-rustc-diagnostic, 005-let-binding, 019-type-annotation-i32, 062-u32-unsigned-integer, 076-array-literal-and-type, 011-println-positional-args (cited), 040-method-call-syntax (cited — pedagogical analogy: brackets-after-value rhymes with dot-after-receiver), ordinary computer-use (terminal, plain-text editor, `rustc` on `PATH`, Linux/macOS shell)
unlocks: future "out-of-bounds runtime panic when `a[i]` has `i >= a.len()` — queue item E, the explicit next move now that today has installed `a[i]`" moves, future "for-loop iteration over an array `for element in a { ... }` — queue item F" moves, future "the integer type family `i8`/`u8`/.../`i128`/`u128`/`isize`/`usize` (Book Ch3-2 Table 3-1) — queue item G, with lessons 019, 062, and 077 supplying three of the twelve variants" moves, future "`isize` as the signed sibling of `usize`" moves, future "slices `&a[1..3]` with a range expression in the index slot — different shape, different result type" moves, future "the `Index` and `IndexMut` traits as the structural reason `a[i]` works — surfaces in Probe 2's E0277 trailer" moves, future "mutable element assignment `a[i] = v;` requiring `let mut` on the array binding" moves, future "multidimensional indexing `b[1][2]` chaining two `[ ]` ops on a `[[T; N]; M]`" moves, future "the `usize` literal suffix `0usize` as an alternative spelling to the `: usize` annotation" moves, future "from-the-end indexing idiom `a[a.len() - 1]` — surfaces in Probe 4's `help:` block" moves
evidence:
  - output/docs/rust/book/ch03-02-data-types.md
  - output/docs/rust/reference/expressions/array-expr.md
```

```text
id: 078-array-out-of-bounds-panic
move: index an array with `a[i]` where `i` is a *runtime-built* `usize` greater than or equal to `a.len()` (the working probe builds the index with `let bad_index: usize = "10".parse().expect("not a number");` so rustc cannot constant-evaluate it), compile the program with `rustc demo.rs` (silent, exits 0), run `./demo`, and observe the panic shape: stdout is empty (the trailing `println!` never runs), stderr carries the three-line `thread 'main' (...) panicked at demo.rs:5:19:` / `index out of bounds: the len is 5 but the index is 10` / `note: run with \`RUST_BACKTRACE=1\` environment variable to display a backtrace` block, and `echo $?` prints `101`; the in-bounds contrast probe is the same source with `"10"` replaced by `"2"`, which compiles and runs identically except `nums[2]` is `30`, so `./demo` prints `element = 30` on stdout and exits `0`; the auxiliary compile-time contrast (re-running lesson 077's auxiliary on this host) keeps the constant index `let x = nums[10];` and fires `error: this operation will panic at runtime` with the `unconditional_panic` lint trailer, witnessing the runtime-vs-compile-time split the lesson centers
main_concept: two co-installed concepts about the same phenomenon, neither observable without the other. (1) *`a[i]` is bounds-checked at runtime*: each evaluation of an indexing expression `a[i]` runs an inserted check that compares `i` to `a.len()` and panics if `i >= a.len()`. The Book Ch3-2 *Invalid Array Element Access* (lines 459-466, load-bearing) states the rule directly: "When you attempt to access an element using indexing, Rust will check that the index you've specified is less than the array length. If the index is greater than or equal to the length, Rust will panic. This check has to happen at runtime ... because the compiler can't possibly know what value a user will enter when they run the code later." Reference `expr.array.index.const` line 122 (load-bearing) draws the precise compile-time-vs-runtime split: "Array access is a constant expression, so bounds can be checked at compile-time with a constant index value. Otherwise a check will be performed at run-time that will put the thread in a panicked state if it fails." Lesson 077's auxiliary Probe 3 saw the *constant-index* case (`nums[10]` with literal `10`) fire `error: this operation will panic at runtime` at compile time via the deny-by-default `unconditional_panic` lint; today's working probe sees the *runtime-index* case (`nums[bad_index]` where `bad_index` came through `.parse()`) succeed compile (`rustc demo.rs` exits 0) and panic only at run time. The choice of `.parse()` rather than the Book's full `io::stdin().read_line(...).expect(...).trim().parse().expect(...)` chain is operational economy: lesson 056 already installed `.parse().expect(...)` as a `usize`-producing chain, the Book example needs lessons 050+054 worth of additional machinery, and the bounds rule does not depend on *where* the runtime index came from. (2) *The panic message has a specific shape — `index out of bounds: the len is N but the index is M`*: the Book at lines 453-457 (load-bearing) gives the canonical transcript "thread 'main' panicked at src/main.rs:19:19: / index out of bounds: the len is 5 but the index is 10 / note: run with `RUST_BACKTRACE=1` environment variable to display a backtrace" with the same five-element array `[1, 2, 3, 4, 5]` and the same out-of-bounds index `10` the lesson uses; the lesson's working probe reproduces this transcript verbatim modulo the per-run thread-id parens and the file location. The wrapping `thread 'main' ... panicked at file:line:col:` and `note: ... RUST_BACKTRACE=1 ...` lines are exactly lesson 053's panic trailer (already installed); the new piece today is the *first line* of the trailer's body — `index out of bounds: the len is N but the index is M`. Why N and M are named: `N` is `a.len()`, `M` is the index value that failed. Co-installation is forced because the rule is operationally invisible without the message that names it (a learner cannot recognize "the bounds check fired" from program-exit-101 alone), and the message shape is meaningless without the rule that produced it. Today is *shallow operational*: install the runtime bounds-check rule, the specific message shape, and the runtime-vs-compile-time split. Memory-safety framing is named ("Rust does not let invalid indexing read past the array's memory; it terminates instead") with a corpus pointer to Book lines 468-471 but not made into a centered concept. Deliberately not installed: `for element in array` iteration (queue item F); `a.get(i)` returning `Option<&T>` as the non-panicking sibling (needs `Option` and `&T` first); backtrace mechanics (`RUST_BACKTRACE=1` / `=full` — lesson 053 already deferred); `std::panic::catch_unwind` for recovering from a panic on the same thread; `get_unchecked(i)` as the `unsafe` form that skips the bounds check; multi-thread panic semantics (today has only the `main` thread); non-array indexing `Vec<T>[i]` / `HashMap<K, V>[k]` / string indexing (different types, different rules); custom panic messages (the indexing message is fixed by rustc); slice indexing with a range `&a[1..3]`; negative literal indexing `a[-1]` (compile-time rejection with a different diagnostic, lesson 077 Probe 4); the precise wording difference `the len is` (runtime) vs `the length is` (compile-time auxiliary); the per-run thread-id in parens lesson 053 already deferred.
depends_on: 001-rustc-compile-and-run, 002-fn-main-entry-point, 005-let-binding, 053-result-expect-and-panic, 056-str-parse-to-i32, 076-array-literal-and-type, 077-array-indexing-and-usize, 011-println-positional-args (cited), 019-type-annotation-i32 (cited), ordinary computer-use (terminal, plain-text editor, `rustc` on `PATH`, Linux/macOS shell, knowing that program output goes to stdout while diagnostics go to stderr and that `echo $?` prints the shell exit status — all reused from lesson 053)
unlocks: future "`for element in array` iteration `for x in a { ... }` over an array's elements without manual indexing — queue item F, the natural sibling-level next step now that out-of-bounds is named as the failure-mode iteration deliberately avoids" moves, future "`a.get(i)` returning `Option<&T>` as the non-panicking sibling of `a[i]` — needs `Option<T>` as a typed name and `&T` as the reference form" moves, future "`Option<T>` enum (`Some(T)` / `None`) as the standard-library type for *value-or-absent* — surfaces here only as the `a.get(i)` deferral" moves, future "the `Index` and `IndexMut` traits surfacing in lesson 077's E0277 trailer — trait machinery, deferred since lesson 040" moves, future "`std::panic::catch_unwind` for recovering from a panic on the same thread — composes with today's centered runtime-panic shape" moves, future "the four-method-family integer-overflow set `wrapping_*` / `checked_*` / `overflowing_*` / `saturating_*` (queue item I) — same `unconditional_panic` lint family fires for compile-time-evaluable overflowing arithmetic" moves, future "the `unconditional_panic` lint as a centered name — surfaces here only via Probe 3's trailer and Check Yourself (c)" moves, future "non-array indexing — `Vec<T>[i]`, `HashMap<K, V>[k]`, string indexing — each with its own bounds-or-key rule" moves, future "slice indexing with a range expression `&a[1..3]` and the analogous bounds rule for range endpoints" moves
evidence:
  - output/docs/rust/book/ch03-02-data-types.md
  - output/docs/rust/reference/expressions/array-expr.md
  - experimental/eduratchet2/runs/rust-moves/observations/078-array-out-of-bounds-panic.rs
  - experimental/eduratchet2/runs/rust-moves/evidence/078-array-out-of-bounds-panic.md
status: accepted
```

```text
id: 079-for-over-array
move: iterate every element of an array with `for element in a { ... }` — the same `for X in COLLECTION { ... }` shape lesson 022 installed for ranges, but with an array (lesson 076) in the COLLECTION slot; on each pass the loop variable is bound to that pass's element value, in array order, with no manual index, no `mut`, and no `[i]` expression; concretely the working probe `fn main() { let a = [10, 20, 30, 40, 50]; for element in a { println!("the value is: {}", element); } }` compiled with `rustc demo.rs` and run as `./demo` prints `the value is: 10` through `the value is: 50` (five lines, in array order) and exits 0 — matching Book Ch3-5 Listing 3-5 verbatim modulo the positional `{}` vs brace-named `{element}` placeholder choice; the positive-contrast probe is the lesson-017 manual-indexing shape `fn main() { let a = [10, 20, 30, 40, 50]; let mut index = 0; while index < a.len() { println!("the value is: {}", a[index]); index = index + 1; } }` (Book Listing 3-4 with `a.len()` instead of the literal `5` and plain `=` instead of `+=`) which compiles and runs identically — same output, different shape — and operationally witnesses the "same task, half the moving parts" framing
main_concept: today installs one centered claim — *`for X in COLLECTION { ... }` accepts an array in the COLLECTION slot* — under two operationally-coupled framings. (1) *Shape extension*: lesson 022 installed `for var in 0..N { ... }` for the range case; today shows that the same `for ... in ...` syntax accepts an array value, with the loop variable bound to each *element value* (not to an index) on each pass, in array order, until the array is exhausted. The Reference at `expr.loop.for.intro` (line 208) gives the formal mechanism — "A `for` expression is a syntactic construct for looping over elements provided by an implementation of `std::iter::IntoIterator`" — but the lesson stays operational and does not install `IntoIterator`, `Iterator`, or `.next()`; the only collection types the run has installed are ranges (lesson 022) and arrays (lesson 076), and today extends the COLLECTION slot to cover the second. (2) *Iteration replaces manual indexing*: Book Ch3-5 lines 446-528 explicitly contrast Listing 3-4's `while index < 5 { ... a[index] ... index += 1; }` with Listing 3-5's `for element in a { ... element ... }`. The Book's argument has two pieces — error-prone (lines 490-493: "if you changed the definition of the `a` array to have four elements but forgot to update the condition to `while index < 4`, the code would panic") and slow (per-iteration bounds check) — and one conclusion (lines 525-528, load-bearing): "The safety and conciseness of `for` loops make them the most commonly used loop construct in Rust." The lesson installs the safety half (the `for` shape removes the out-of-bounds failure mode lesson 078 named: there is no `[i]` expression, so the runtime bounds check has nothing to fire on) and quotes the conclusion; the speed half is a codegen detail not load-bearing for the shape itself. The contrast probe captures the same five-line output from both shapes, so the lesson's "same task, fewer moving parts" framing is operationally witnessed: the working probe has 5 lines of `fn main` body content and uses neither `let mut`, nor `<`, nor `a[i]`, nor `+ 1`, while the contrast probe needs all four. Today is *shallow operational*: install only `for X in ARRAY { ... }`. Deliberately not installed: `.iter()` as a method or as an iterator-producing concept (today's bare form works without it on arrays for `Copy` element types in the run's default edition); `for (i, v) in a.iter().enumerate()` (composes `.iter()` and `.enumerate()`); the `Iterator` trait as machinery; iterating by reference vs by value (`for x in &a` vs `for x in a` — invisible for `Copy` element types like `i32`, separate move for non-`Copy` types); `for x in a.into_iter()` as the explicit by-value form; slice iteration (`for x in &a[1..3]` or `Vec<T>` iteration); 2024-edition array iteration semantic changes; `while let Some(x) = iter.next()`; iterator adapters (`.map`, `.filter`, `.collect`, `.fold`); `break` and `continue` inside `for element in a` (lessons 027, 035 already compose with today, no new fact).
depends_on: 001-rustc-compile-and-run, 002-fn-main-entry-point, 005-let-binding, 022-for-range, 076-array-literal-and-type, 011-println-positional-args (cited), 017-while-loop (cited — outer shape of the contrast probe), 077-array-indexing-and-usize (cited — referenced only in the contrast framing, used by the contrast probe's `a[index]` but not by the working probe), 078-array-out-of-bounds-panic (cited — the failure mode iteration avoids), ordinary computer-use (terminal, plain-text editor, `rustc` on `PATH`, Linux/macOS shell)
unlocks: future "`.iter()` method on arrays — exposing an array as an iterator without consuming it; sibling of today's bare-array form, needed before `.enumerate()` / `.zip()` / iterator adapters" moves, future "`for (i, v) in a.iter().enumerate()` — the indexed-iteration form that yields both index and element each pass" moves, future "the `Iterator` trait as the centered standard-library machinery making `for ... in ...` work on more than ranges and arrays — Reference `expr.loop.for.intro` names it" moves, future "the `IntoIterator` trait — the precise hook the `for` desugar invokes (Reference `expr.loop.for.desugar` lines 240-271)" moves, future "iterating by reference `for x in &a` vs by value `for x in a` — distinct behaviours for non-`Copy` element types" moves, future "`for x in a.into_iter()` — explicit by-value iteration form" moves, future "slice iteration `for x in &a[1..3]` and `Vec<T>` iteration — different collection types, same `for` shape" moves, future "`while let Some(x) = iter.next()` — manual-pull iteration form, needs `Option` and `.next()`" moves, future "iterator adapters `.map` / `.filter` / `.collect` / `.fold` — composable transformations on iterators" moves, future "the 2024-edition array iteration semantic changes — edition-detail unblocking by-value iteration on arrays in older code" moves, future "Book Ch3-5 *Returning Values from Loops* style `(1..N).rev()` countdown — Book queue item Q, small composition" moves
evidence:
  - output/docs/rust/book/ch03-05-control-flow.md
  - output/docs/rust/reference/expressions/loop-expr.md
  - experimental/eduratchet2/runs/rust-moves/observations/079-for-over-array.rs
```

```text
id: 080-integer-type-family
move: name the twelve typed integer names from Book Ch3-2 Table 3-1 — `i8`, `u8`, `i16`, `u16`, `i32`, `u32`, `i64`, `u64`, `i128`, `u128`, `isize`, `usize` — organized by two axes (signed `i`-prefix vs unsigned `u`-prefix; bit width `8`, `16`, `32`, `64`, `128`, or architecture-dependent `size`), and plug four of them into the lesson-019 `: TYPE` slot in one working probe; concretely the working probe `fn main() { let small: u8 = 250; let signed_small: i8 = -100; let big: i64 = 9_000_000_000; let architecture: usize = 42; println!("u8 small = {}", small); println!("i8 signed_small = {}", signed_small); println!("i64 big = {}", big); println!("usize architecture = {}", architecture); }` compiled with `rustc demo.rs` and run as `./demo` prints `u8 small = 250`, `i8 signed_small = -100`, `i64 big = 9000000000`, `usize architecture = 42` and exits 0; the contrast probe `fn main() { let too_big: u8 = 256; println!("{}", too_big); }` fires `error: literal out of range for \`u8\`` with the `note:` gloss `the literal \`256\` does not fit into the type \`u8\` whose range is \`0..=255\`` plus a deny-by-default `overflowing_literals` lint trailer
main_concept: one centered claim with two coupled framings. (1) *Twelve typed integer names organized by sign and width*: the Book Ch3-2 *Integer Types* section (lines 56-91, load-bearing) lists every variant in Table 3-1 (lines 63-72), with the *Length* column giving six widths (`8`, `16`, `32`, `64`, `128`, *Architecture-dependent*) and the *Signed* / *Unsigned* columns giving the two sign axes, for twelve cells total. The Book's own framing at lines 56-61 ("signed integer types start with `i` instead of `u`") and lines 74-81 ("`Signed` and `unsigned` refer to whether it's possible for the number to be negative") makes the convention explicit. Each name plugs into the lesson-019 `: TYPE` slot the way `i32`, `u32`, `usize`, `f64`, `char` already do — no new syntax. (2) *Bit width fixes the range*: Book lines 83-87 (load-bearing) gives the formulas verbatim — "Each signed variant can store numbers from −(2n − 1) to 2n − 1 − 1 inclusive, where *n* is the number of bits that variant uses ... a `u8` can store numbers from 0 to 28 − 1, which equals 0 to 255." The lesson restates these as `iN` holds `-(2^(N-1))..=(2^(N-1) - 1)` and `uN` holds `0..=(2^N - 1)`, with the same two concrete examples (`u8` is `0..=255`, `i8` is `-128..=127`). The contrast probe's `error: literal out of range for \`u8\`` with the `note:` gloss containing `0..=255` is rustc itself enforcing this rule for the smallest unsigned variant; the probe is the operational witness that range-from-bit-width is rustc-enforced, not just documentation. The architecture-dependent `isize`/`usize` row (Book lines 89-91) is named as part of the family but its own install belongs to lesson 077 and is reused as a gloss only. The integer-literal default `i32` (Book line 110) carries over from lesson 019 unchanged — it is what makes the annotation *required* to bind a literal as any non-`i32` integer type. Co-installation of the family-naming and the range rule is forced because one without the other is inert: naming `i8`, `u8`, `i16` etc. without a usable range is a vocabulary list, and stating range formulas without the family is a one-off. The probe binds four of the twelve types to keep cognitive load bounded — `u8` and `i8` exhibit the smallest concrete ranges (the ones the diagnostic spells out fully), `i64` exhibits a width past the default and motivates the auxiliary `i32`-overflow probe in evidence, and `usize` keeps lesson 077's typed-name install present in the family enumeration. Today is *shallow operational*: install the twelve-name set, the two-axis convention, the range formula, and the compile-time literal-out-of-range diagnostic. Deliberately not installed: integer literal forms (queue item H — `_` separator, `0x`/`0o`/`0b`, type suffix `57u8`, byte literal `b'A'`); integer overflow at *arithmetic* runtime (queue item I — wrap/panic semantics and the four method families `wrapping_*` / `checked_*` / `overflowing_*` / `saturating_*`); `as` casts between integer types (`i32 as u8`, with sign-extension rules); two's complement representation (Book line 81, named only); `i128`/`u128` arithmetic (variants named, no probe); operations on `isize` (sibling of `usize`, named only); the `Copy` trait (deferred since lesson 040); `MIN`/`MAX` associated constants on each primitive (`u8::MAX`, etc., named in *What To Ignore*); the `overflowing_literals` lint as a centered name (surfaces in the contrast trailer; lint configuration deferred); when-to-pick advice between integer types (Book lines 109-112, deferred to operational practice).
depends_on: 001-rustc-compile-and-run, 002-fn-main-entry-point, 003-read-rustc-diagnostic, 005-let-binding, 019-type-annotation-i32, 062-u32-unsigned-integer, 077-array-indexing-and-usize, 011-println-positional-args (cited), ordinary computer-use (terminal, plain-text editor, `rustc` on `PATH`, Linux/macOS shell)
unlocks: future "integer literal forms — separator `_`, hex `0x`, octal `0o`, binary `0b`, type suffix `57u8`, byte literal `b'A'` (Book Ch3-2 Table 3-2) — queue item H, the natural sibling-level next step now that all twelve `: TYPE` slots are named" moves, future "integer overflow — debug panic vs release wrap, the four method families `wrapping_*` / `checked_*` / `overflowing_*` / `saturating_*` (Book Ch3-2 *Integer Overflow*) — queue item I, with `u8` from today as the small-range example" moves, future "integer-to-integer `as` casts `i32 as u8` / `u8 as i64` etc., extending lesson 034's `i32 as f64` pattern" moves, future "`isize` as the signed sibling of `usize`, named in today's family list but not centered" moves, future "the `MIN` and `MAX` associated constants on each primitive integer type — `u8::MAX == 255`, `i8::MIN == -128`, etc." moves, future "two's complement representation as the storage scheme for signed variants — Book line 81, deferred today" moves, future "the `Copy` trait as the structural reason integers can be copied freely — trait machinery deferred since lesson 040" moves, future "the `overflowing_literals` lint as a centered name — surfaces in today's contrast probe's trailer" moves
evidence:
  - output/docs/rust/book/ch03-02-data-types.md
  - output/docs/rust/std/primitive.u8.md
  - output/docs/rust/std/primitive.i8.md
```
