```text
id: 081-integer-literal-forms
move: write integer literals in five forms beyond plain decimal — hex `0xff` (base 16), octal `0o77` (base 8), binary `0b1111_0000` (base 2), type-suffix `57u8` (a literal touching one of the twelve integer type names from lesson 080 with no space), byte literal `b'A'` (letter `b` then one ASCII character in single quotes, producing a `u8` whose value is the ASCII code of the character) — plus two notational conventions that work with any of the four base forms: the visual separator `_` (which has no effect on value, so `1_000` and `1000` are identical to rustc), and the integer-literal default `i32` from lesson 019 still applying to any unsuffixed literal in any base; concretely the working probe `fn main() { let dec = 1_000; let hex = 0xff; let oct = 0o77; let bin = 0b1111_0000; let suffix = 57u8; let byte = b'A'; println!("dec = {}", dec); println!("hex = {}", hex); println!("oct = {}", oct); println!("bin = {}", bin); println!("suffix = {}", suffix); println!("byte = {}", byte); }` compiled with `rustc demo.rs` and run as `./demo` prints `dec = 1000`, `hex = 255`, `oct = 63`, `bin = 240`, `suffix = 57`, `byte = 65` and exits 0; the contrast probe `fn main() { let byte = b'ℤ'; println!("byte = {}", byte); }` fires `error: non-ASCII character in byte literal` (uncoded) with the caret on `ℤ` and two label lines `must be ASCII` and `this multibyte character does not fit into a single byte`
main_concept: one centered claim — *integer literals come in five non-decimal syntactic forms beyond the already-known decimal form, all producing the same kind of value (an integer)* — under two coupled framings supplied by Book Ch3-2 *Integer Types* lines 93-107 (load-bearing). (1) *Five new spellings beyond decimal*: Book Table 3-2 (lines 99-107) lists Decimal `98_222`, Hex `0xff`, Octal `0o77`, Binary `0b1111_0000`, and Byte (`u8` only) `b'A'` — five rows the lesson uses verbatim modulo a small variant for *Decimal* (`1_000` to also exercise the separator). The Reference at `tokens.md` line 540-556 corroborates with the formal grammar (`INTEGER_LITERAL → ( BIN_LITERAL | OCT_LITERAL | HEX_LITERAL | DEC_LITERAL ) [SUFFIX_NO_E]?`), and at line 295 names the byte literal as "equivalent to a `u8` unsigned 8-bit integer *number literal*" (load-bearing for the type claim) and "single ASCII character (in the `U+0000` to `U+007F` range)" (load-bearing for the ASCII-only claim, which the contrast probe witnesses as `error: non-ASCII character in byte literal` with two label lines `must be ASCII` and `this multibyte character does not fit into a single byte`). The hex/octal/binary forms are *just other notations for the same numbers* — `0xff`, `255`, `0o377`, `0b1111_1111` are four spellings of the same value — so the lesson does not install number-base theory, only the prefix and digit conventions for each base. (2) *Two notational conventions*: Book lines 93-97 (load-bearing) state both the type suffix ("such as `57u8`, to designate the type") and the visual separator ("use `_` as a visual separator ... `1_000`, which will have the same value as if you had specified `1000`"). The type-suffix form `57u8` is a one-token alternative to `let x: u8 = 57;` — the suffix slot accepts any of the twelve integer type names from lesson 080 (the Reference at line 145 lists exactly those twelve as the integer-suffix set). The `_` separator is purely visual and may sit between digits in any of the four base forms (Reference lines 540-556 license this for decimal, hex, octal, and binary equally). The lesson 019/080 default rule "an unsuffixed integer literal is `i32`" still applies to any unsuffixed form in any base; the suffix is one of the things that *does* fix the type. The byte literal is the only form whose printed value is not the digits the learner typed — `b'A'` prints `65` because the binding's type is `u8` and `{}` formats it as a number. Today is *shallow operational*: install the five non-decimal forms, the separator, the suffix as one of the five, and the ASCII-only restriction on byte literals. Deliberately not installed: float literals `3.14` / `1e10` / `2.0f32` (separate table; queue item O); char escape sequences `'\n'` / `'\u{1F600}'` (lesson 074 deferred); byte string literals `b"hello"` (different type `&[u8; N]`); raw string literals `r"..."` / `r#"..."#` (different family); integer-to-integer `as` casts (extending lesson 034); `MIN`/`MAX` associated constants (deferred from lesson 080); integer overflow at arithmetic (queue item I); the full Reference lexer grammar as named concepts (`BIN_LITERAL`, `SUFFIX_NO_E`); the `overflowing_literals` lint as a centered concept (surfaces in Probe 7's auxiliary transcript on `256u8`); the exact set of allowed corner-case spellings like `0b________1` (legal per Reference but pedagogically distracting).
depends_on: 001-rustc-compile-and-run, 002-fn-main-entry-point, 003-read-rustc-diagnostic, 005-let-binding, 019-type-annotation-i32, 062-u32-unsigned-integer, 080-integer-type-family, 011-println-positional-args (cited), 074-char-type (cited — `'A'` is a `char` literal; today's `b'A'` is the `u8` byte-literal sibling — the `b` prefix is what flips the type), ordinary computer-use (terminal, plain-text editor, `rustc` on `PATH`, Linux/macOS shell, plus the ordinary fact that ASCII is the standard 7-bit character code table where `'A'` is `65` — the lesson does not depend on the table beyond this single equality, which the working probe demonstrates directly)
unlocks: future "integer overflow at arithmetic — debug panic vs release wrap, four method families `wrapping_*` / `checked_*` / `overflowing_*` / `saturating_*` (Book Ch3-2 *Integer Overflow*) — queue item I, with `u8` (lesson 080) as the small-range example and the `_` separator (today) for readable larger literals" moves, future "float literals `3.14` / `1e10` / `2.0f32` — Book Ch3-2 *Floating-Point Types*, queue item O" moves, future "char escape sequences `'\n'`, `'\t'`, `'\u{1F600}'` — extending lesson 074's `char` literal" moves, future "byte string literals `b\"hello\"` — same `b` prefix as today's byte literal but different type `&[u8; N]`, different shape" moves, future "raw string literals `r\"...\"`, `r#\"...\"#` — different prefix family entirely" moves, future "integer-to-integer `as` casts `0xff as i64`, `57u8 as i32` — extending lesson 034's `i32 as f64`, with sign-extension rules" moves, future "the `overflowing_literals` lint as a centered name — surfaces here in Probe 7's auxiliary transcript on `256u8`" moves, future "the formal Reference lexer grammar `INTEGER_LITERAL`, `BIN_LITERAL`, `SUFFIX_NO_E` etc. as named concepts beyond the Book's audience-level table" moves, future "the `MIN` and `MAX` associated constants on each integer type — `u8::MAX`, `i32::MIN`, etc." moves
evidence:
  - output/docs/rust/book/ch03-02-data-types.md
  - output/docs/rust/reference/tokens.md
  - experimental/eduratchet2/runs/rust-moves/observations/081-integer-literal-forms.rs
```

```text
id: 082-cargo-build-release
move: from inside a Cargo package directory (the same setup as cycle 064: `Cargo.toml` and `src/`, scaffolded by `cargo new`), run `cargo build --release` to compile `src/main.rs` with the optimizing settings turned on; the executable is written to `target/release/<name>` instead of `target/debug/<name>` (where `<name>` is the package name from `Cargo.toml`'s `[package] name = "..."` line), and the `Finished` line changes from cycle 064's ``Finished `dev` profile [unoptimized + debuginfo]`` to ``Finished `release` profile [optimized]``; run the optimized binary yourself with `./target/release/<name>` — same `./executable` shape as cycle 001, applied at the parallel `release/` path; concretely the working probe `cargo new --vcs none hello_release && cd hello_release && cargo build && cargo build --release` from a `mktemp -d` scratch dir prints (a) ``Compiling hello_release v0.1.0 (...) / Finished `dev` profile [unoptimized + debuginfo] target(s) in 1.25s`` and (b) ``Compiling hello_release v0.1.0 (...) / Finished `release` profile [optimized] target(s) in 1.72s`` exit 0 each, then `ls target/` shows two parallel subdirectories `debug/` and `release/`, and `./target/release/hello_release` prints `Hello, world!` exit 0; the parallel-directories witness probe edits `src/main.rs` to `println!("Built optimized!");` and runs `cargo build --release` again — only `./target/release/hello_release` prints the new `Built optimized!` while `./target/debug/hello_release` still prints the old `Hello, world!`, demonstrating empirically that the two profiles produce two independent binaries from one source
main_concept: Cargo has two build profiles, and `cargo build --release` selects the non-default one. Cycle 064 installed the default `dev` profile implicitly: `cargo build` writes to `target/debug/<name>` and the `Finished` line says `dev` and `[unoptimized + debuginfo]`. Today centers the `dev`-vs-`release` distinction that cycle 064 explicitly deferred. The Book ch01-03 *Building for Release* section (lines 203-213, load-bearing) is the canonical pedagogical source: "you can use `cargo build --release` to compile it with optimizations. This command will create an executable in *target/release* instead of *target/debug*. The optimizations make your Rust code run faster, but turning them on lengthens the time it takes for your program to compile. This is why there are two different profiles: one for development, when you want to rebuild quickly and often, and another for building the final program you'll give to a user that won't be rebuilt repeatedly and that will run as fast as possible. If you're benchmarking your code's running time, be sure to run `cargo build --release` and benchmark with the executable in *target/release*." Five Book-attributable claims map to lesson sentences: (1) `cargo build --release` produces optimized output (lesson *The Move* + *What Changed* bullet 1); (2) the executable lives at `target/release/<name>` (lesson *The Move* + the `ls target/` probe step); (3) two profiles exist — one for development, one for shipping (lesson *Mental Model Delta* + *What Changed* bullet 3); (4) optimizations cost compile time (the *reason* for the two profiles, restated as the iteration-vs-shipping framing); (5) benchmarking requires the release binary (lesson *What Changed* bullet 4). Cargo man-page `cargo-build.md` lines 165-167 ("Build optimized artifacts with the `release` profile") corroborates as a second corpus witness. The probe's `Finished` line on this host (rustc 1.95.0) reads ``Finished `release` profile [optimized] target(s) in <time>s`` — two characters-in-place changes from cycle 064's ``Finished `dev` profile [unoptimized + debuginfo]`` line: the profile name (`dev` → `release`) and the bracketed phrase (`[unoptimized + debuginfo]` → `[optimized]`). The two parallel directories `target/debug/` and `target/release/` each hold their own independent copy of the executable; the witness probe edits source and rebuilds only the release profile, leaving the older debug binary's stale output as proof that `cargo build --release` does not touch `target/debug/`. The lesson does not claim a measured runtime speed difference on its own probe — the trivial `println!` program is too small to exhibit one, and the Book's framing of "make your Rust code run faster" is a rule about non-trivial code; the lesson is explicit that the probe demonstrates path and output-line shapes only. Deliberately not installed: the actual optimization level (`opt-level = 3`); the specific optimizations rustc applies (inlining, constant folding, dead-code elimination); the `[profile.dev]` / `[profile.release]` manifest sections that let you tweak them; custom profiles (`[profile.bench]`, `[profile.test]`, user-defined profiles) and the general `--profile <name>` flag; `cargo run --release` and `cargo test --release` (same flag composing onto other subcommands — separate moves); the internals of `target/release/` beyond the executable (`deps/`, `.fingerprint/`, `build/`); `cargo bench`; LTO, codegen-units, symbol stripping; the `-r` shorthand for `--release`. Cycle 064's *What To Ignore* named exactly this lesson as a deferred future move ("`cargo build --release` and the `dev` vs `release` profile distinction, optimization levels, `[profile.dev]` / `[profile.release]` manifest sections"); today closes the `--release` flag and the `target/release/` path piece of that deferral, leaving `[profile.*]` syntax and optimization-level taxonomy as the next layer.
depends_on: 001-rustc-compile-and-run, 002-fn-main-entry-point, 011-println-positional-args, 032-cargo-new-and-run, 064-cargo-build-standalone, ordinary computer-use (terminal, plain-text editor, `cargo` on `PATH`, `cd` and `ls`)
unlocks: future "the `[profile.dev]` and `[profile.release]` manifest sections in `Cargo.toml` and the `opt-level` setting (`opt-level = 0` for dev, `opt-level = 3` for release) — the next layer of profile machinery, where today's `[optimized]` / `[unoptimized + debuginfo]` literal strings get unpacked into actual settings" moves, future "the `--profile <name>` flag and custom profiles (`[profile.bench]`, `[profile.test]`, user-defined profiles) — generalizing today's special-case `--release` to the full profile mechanism" moves, future "`cargo run --release` and `cargo test --release` — the same `--release` flag composing onto other Cargo subcommands" moves, future "`cargo bench` and Rust benchmarking — the third built-in subcommand, paired with today's release-binary-only benchmarking rule" moves, future "release-tuning knobs: LTO (link-time optimization), codegen-units, symbol stripping, panic strategy" moves, future "the specific optimizations rustc applies under `[optimized]` — inlining, constant folding, dead-code elimination — as the realized meaning of the bracketed phrase" moves, future "debug info as a separately-installed concept — what `[unoptimized + debuginfo]` provides that `[optimized]` strips, and how it relates to a debugger" moves
evidence:
  - output/docs/rust/book/ch01-03-hello-cargo.md
  - output/docs/rust/cargo/commands/cargo-build.md
  - experimental/eduratchet2/runs/rust-moves/observations/082-cargo-build-release/Cargo.toml
  - experimental/eduratchet2/runs/rust-moves/observations/082-cargo-build-release/src/main.rs
  - experimental/eduratchet2/runs/rust-moves/observations/082-cargo-build-release/.gitignore
status: accepted
```

```text
id: 083-integer-overflow
move: from inside a Cargo package directory (the same setup as cycle 082), write a four-line program that overflows `u8` arithmetic at runtime — `fn main() { let mut x: u8 = 255; x = x + 1; println!("x = {}", x); }` — and build it both ways: `cargo build` then `./target/debug/<name>` produces the panic ``thread 'main' (...) panicked at src/main.rs:3:9: / attempt to add with overflow / note: run with `RUST_BACKTRACE=1` environment variable to display a backtrace`` exit `101` (no stdout, panic on stderr — the `RUST_BACKTRACE=1` line and the `101` exit are reused unchanged from lessons 053 and 078); `cargo build --release` then `./target/release/<name>` runs to completion and prints `x = 0` exit `0` (the value wrapped — `255 + 1 = 256` is one past the `u8` range and two's-complement-wraps to `0`); the same source under two profiles produces two different runtime outcomes, and both are real behaviors of rustc 1.95.0; concretely the canonical four-line probe is in `observations/083-integer-overflow/src/main.rs`, the `Cargo.toml` is the bit-exact `cargo new --vcs none overflow_demo` template (`name = "overflow_demo"`, `edition = "2024"`), and the auxiliary `+ 2` form (release prints `x = 1`, witnessing 257→1) and the auxiliary `0u8 - 1` form (debug panics with `attempt to subtract with overflow`, release wraps to `255`) are documented in the evidence appendix as Book-corroborating witnesses; the lesson then names — but does NOT call probes against — the four standard-library method families on primitive numeric types: `wrapping_*` (always wrap, in any profile), `checked_*` (return `None` on overflow), `overflowing_*` (return the value and a `bool`), `saturating_*` (clamp to MIN/MAX); each method family is a future move
main_concept: one centered claim with two coupled framings under the Book's *Integer Overflow* subsection (Ch3-2 lines 114-143, load-bearing). (1) *Integer overflow at runtime is real, profile-dependent, and named*: when arithmetic produces a value outside the type's range and rustc cannot evaluate the arithmetic at compile time (lesson 080's literal-out-of-range rule does not apply), the *runtime* behavior depends on the Cargo build profile from cycles 064 and 082. Book lines 119-124 attribute the *debug* behavior to "checks for integer overflow that cause your program to *panic* at runtime" — the lesson grounds the exact panic message `attempt to <op> with overflow` empirically (probe step 5: `attempt to add with overflow`; step 9: `attempt to subtract with overflow`) since the Book gives the *concept* but not the verbatim wording. The lesson reuses the panic trailer shape (`thread 'main' (...) panicked at ...:line:col:` / `RUST_BACKTRACE=1` note / exit `101`) that lessons 053 and 078 already installed; only the message slot is new. Book lines 126-131 attribute the *release* behavior to "Rust does *not* include checks ... performs *two's complement wrapping*", with the worked examples 256→0 and 257→1 for `u8` reproduced verbatim by probe steps 7 and 8. The under-flow direction (0u8 - 1 → 255) is captured in step 9 as the same overflow concept under the Book's umbrella (lines 116-118 frame *integer overflow* as "a value outside that range" — direction-neutral). (2) *Four method families exist for explicit control*: Book lines 135-143 (load-bearing) name `wrapping_*` (always wrap, in any profile), `checked_*` (return `None` on overflow), `overflowing_*` (return value and a Boolean indicating overflow), `saturating_*` (saturate at the value's minimum or maximum values). The lesson names all four with the brief Book descriptions; the *Check Yourself* prompts test the family-name-to-description mapping and nothing more. Book lines 131-133 ("Relying on integer overflow's wrapping behavior is considered an error") supply the value claim that the wrap is not a feature — restated verbatim in *What Changed* bullet 4. Today is *shallow operational*: install the runtime-overflow concept, the debug-panic-vs-release-wrap rule, the panic-message slot `attempt to <op> with overflow`, and the four family names. Deliberately not installed: the actual signatures of the four method families (`u8::wrapping_add(self, rhs: u8) -> u8` etc.) and method-call invocation; the return-type machinery (`Option<T>` for `checked_*`, `(T, bool)` for `overflowing_*`); the `MIN`/`MAX` associated constants named in `saturating_*`'s description (deferred from lesson 080); the `arithmetic_overflow` deny-by-default lint; the `[profile.*]` `overflow-checks` knob that toggles the check independently of profile; the `wrapping_neg` / `unchecked_*` / `strict_*` newer method families outside Book scope; the `std::num::Wrapping<T>` newtype; IEEE-754 floating-point overflow; *underflow* as a separately named concept (Book treats subtractive wrap as the same overflow concept, lines 116-118); two's complement representation as a typed concept (lesson 080 named it; the Book's phrase *two's complement wrapping* is reused only as the name of the release-mode wrap behavior); multiplicative and signed-integer overflow as additional probe shapes (the same `attempt to <op> with overflow` family applies, but is not captured today).
depends_on: 001-rustc-compile-and-run, 002-fn-main-entry-point, 005-let-binding, 006-mut-binding, 011-println-positional-args, 032-cargo-new-and-run, 053-result-expect-and-panic, 064-cargo-build-standalone, 078-array-out-of-bounds-panic, 080-integer-type-family, 082-cargo-build-release, ordinary computer-use (terminal, plain-text editor, `cargo` on `PATH`, `cd`, `echo $?` for the shell exit status, the stdout/stderr split already used by lessons 053 and 078)
unlocks: future "the `wrapping_*` method family — `u8::wrapping_add`, `u8::wrapping_sub`, etc. as concrete calls; method-call mechanics (lesson 040) returning the wrapped value as a plain integer in any profile" moves, future "the `checked_*` method family and `Option<T>` as a named return-type concept — `u8::checked_add(255, 1)` returns `None`, with the `Some(v)` / `None` enum from std" moves, future "the `overflowing_*` method family and the `(T, bool)` tuple return type — `u8::overflowing_add(255, 1)` returns `(0, true)`, building on lesson 072's tuple-type install" moves, future "the `saturating_*` method family — `u8::saturating_add(255, 1)` returns `255`, with the `MIN`/`MAX` associated constants becoming centered concepts" moves, future "`u8::MAX` and `u8::MIN` (and their siblings on every primitive integer type) as named associated constants — deferred from lesson 080, surfaced again here in `saturating_*`'s description" moves, future "the `[profile.dev]` / `[profile.release]` `overflow-checks` setting in `Cargo.toml` — toggles today's debug-mode runtime check independently of the profile, the next layer of profile-tuning machinery deferred from lesson 082" moves, future "the `arithmetic_overflow` deny-by-default lint as a centered name — the compile-time mechanism that catches some obviously-overflowing arithmetic before runtime, distinct from today's runtime check" moves, future "the `panic!` macro itself — the underlying mechanism the runtime overflow check invokes, deferred since lesson 053" moves, future "the `std::num::Wrapping<T>` newtype — alternative API for opt-in wrapping arithmetic" moves, future "signed-integer overflow as a centered probe (`i8::MAX + 1` wraps to `i8::MIN == -128` in release) — same `attempt to <op> with overflow` family applies, separate witness" moves, future "IEEE-754 floating-point overflow as a separately-installed concept — different family, different rules (`f64::MAX + f64::MAX` becomes `inf`, not a panic or wrap)" moves
evidence:
  - output/docs/rust/book/ch03-02-data-types.md
  - experimental/eduratchet2/runs/rust-moves/observations/083-integer-overflow/Cargo.toml
  - experimental/eduratchet2/runs/rust-moves/observations/083-integer-overflow/src/main.rs
  - experimental/eduratchet2/runs/rust-moves/observations/083-integer-overflow/.gitignore
  - experimental/eduratchet2/runs/rust-moves/evidence/083-integer-overflow.md
status: accepted
```

```text
id: 084-cargo-check
move: from inside a Cargo package directory (the same setup as cycle 064: `Cargo.toml` and `src/`, scaffolded by `cargo new`), run `cargo check` to verify that `src/main.rs` compiles without producing an executable; the output's leading line of work is ``Checking <name> v0.1.0 (...)`` instead of cycle 064's ``Compiling <name> v0.1.0 (...)``, the `Finished` line keeps cycle 064's shape (``Finished `dev` profile [unoptimized + debuginfo] target(s) in <time>s``), and the binary at `target/debug/<name>` is absent — `ls target/debug/<name>` exits 1 with `No such file or directory`, so there is nothing to run; concretely the working probe `cargo new --vcs none hello_check && cd hello_check && cargo check && ls target/debug/hello_check && cargo build && ./target/debug/hello_check` from a `mktemp -d` scratch dir prints (a) ``Checking hello_check v0.1.0 (...) / Finished `dev` profile [unoptimized + debuginfo] target(s) in 0.46s`` exit 0, (b) `ls: target/debug/hello_check: No such file or directory` exit 1 (with `target/debug/` containing only the bookkeeping subdirectories `build deps examples incremental` and lacking the binary `hello_check` and the dep file `hello_check.d`), (c) ``Compiling hello_check v0.1.0 (...) / Finished `dev` profile [unoptimized + debuginfo] target(s) in 0.76s`` exit 0 (with `target/debug/` now also containing `hello_check` and `hello_check.d`), and (d) `Hello, world!` exit 0; the cache-hit witness probe runs `cargo check` twice in a row in a separate scratch dir and observes that the second invocation prints only ``Finished `dev` profile [unoptimized + debuginfo] target(s) in 0.00s`` (no `Checking` line), reusing on-disk metadata in the same way cycle 064 documented for `cargo build`
main_concept: Cargo has a third verb beyond cycles 032's `cargo run` and 064's `cargo build`. `cargo check` is the typecheck-only form: it does the compile work up to but not including code generation, leaves the same Cargo bookkeeping in `target/debug/` minus the executable, and is meant for fast iteration while editing. The Book ch01-03 *Hello, Cargo!* lines 172-187 (load-bearing) is the canonical pedagogical source: lines 172-173 give the introduction ("Cargo also provides a command called `cargo check`. This command quickly checks your code to make sure it compiles but doesn't produce an executable"); lines 175-179 give the verbatim sample output with the leading verb `Checking` and the same `Finished` line shape as `cargo build`; lines 181-184 give the *why* ("Often, `cargo check` is much faster than `cargo build` because it skips the step of producing an executable"); lines 184-187 give the workflow framing ("many Rustaceans run `cargo check` periodically as they write their program to make sure it compiles. Then, they run `cargo build` when they're ready to use the executable"); lines 189-195 recap the four-verb framing the lesson positions today's move within (`cargo new` from cycle 032, `cargo build` from cycle 064, `cargo run` from cycle 032, `cargo check` today). The man-page `cargo-check.md` lines 14-19 corroborates as a second-corpus witness ("Check a local package and all of its dependencies for errors. This will essentially compile the packages without performing the final step of code generation, which is faster than running `cargo build`. The compiler will save metadata files to disk so that future runs will reuse them if the source has not been modified."). Five Book-attributable claims map to lesson sentences: (1) `cargo check` checks that the code compiles without producing an executable (lesson *The Move* + *What Changed* bullet 1); (2) the leading line of work begins with `Checking` not `Compiling` (lesson *The Move* + *Try It* step 3 + *What Changed* bullet 1, witnessed empirically by step 3 of the probe); (3) no executable lands at `target/debug/<name>` (lesson *The Move* + *Try It* step 4, witnessed empirically by `ls target/debug/hello_check` exit 1); (4) `cargo check` is faster than `cargo build` because the code-generation step is skipped (lesson *What Changed* bullet 3 — the *why*); (5) the recommended workflow is `cargo check` while editing, `cargo build` (or `cargo run`) when you actually want the binary (lesson *Mental Model Delta* + *What Changed* bullet 4). The contrast is between two valid-but-different working forms (steps 3 and 5 of the probe — same package, same source, same toolchain, only the Cargo verb differs); no working-vs-broken contrast is needed because both commands succeed and the lesson's claim is about the *shape* of their effects (`Checking` vs `Compiling` in the output; binary absent vs present at `target/debug/<name>`). The lesson does not claim a measured speed difference on this probe — the trivial `println!` program is too small to exhibit a visible one — and the Book's "much faster" sentence is restated as a *rule*, with the man-page corroborating as a second corpus witness, not as a measurement. Today is *shallow operational*: install the third Cargo verb and the no-executable-at-`target/debug/<name>` consequence; install the leading-verb flip in the output as the textual witness; install the iteration-vs-build workflow as the Book's recommended use. Deliberately not installed: `cargo check --release` (the `--release` flag from cycle 082 composing onto `cargo check`); `cargo clippy` (a separate also-fast linting tool); `--message-format=json` and other Cargo verbosity controls; IDE/editor integrations that run `cargo check` on save (rust-analyzer and friends); `cargo check --all-targets` / `--tests` / `--examples` (targets beyond the default binary); the man-page caveat that "some diagnostics and errors are only emitted during code generation, so they inherently won't be reported with `cargo check`" (rare in practice for the audience's program sizes); the internal contents of `target/debug/`'s bookkeeping subdirectories (`build/`, `deps/`, `examples/`, `incremental/`) — same deferral as cycle 064.
depends_on: 001-rustc-compile-and-run, 002-fn-main-entry-point, 011-println-positional-args, 032-cargo-new-and-run, 064-cargo-build-standalone, ordinary computer-use (terminal, plain-text editor, `cargo` on `PATH`, `cd` and `ls`)
unlocks: future "`cargo check --release` — the `--release` flag from cycle 082 composing onto `cargo check`, producing a `Checking` line plus the `release` profile `Finished` shape, still no executable" moves, future "`cargo clippy` — the separate, also-fast linting subcommand built on the same code-gen-skipped pipeline as `cargo check`, with its own lint vocabulary on top of rustc's diagnostics" moves, future "`cargo check --all-targets` / `--tests` / `--examples` — checking targets beyond the default binary, requiring some idea of what tests/examples are in a Cargo package" moves, future "the man-page caveat that some diagnostics are only emitted during code generation — `cargo check` does not catch them, `cargo build` does — relevant for monomorphization-driven errors in generic code" moves, future "rust-analyzer and editor on-save `cargo check` integration — the operational layer above today's command-line invocation" moves, future "`cargo fix` — built on the same code-gen-skipped checking pipeline, applies suggested edits automatically" moves
evidence:
  - output/docs/rust/book/ch01-03-hello-cargo.md
  - output/docs/rust/cargo/commands/cargo-check.md
  - experimental/eduratchet2/runs/rust-moves/observations/084-cargo-check/Cargo.toml
  - experimental/eduratchet2/runs/rust-moves/observations/084-cargo-check/src/main.rs
  - experimental/eduratchet2/runs/rust-moves/observations/084-cargo-check/.gitignore
  - experimental/eduratchet2/runs/rust-moves/evidence/084-cargo-check.md
status: accepted
```

```text
id: 085-toolchain-housekeeping
move: from any directory in any shell, run three commands that operate on the installed Rust toolchain (not on any project or source file): `rustc --version` (prints `rustc x.y.z (abcabcabc yyyy-mm-dd)` — Book Ch1-1 line 83 names this format verbatim — version number, commit hash, commit date in parentheses; on this host `rustc 1.95.0 (59807616e 2026-04-14)`), `cargo --version` (prints the same shape, e.g. `cargo 1.95.0 (f2d3ce0bd 2026-03-21)`; the Book's "If you see a version number, you have it!" Ch1-3 line 27), and `rustup update` (re-runs `rustup` to refresh the stable toolchain — Book Ch1-1 lines 112-119 invocation `$ rustup update` verbatim, "Once Rust is installed via `rustup`, updating to a newly released version is easy. From your shell, run the following update script"); concretely the working probe runs all three from a non-package directory, with `rustc --version` and `cargo --version` printing one line each exit 0, and `rustup update` printing the four-line transcript ``info: syncing channel updates for stable-x86_64-apple-darwin / stable-x86_64-apple-darwin unchanged - rustc 1.95.0 (59807616e 2026-04-14) / info: checking for self-update (current version: 1.29.0) / info: cleaning up downloads & tmp directories`` exit 0 (the `unchanged` outcome on this host because the channel was already current; the Book's framing of "newly released version" names the alternative outcome where rustup downloads a new stable); the lesson does not include a working-vs-broken contrast (the three commands succeed by design on a healthy toolchain; intentional break cases — Rust not on PATH, cargo not on PATH, network down — would require breaking the host install or are intermittent), but does name the two non-destructive contrastive pieces: `rustc --version` as sanity-check vs the Book's `%PATH%`/`$PATH` failure mode (lines 86-105, named not probed), and `rustup update`'s two outcomes (unchanged vs downloaded a new stable, both Book-framed)
main_concept: three small commands that share one operational role — toolchain housekeeping. None of them touches a `.rs` file, none needs a Cargo project, none compiles anything; all three run from any directory in any shell against the installed Rust toolchain. The first two ("what version do I have right now?") are inspection: `rustc --version` and `cargo --version` print a one-line `<tool> x.y.z (abcabcabc yyyy-mm-dd)` with the leading word, then version triple, then parenthesized commit hash and date; the Book's exact format string `rustc x.y.z (abcabcabc yyyy-mm-dd)` (Ch1-1 line 83) is the canonical shape and the probe's `rustc 1.95.0 (59807616e 2026-04-14)` is the host's literal instantiation; cargo's output matches this shape character-class for character-class. The third ("make me current") is refresh: `rustup update` re-runs `rustup` (the program that installed Rust on this host — Book Ch1-1 lines 27-41 names rustup as "the `rustup` tool, which installs the latest stable version of Rust") and reports `<channel> unchanged - <build>` if the stable channel is already current, or downloads and installs the newer stable if one exists. The three are co-installed (rather than split into three cycles) because (a) they share one operational role, (b) they share one mental-model delta — the learner had no toolchain-management vocabulary before today and has the minimum sufficient vocabulary after, (c) they share the same shell context with no flags or one trivial flag (no editor, no package, no file), (d) the Book itself treats them as one interrelated sanity-check-and-refresh set across two adjacent chapters (Ch1-1 *Troubleshooting* + *Updating and Uninstalling* and Ch1-3 *Hello, Cargo!*), and (e) `rustup update` is operationally meaningful only against the `--version` baselines that let the learner recognize what changed. Today is *shallow operational*: install only the three commands, the `rustc --version` format, the cargo-mirrors-rustc-shape claim, and the rustup-as-toolchain-refresher framing. Deliberately not installed: `rustup self uninstall` (Book lines 121-125; named in *What To Ignore* as out of scope per the queue's "administrative" label); `rustc -V` shorthand (works on this host per probe step 5; lesson uses `--version` to match the Book); `rustup --version` (works on this host per probe step 4 — `rustup 1.29.0 (28d1352db 2026-03-05)` plus two `info:` trailers; named only via the natural `rustup update` transcript that prints `current version: 1.29.0`, not as a fourth centered command); toolchain channels and `rustup toolchain install <channel>` / `rustup default <channel>`; `rustup component add <name>` (rustfmt, clippy); `rustup override set <toolchain>` and `rust-toolchain.toml` per-directory pinning; `rustup target add <triple>` (cross-compilation); the Linux/macOS `curl ... | sh` install script and Windows installer (out of scope per queue's host-OS-sprawl deferral); `cargo --help` / `rustup --help` content; the JSON or HTML rendering of any of these outputs.
depends_on: 001-rustc-compile-and-run, 064-cargo-build-standalone (cited — establishes `cargo` is on PATH), ordinary computer-use (terminal/shell prompt; reading text on stdout; the named one-line ordinary-computer-use fact that `rustup` is the official tool that installed Rust on this host — Book Ch1-1 lines 27-41 names it; no editor, no source file, no project required for any of today's three commands)
unlocks: future "`rustup self uninstall` — the Book's sibling administrative command (Ch1-1 lines 121-125), deferred today as out of scope per the Ch1-3 closure queue" moves, future "`rustup --version` as a centered fourth toolchain-housekeeping command — its output also matches the `<tool> x.y.z (abcabcabc yyyy-mm-dd)` shape today installs" moves, future "toolchain channels (`stable`, `beta`, `nightly`) and `rustup toolchain install <channel>` / `rustup default <channel>` — extending today's `rustup update` to channel-aware management" moves, future "`rustup component add <name>` — adding components like `rustfmt` and `clippy` to the installed toolchain" moves, future "`rustup target add <triple>` — adding cross-compilation targets" moves, future "`rust-toolchain.toml` per-directory toolchain pinning and `rustup override set <toolchain>` — directory-scoped overrides of the today-installed default-stable-channel framing" moves, future "the `rustc -V` and `cargo -V` single-uppercase-`V` shorthands as alternative spellings of `--version`" moves, future "`cargo --help` and `rustup --help` as a way to discover further subcommands beyond today's three" moves, future "edition declarations in `Cargo.toml` (`edition = \"2024\"`) — toolchain-version-adjacent but a separate move" moves
evidence:
  - output/docs/rust/book/ch01-01-installation.md
  - output/docs/rust/book/ch01-03-hello-cargo.md
  - experimental/eduratchet2/runs/rust-moves/evidence/085-toolchain-housekeeping.md
```

```text
id: 086-rustup-doc
move: from any directory in any shell, run `rustup doc` to open the local Rust documentation in the default browser; the bare command resolves to `~/.rustup/toolchains/<channel>/share/doc/rust/html/index.html` (on this host `/Users/eli/.rustup/toolchains/stable-x86_64-apple-darwin/share/doc/rust/html/index.html` per `rustup doc --path` exit 0), the file is a real HTML file (10730 bytes, `<title>Rust Documentation</title>`), the same directory contains subdirectories `book/`, `reference/`, `std/`, `cargo/`, `clippy/`, etc. (each with its own `index.html` — `book/index.html` `<title>The Rust Programming Language ...</title>`, `std/index.html` `<title>std - Rust</title>`, `reference/index.html` `<title>Introduction - The Rust Reference</title>`), and the bare invocation prints exactly one line of confirmation `Opening docs in your browser` exit 0 to the terminal while opening a new tab in the default browser; concretely the working probe runs from `/tmp` (a non-package directory with no `.rs` files) — `cd /tmp && rustup doc` prints `Opening docs in your browser` exit 0 and opens the local doc index in the default browser; the audit-substitute probe `cd /tmp && rustup doc --path` prints the resolved file path with no browser action, and `ls` + `grep -o "<title>...</title>"` on that path and on `book/index.html`, `std/index.html`, `reference/index.html` confirm the files exist on local disk; the contrast probe `cd /tmp && rustup doc --book` prints `Opening docs named \`book\` in your browser` exit 0 and `rustup doc --path --book` resolves to `.../html/book/index.html` (different one-line message, different path) — the lesson defers `--book` to *What To Ignore* but uses this implicit contrast to ground the centered "bare command opens the index" framing
main_concept: Rust ships a local copy of its own documentation as HTML on disk under the rustup-managed toolchain directory (`~/.rustup/toolchains/<channel>/share/doc/rust/html/`), installed alongside `rustc` and `cargo` by `rustup` (lesson 085's "rustup is the program that installed Rust"), and `rustup doc` is the toolchain-housekeeping subcommand that opens that local copy in the default web browser. The Book Ch1-1 *Reading the Local Documentation* lines 130-138 (load-bearing) is the canonical pedagogical source: lines 132-134 give the framing verbatim ("The installation of Rust also includes a local copy of the documentation so that you can read it offline. Run `rustup doc` to open the local documentation in your browser"); lines 136-138 give the use case ("Any time a type or function is provided by the standard library and you're not sure what it does or how to use it, use the application programming interface (API) documentation to find out!"). Five Book-attributable claims map to lesson sentences: (1) the local doc set was installed alongside `rustc` and `cargo`, not separately fetched (lesson *What Changed* bullet 2 — verbatim Book quote); (2) the docs are readable offline (lesson *Prerequisites* "no network needed" + *Check Yourself* (b)); (3) `rustup doc` opens that local copy in the browser (lesson *The Move* + *What Changed* bullet 1 + *Try It* literal transcript); (4) the API docs are the lookup for stdlib types and functions (lesson *What Changed* bullet 3 — verbatim Book quote); (5) the command runs without a project — same shell context as cycle 085's three commands (lesson *The Move*'s "from any directory" framing). Beyond the Book, the lesson names three doc sets specifically (Book, Reference, std API docs) grounded by direct file-system witnesses (`<title>` of `book/index.html`, `std/index.html`, `reference/index.html`) and stops there — the full `rustup doc --help` flag list (`--alloc`, `--cargo`, `--clippy`, `--core`, `--edition-guide`, `--embedded-book`, `--error-codes`, `--nomicon`, `--proc_macro`, `--reference`, `--releases`, `--rust-by-example`, `--rustc`, `--rustc-docs`, `--rustdoc`, `--std`, `--style-guide`, `--test`, `--unstable-book`, plus `[TOPIC]`) is named in *What To Ignore* and not enumerated. The lesson is *shallow operational*: install only the bare command, the offline-and-local framing, the rustup-installed-it framing, and the "Book + Reference + std API docs" naming. The unusual-among-toolchain-housekeeping aspect is that the *most important* effect (a new browser tab) is hard to capture in a terminal transcript, so the lesson uses three substitute probes: (a) the one-line `Opening docs in your browser` stdout the command does emit, (b) `rustup doc --path` printing the resolved file path without opening anything, and (c) `ls` + `<title>` on the resolved files. The lesson names the browser tab as the witness and the path/file-existence probes as the auditable substitute. Deliberately not installed: `rustup doc --book` / `--reference` / `--std` / `--cargo` / etc. as centered commands (used only as a contrast probe in step 5 of the appendix); `rustup doc <topic>` (topic-aware lookup); the exact on-disk path memorization; `cargo doc` and `cargo doc --open` (project-scoped, Ch14-2 territory — different command, different scope); doc comments (`///`) and `cargo test --doc` (Ch14-2); the rustdoc-generated HTML internals, in-page search, and `rustdoc` as a tool; cross-host path differences (Linux `$HOME/.rustup/...`, Windows `%USERPROFILE%\.rustup\...`); browser identity (Safari vs Chrome vs Firefox).
depends_on: 085-toolchain-housekeeping (load-bearing — `rustup` is the program that installed Rust on this host and manages the toolchain; today extends 085's `rustup update` with a sibling subcommand `rustup doc`), 001-rustc-compile-and-run (cited — shell prompt; typing a command and reading output), ordinary computer-use (terminal/shell prompt; the host has a default web browser configured — same kind of fact as "the host has a terminal"; no network needed; the local Rust documentation is HTML at a path on disk under the rustup-managed toolchain directory and `rustup doc` resolves to a `file://` URL — named once, not centered as a concept)
unlocks: future "`rustup doc --book` / `--reference` / `--std` / `--cargo` / `--clippy` / etc. — the flag list for opening one specific doc set instead of the index" moves, future "`rustup doc <topic>` — topic-aware lookup (e.g. `rustup doc usize`, `rustup doc std::fs`)" moves, future "`cargo doc` and `cargo doc --open` — project-scoped documentation built from the current Cargo project's source code; different command, different scope; Ch14-2 territory" moves, future "doc comments (`///`) and how `cargo doc` consumes them — Ch14-2 territory" moves, future "`cargo test --doc` — running examples embedded in doc comments as tests; Ch14-2 territory" moves, future "looking up a specific stdlib type's methods in the API docs (e.g. opening `std::vec::Vec` and reading its `push`/`pop` signatures) — pedagogical follow-on once the audience has any stdlib type beyond `String`/`Vec`/`Result`" moves, future "the in-page search box on the rustdoc HTML — UI navigation rather than a Rust language move" moves
evidence:
  - output/docs/rust/book/ch01-01-installation.md
  - experimental/eduratchet2/runs/rust-moves/evidence/086-rustup-doc.md
```

```text
id: 087-rustfmt
move: from inside a Cargo package directory (the same setup as cycle 064: `Cargo.toml` and `src/`, scaffolded by `cargo new`), run `cargo fmt` and observe the *before-vs-after source-file diff* — Cargo invokes `rustfmt` on the package's Rust files and rewrites them in place to the *community standard* style (conventional indentation, spacing, line breaks, brace placement); concretely the working probe `cargo new --vcs none hello_fmt && cd hello_fmt`, then writing the deliberately ugly two-line source `fn main(){let x=42;\nprintln!("x = {}",x);}` to `src/main.rs`, then `cargo build` (exit 0, ``Compiling hello_fmt v0.1.0 (...) / Finished `dev` profile [unoptimized + debuginfo] target(s) in 1.57s``) and `./target/debug/hello_fmt` (prints `x = 42`, exit 0), then `cargo fmt` (no terminal output, exit 0) — at which point `src/main.rs` has been rewritten in place to four lines `fn main() {\n    let x = 42;\n    println!("x = {}", x);\n}` with spaces around `=`, after `,`, and between `)` and `{`, four-space indent on the body, and the closing `}` on its own line — and finally `cargo build` (recompiles the changed source) plus `./target/debug/hello_fmt` again (prints `x = 42`, exit 0, *bit-identical* to the before-output) witnessing that the rewrite is style-only; the auxiliary witness `diff -u /tmp/before.rs /tmp/after.rs` (captured by copying `src/main.rs` before and after) shows two lines removed and four lines added with every change being whitespace/indentation/line-break, no tokens added or removed; the auxiliary `cargo run`-based scratch (`semantics_witness/`) repeats the cycle and produces the same `x = 42` output before and after — operationally witnessing the Book Appendix D claim "should only change the code style, not the code semantics" verbatim
main_concept: one centered claim with two coupled framings under Book Ch1-2 lines 115-120 and Appendix D lines 8-26 (both load-bearing). (1) *`cargo fmt` rewrites the package's Rust source files in place to the community standard style*: from inside a Cargo package directory, `cargo fmt` invokes the underlying `rustfmt` tool to reformat `src/main.rs` (and all other files in the crate's module tree) according to the conventional rules — Book Appendix D line 24 verbatim "Running this command reformats all the Rust code in the current crate." The rewrite covers indentation (four-space body indent), spacing (around `=`, after `,`, between `)` and `{`), line breaks (newline after `{`, closing `}` on its own line), and brace placement (the opening `{` of `fn main` on the *same* line as the function declaration with one space between — the rule Book Ch1-2 line 112 names verbatim, "It's good style to place the opening curly bracket on the same line as the function declaration, adding one space in between"; rustfmt enforces this rule among many). The lesson installs *that there is a standard formatter* and that the operational answer to "what is the right way to format this?" is `cargo fmt`; the rule *list* is deliberately deferred. (2) *The rewrite is style-only — what the program does is unchanged*: Book Appendix D line 25 verbatim "This should only change the code style, not the code semantics." The probe witnesses this with bit-identical `x = 42` exit 0 stdout before and after `cargo fmt` (Steps 2 and 5 of the appendix; Step 6 auxiliary repeats with `cargo run`). The diff is whitespace-only — no token added or removed — so the compiled behavior is unchanged by construction, and the lesson's *Mental Model Delta* "After" frames the rewrite as the operational shape of "style, not semantics". The bundling claim is co-installed without being centered: Book Ch1-2 line 118 verbatim "The Rust team has included this tool with the standard Rust distribution, as `rustc` is, so it should already be installed on your computer" reused as cycle 085's framing — the same rustup that delivered `rustc` and `cargo` also delivered `rustfmt` and `cargo-fmt`, both as rustup-managed shims at `~/.cargo/bin/` (operational witness via `which` and `rustup component list --installed`). Today is *shallow operational*: install only the Cargo-aware `cargo fmt` form, the in-place rewrite, the style-only invariant, and the toolchain-bundling framing. Deliberately not installed: `rustfmt` invoked directly on a single file (Appendix D names it as the finer-grained sibling — `rustfmt allows finer grained control` — but today only installs the Cargo-aware form); `rustfmt.toml` / `.rustfmt.toml` configuration files for tweaking the standard style; the exact rule list (four-space indent, max line length, trailing-comma policy, blank-line conventions); `cargo fmt -- --check` (Step 8 auxiliary captured the transcript exit 1; CI use case deferred); `rustfix` (sibling tool in Appendix D, different scope); `cargo clippy` (separate linting tool, lesson 084 unlocks); IDE / on-save format integration; multi-file crates with `mod` declarations (Step 7 auxiliary witnessed `cargo fmt` formatting two files in the module tree, and an orphan `src/extra.rs` not declared in any `mod` is left unchanged — clarification of "all the Rust code in the current crate" but not centered today; `mod` is not yet installed); the standard style as a "fixed point" (running `cargo fmt` twice in a row is a no-op the second time — auxiliary observation in Step 3, not centered).
depends_on: 001-rustc-compile-and-run, 002-fn-main-entry-point, 011-println-positional-args, 032-cargo-new-and-run, 064-cargo-build-standalone, 008-define-and-call-function (cited — `fn name() { ... }` shape used by the probe's source), 085-toolchain-housekeeping (cited — the same rustup that installed `rustc` and `cargo` also installed `rustfmt` and `cargo-fmt`), ordinary computer-use (terminal, plain-text editor, `cargo` on `PATH`, `cd`, the ability to open a text file before and after a change to *see* what changed — the lesson's central witness is a visible source-file diff)
unlocks: future "`rustfmt` invoked directly on a single file (e.g. `rustfmt src/main.rs`) — the finer-grained sibling form Appendix D names alongside `cargo fmt`" moves, future "`rustfmt.toml` / `.rustfmt.toml` configuration files for tweaking the standard style — indent width, max line length, trailing-comma policy, etc." moves, future "`cargo fmt -- --check` — the CI flag that exits non-zero if any file would be reformatted, without modifying it" moves, future "`rustfix` — Appendix D's sibling tool that auto-applies compiler suggestions, distinct from formatting" moves, future "`cargo clippy` — the separate linting subcommand for *suspicious patterns* rather than style; named in lesson 084's unlocks" moves, future "the rustfmt rule set as named concepts — four-space indent, max line length 100, trailing commas in multi-line `match` arms, blank-line conventions" moves, future "IDE / editor on-save `cargo fmt` integration — operational layer above today's command-line invocation" moves, future "multi-file crates and `mod` declarations — the structural reason `cargo fmt` reaches every `.rs` in the crate's module tree (Step 7 auxiliary)" moves, future "the standard style as a fixed point — `cargo fmt` is idempotent on already-formatted files" moves, future "`rustup component add rustfmt` — adding the rustfmt component to a toolchain that does not have it (Step 0 evidence shows it preinstalled on this host; not all toolchains include it by default in unusual install paths)" moves
evidence:
  - output/docs/rust/book/ch01-02-hello-world.md
  - output/docs/rust/book/appendix-04-useful-development-tools.md
```

```text
id: 088-f32-floating-point
move: bind a 32-bit float with `let small: f32 = 3.0;` — same `: TYPE` annotation slot from lesson 019, same float literal shape (`3.0`) from lesson 033, type name `f32` instead of `f64`; the unsuffixed float literal default is still `f64` (lesson 033 unchanged) so `let big = 2.0;` infers `f64`; concretely the working probe `fn main() { let big = 2.0; let small: f32 = 3.0; println!("big = {}", big); println!("small = {}", small); }` (the Book's canonical example at ch03-02-data-types.md lines 158-162 verbatim modulo `x`/`y` → `big`/`small` rename and two added `println!` lines) compiled with `rustc demo.rs` and run as `./demo` exits 0 silent and prints `big = 2` then `small = 3` (whole-valued floats print without a trailing decimal under default `{}` formatting); the contrast probe `fn main() { let small: f32 = 3; println!("small = {}", small); }` fires `error[E0308]: mismatched types` with `--> broken.rs:2:22` underlining `f32` (annotation, `expected due to this`) and `3` (literal, `expected `f32`, found integer`), then `help: use a float literal` with the source-diff suggestion `let small: f32 = 3.0;` and `++` under the new `.0` — identical shape to lesson 033's `f64 = 3` diagnostic with `f32` substituted for `f64`
main_concept: one centered claim — *Rust has two floating-point primitives, `f32` (32 bits) and `f64` (64 bits), and the lesson-019 `: TYPE` annotation slot accepts `f32` the same way it accepts `f64`* — under three coupled framings supplied by Book Ch3-2 *Floating-Point Types* lines 145-165 (load-bearing). (1) *Two primitives, two widths, both signed* (Book lines 147-151 verbatim): "Rust also has two primitive types for *floating-point numbers*, which are numbers with decimal points. Rust's floating-point types are `f32` and `f64`, which are 32 bits and 64 bits in size, respectively. The default type is `f64` because on modern CPUs, it's roughly the same speed as `f32` but is capable of more precision. All floating-point types are signed." Lesson 033 already installed one of the two (`f64`); today installs the second by name and width. (2) *Default still `f64`*: Lesson 033's unsuffixed-float-literal-default rule is unchanged. The working probe's `let big = 2.0;` infers `f64` (Book inline comment `// f64`); to bind a literal at `f32` instead, write `: f32` (Book inline comment `// f32` on `let y: f32 = 3.0;`). (3) *IEEE-754* (Book line 165 verbatim): "Floating-point numbers are represented according to the IEEE-754 standard." Name installed; mechanics (`NaN`, `infinity`, signed zero, denormals, comparison gotchas) stay deferred from lesson 033. The contrast probe is the load-bearing negative witness for *`f32` is a real type the annotation slot accepts*: rustc's diagnostic names `expected `f32`` in the same `expected due to this` slot it would name `f64`, and the `help: use a float literal` source-diff is bit-for-bit the lesson-033 shape with `f32` substituted, confirming the integer-in-float-slot rejection rule generalizes to the family. The auxiliary precision-witness probe (1.0 / 3.0 at both types) prints `0.3333333333333333` for `f64` and `0.33333334` for `f32` — 16 significant digits vs 8, the operational shape of the Book's "more precision" claim, witnessed by `println!`'s default float formatter; this probe uses one `_f32` literal suffix only, not centered as a concept today (suffix is a future move). Today is *shallow operational*: install only the second floating-point name, the rule that the annotation slot accepts it, the unchanged default, the IEEE-754 standard name, and the integer-literal-rejection rule generalized to `f32`. Deliberately not installed: float type-suffix forms `2.0_f32` / `3.0_f64` (parallel to lesson 081's integer suffix; future move); IEEE-754 mechanics (`NaN`, `infinity`, `0.0 / 0.0`, signed zero, denormals, why `NaN == NaN` is `false` — lesson 033 deferred all); float comparison gotchas (`0.1 + 0.2 != 0.3`); float methods (`.sqrt()`, `.floor()`, `.abs()`, etc.); float literals with exponents (`1.5e10`, `2.5E-3`); float `as` casts (float-to-integer, integer-to-`f32`, `f32`/`f64` cross-casts — lesson 034 installed only `i32 as f64`); format specifiers like `{:.2}`, `{:e}` (today uses default `{}`, which prints whole-valued floats without a trailing decimal); mixing `f32` and `f64` in arithmetic without an `as` cast; when to pick `f32` vs `f64` operationally.
depends_on: 001-rustc-compile-and-run, 002-fn-main-entry-point, 019-type-annotation-i32, 033-f64-floats, 003-read-rustc-diagnostic (cited — diagnostic map for the contrast probe), 005-let-binding (cited — `let name = value;` reused twice in the working probe), 011-println-positional-args (cited — `{}` positional printing reused twice), 080-integer-type-family (cited — pedagogical parallel: integer family is twelve names by sign-and-width; float family is two names by bit width, both signed), ordinary computer-use (terminal, plain-text editor, `rustc` on `PATH`, Linux/macOS shell)
unlocks: future "float type-suffix forms `2.0_f32`, `3.0_f64` — the float parallel to lesson 081's integer suffix `57u8`, with `f32` and `f64` as the suffix set" moves, future "IEEE-754 mechanics — `NaN`, `infinity`, `0.0 / 0.0`, signed zero `0.0` vs `-0.0`, denormals, why `NaN == NaN` is `false`" moves, future "float comparison gotchas — `0.1 + 0.2 != 0.3` and the general rule that float equality is fragile under arithmetic" moves, future "float methods on `f32` and `f64` — `.sqrt()`, `.floor()`, `.abs()`, `.powi(...)`, `.is_nan()`, `.is_infinite()`, etc." moves, future "float literals with exponents — `1.5e10`, `2.5E-3`, `1e-9` as alternate spellings for the same kind of value" moves, future "float-to-integer `as` casts — `3.14_f32 as i32`, `2.0_f64 as u8` with truncation/saturation rules" moves, future "integer-to-`f32` `as` casts — `5_i32 as f32`, extending lesson 034's `i32 as f64`" moves, future "`f32`/`f64` cross-casts — `1.0_f32 as f64`, `2.0_f64 as f32` (the latter lossy)" moves, future "format specifiers for floats — `{:.2}`, `{:.6}`, `{:e}`, `{:.*}`" moves, future "mixing `f32` and `f64` in arithmetic — the rule that they don't share an operator without an `as` cast" moves, future "when to pick `f32` vs `f64` operationally — memory budget, GPU/embedded constraints, numerical-stability tradeoffs"
```

```text
id: 089-ch3-3-function-conventions
move: install two coupled Book Ch3-3 conventions in one move — (a) `snake_case` as the conventional naming style for function and variable names ("all letters are lowercase and underscores separate words", Book Ch3-3 line 9-10), and (b) function definition *order* is free — a function defined later in the source file is callable from a function defined earlier ("Rust doesn't care where you define your functions, only that they're defined somewhere in a scope that can be seen by the caller", Book Ch3-3 lines 33-36); concretely the working probe is the Book's verbatim two-function example at Ch3-3 lines 15-25 — `fn main() { println!("Hello, world!"); another_function(); } fn another_function() { println!("Another function."); }` — compiled with `rustc demo.rs` (silent, exit 0) and run as `./demo` (prints `Hello, world!` then `Another function.`, exit 0). Probe 1 witnesses both conventions at once: `another_function` is snake_case AND is defined *after* `main`. Probe 2 is the contrast — function renamed to CamelCase `AnotherFunction` (definition + call site both updated): `rustc camel.rs` emits `warning: function \`AnotherFunction\` should have a snake case name` with `--> camel.rs:7:4`, caret underlining the name, inline `help: convert the identifier to snake case: \`another_function\``, `= note: \`#[warn(non_snake_case)]\` (part of \`#[warn(nonstandard_style)]\`) on by default`, trailer `warning: 1 warning emitted`, *exit 0*, executable produced; `./camel` runs and prints the same two lines. The snake_case convention is enforced as a *warning* (lesson 069's category), not an error — a centered "convention vs requirement" framing. Probe 3 (auxiliary) is a CamelCase variable name `let MyCount = 5;` firing the same `non_snake_case` lint with the noun `variable` in place of `function`, corroborating that one lint covers both kinds of names per the rustc lint listing ("variables, methods, functions, lifetime parameters and modules") and that the snake_case rule from Book line 9-10 covers `let` bindings (lesson 005) too.
main_concept: one centered claim with two coupled framings under Book Ch3-3 lines 9-36 (one continuous passage, both load-bearing). (1) *snake_case is Rust's conventional naming style for function and variable names*: Book line 9-10 verbatim, "Rust code uses *snake case* as the conventional style for function and variable names, in which all letters are lowercase and underscores separate words." The same convention covers `fn` names (lesson 008) and `let` names (lesson 005); the rustc `non_snake_case` lint enforces the convention as a *warning* (on by default, in the `nonstandard_style` group), not as a hard rule — code violating the convention compiles and runs, but rustc surfaces the conventional fix in the diagnostic's inline `help:`. (2) *Function definition order is free*: Book Ch3-3 lines 33-36 verbatim, "Note that we defined `another_function` *after* the `main` function in the source code; we could have defined it before as well. Rust doesn't care where you define your functions, only that they're defined somewhere in a scope that can be seen by the caller." This closes lesson 008's explicit deferral (008's *What To Ignore For Now* named "Where the definition can sit" with this same Book quote and reserved the general rule for a future move; today is that move). The two conventions co-install naturally because the Book itself teaches them in one passage with one example, and the verbatim working probe demonstrates both at once: `another_function` (snake_case) is defined AFTER `main` and successfully called from inside `main`. The bundling is principled, not opportunistic — it reflects the corpus's own unit of instruction. Today is *shallow operational*: install only the snake_case convention for `fn` and `let` names, the free-order rule for one-file packages, and the convention-vs-requirement framing (`non_snake_case` as a warning lint). Deliberately not installed: lint configuration (`#[allow(non_snake_case)]`, `#[deny(...)]`, `#[forbid(...)]` — the level taxonomy lesson 069 deferred); `pub` and module visibility (the "scope that can be seen by the caller" framing names the rule but does not unpack `pub`/`mod`); module-level scoping (free-order rule is *within the same module*; cross-module is more complex); other naming conventions (CamelCase for type names — structs, enums; `'lowercase` for lifetime names; `SCREAMING_SNAKE_CASE` already at lesson 075); recursion (a function calling itself is the same definition-order rule at a tighter loop, but recursion is its own move); the compiler's two-pass approach (the mechanism behind free definition order — collect items first, then resolve bodies — is real but mechanism-level, not learner-facing rule).
depends_on: 008-define-and-call-function (load-bearing — `fn name() { ... }` + `name();`; today extends 008's `name` slot with the snake_case rule and closes 008's explicit deferral on definition order, with the same Book quote 008's *What To Ignore* used), 002-fn-main-entry-point (cited — `fn main` runs when the executable launches), 011-println-positional-args (cited — `println!` reused twice in Probe 1, once in Probe 3), 005-let-binding (cited — `let name = value;`; today's snake_case rule covers `let` names too per Book line 9-10), 069-rustc-warnings (cited — Probe 2's `warning:` headline + `exit=0` + produced executable is read with 069's category map; the `non_snake_case` lint is one specific lint in 069's general category), 075-const-declaration (cited — `SCREAMING_SNAKE_CASE` is the *different* naming convention for `const`; today's *Check Yourself* answer (a) sorts `READ_LINE` out as belonging to 075's territory, not snake_case), ordinary computer-use (terminal, plain-text editor, `rustc` on `PATH`, Linux/macOS shell)
unlocks: future "lint configuration — `#[allow(non_snake_case)]`, `#[warn(...)]`, `#[deny(...)]`, `#[forbid(...)]` and their crate-level `#![...]` cousins" moves (deferred since lesson 069), future "`CamelCase` (UpperCamelCase) for type names — structs, enums, traits — and the sibling `non_camel_case_types` lint" moves, future "`'lowercase` for lifetime names" moves, future "`pub` and module visibility — the unpacking of 'scope that can be seen by the caller'" moves, future "`mod` declarations and multi-file crates — definition-order is free *within the same module*, cross-module is more complex" moves, future "recursion — a function calling itself is the same free-order rule at a tighter loop" moves, future "the rustc lint listing as a navigable resource — `non_snake_case`, `non_camel_case_types`, `non_upper_case_globals`, `unused_variables`, etc." moves, future "function parameters and return values within Ch3-3 — already at lessons 020 (single parameter), 036 (multiple parameters), 021 (return value); future Ch3-3 moves are statement-vs-expression nuances at lesson 024 onward (already installed)" moves, future "the rustfmt rule set — Block-level conventions for snake_case-styled identifiers, naming-related formatting hooks (deferred since lesson 087)" moves, future "the lint group `nonstandard_style` as a named concept — three sibling lints (non_snake_case, non_camel_case_types, non_upper_case_globals) bundled under one group" moves
evidence:
  - output/docs/rust/book/ch03-03-how-functions-work.md
  - output/docs/rust/rustc/lints/listing/warn-by-default.md
  - experimental/eduratchet2/runs/rust-moves/observations/089-ch3-3-function-conventions.rs
```

```text
id: 090-loop-labels
move: prefix any loop with `'name:` (single-quote, snake_case identifier, colon) before its loop keyword (`loop`, `while`, `for`), and inside any nested loop write `break 'name;` or `continue 'name;` to target the labeled loop instead of the innermost; concretely the working probe `fn main() { 'outer: for i in 0..3 { for j in 0..3 { if j == 1 { println!("i = {i}, j = {j}: break 'outer"); break 'outer; } println!("i = {i}, j = {j}"); } } println!("after the labeled loops"); }` compiled with `rustc demo.rs` and run as `./demo` exits 0 silent and prints exactly three lines `i = 0, j = 0`, `i = 0, j = 1: break 'outer`, `after the labeled loops` — the outer loop body never sees `i = 1` or `i = 2` because `break 'outer;` exits the labeled outer loop directly, even with the inner unlabeled loop in between; the contrast probe with the same source modulo `break 'outer;` -> `break;` (and a `warning: unused label` headline at exit 0) prints six inner-loop lines (the outer loop continues for all three values of `i`, restarting and breaking the inner loop each time), making the targeted-loop difference visible in output; the parallel `continue 'outer;` probe `'outer: for i in 0..3 { for j in 0..3 { if j == 1 { ...; continue 'outer; } println!(...); } println!("end of outer body i = {i}"); }` prints six lines but no `end of outer body i = ...` line — `continue 'outer;` returns control to the outer loop's head directly, skipping the rest of every nested body in between (lesson 035's "head" vocabulary applied to a named outer loop); the syntax-error contrast probe with `outer:` (no leading `'`) at the label site fires `error: malformed loop label` plus `help: use the correct loop label format` with `+ '` source-diff and a sibling `error[E0425]: cannot find value `outer`` at the use site naming `help: use the similarly named label: `'outer``, witnessing the Book's "Loop labels must begin with a single quote" rule
main_concept: one centered claim with three coupled framings under Book Ch3-5 *Disambiguating with Loop Labels* lines 359-409 (load-bearing) and Reference `loop-expr.md` *Loop labels* / `break` *with labels* / `continue` *with labels* lines 295-299, 354-355, 467-469 (load-bearing). (1) *Loop labels are an optional disambiguator on Rust's three loop forms*: a loop expression may optionally carry a label, written as a single-quote, snake_case identifier, colon, sitting before the loop keyword — Reference line 295 verbatim "A loop expression may optionally have a *label*. The label is written as a lifetime preceding the loop expression, as in `'foo: loop { break 'foo; }`, `'bar: while false {}`, `'humbug: for _ in 0..0 {}`." Three loop forms (lessons 017, 022, 027) all accept labels in the same position. The Book's lines 361-365 supply the audience-level introduction "If you have loops within loops, `break` and `continue` apply to the innermost loop at that point. You can optionally specify a *loop label* on a loop that you can then use with `break` or `continue` to specify that those keywords apply to the labeled loop instead of the innermost loop. Loop labels must begin with a single quote." (2) *Labeled `break 'name;` and labeled `continue 'name;` target the named loop, not the innermost*: Reference lines 354-355 verbatim for `break` "A `break` expression is normally associated with the innermost `loop`, `for` or `while` loop enclosing the `break` expression, but a [label] can be used to specify which enclosing loop is affected", and Reference lines 467-469 verbatim for `continue` "Like `break`, `continue` is normally associated with the innermost enclosing loop, but `continue 'label` may be used to specify the loop affected." Bare `break;` (lesson 027) and bare `continue;` (lesson 035) keep their original innermost-only meanings; today *adds* the label form without replacing the bare form. (3) *The leading single-quote is the syntactic discriminator*: Book line 365 verbatim "Loop labels must begin with a single quote." The syntax-error contrast probe witnesses rustc's diagnostic when the `'` is omitted: `outer:` parses as a malformed loop label at the definition site (`error: malformed loop label` plus `help: use the correct loop label format` with `+ '` source-diff) and as an unknown value name at the use site (`error[E0425]: cannot find value `outer` in this scope` plus `help: use the similarly named label: `'outer``). The label-vs-value distinction lives entirely in the `'` prefix. The Book's verbatim two-counter example at lines 367-409 (with `'counting_up: loop { ... loop { ... break 'counting_up; ... } ... }`) is corroborated by Probe 5 and reproduces bit-for-bit on rustc 1.95.0; the lesson centers a smaller `for`-based shape to reduce moving parts. The Reference's labels-follow-hygiene-and-shadowing rule (lines 301-318) is named in *What To Ignore For Now* and deferred. Today is *shallow operational*: install only the `'name:` syntax on the three loop forms, the `break 'name;` and `continue 'name;` targeting rule, and the single-quote-required rule, with corroborating empirical contrast. Deliberately not installed: lifetime parameters `'a`, `'static` (same single-quote prefix, different concept — annotations on references); `break 'name value;` (composition with lesson 028's `break value;`); labeled bare blocks (`'name: { ... break 'name; }` — Reference's *Labeled block expressions* section, edition-2018-and-later feature); three-or-more nested loops (composition); the `unused_labels` lint as a centered concept (named in *What To Ignore* and witnessed in Probe 2's transcript, where the bare-`break;` contrast leaves the `'outer:` label dead); label-shadowing rules; interaction with `match` and `if let`.
depends_on: 027-loop-and-break (load-bearing — bare `break;` exits the innermost loop; today adds `break 'name;` for a named loop), 035-continue (load-bearing — bare `continue;` returns control to the innermost loop's head; today adds `continue 'name;` for a named loop), 022-for-range (cited — `for var in 0..N { ... }` is the construct used by both nests in the working probe), 017-while-loop (cited — labels also accept `while`, named in *What Changed* not exercised in the probe), 014-if-else (cited — `if condition { ... }` gates the labeled `break`/`continue`), 013-comparison-operators (cited — `==` produces the boolean for the `if`), 011-println-positional-args (cited — `println!` for visible output), 089-ch3-3-function-conventions (cited — snake_case naming convention applies after the leading `'`), 003-read-rustc-diagnostic (cited — diagnostic map for the syntax-error contrast probe), 069-rustc-warnings (cited — `warning: unused label` in the bare-`break;` contrast probe is read with 069's category map), ordinary computer-use (terminal, plain-text editor, `rustc` on `PATH`, Linux/macOS shell)
unlocks: future "`break 'name value;` — composition of today's labeled `break` form with lesson 028's `break value;` to return a value from a labeled outer loop, the fourth surface of lesson 024's expression rule applied to a named outer loop" moves, future "labeled bare blocks `'name: { ... break 'name; }` — Rust edition-2018-and-later supports labels on bare blocks, distinct from labels on loops; Reference's *Labeled block expressions* section" moves, future "the `unused_labels` lint as a centered concept — fires when a labeled loop's body uses only bare `break;` / `continue;` (no label-targeting actually occurs); deferred from lesson 069's general warning category" moves, future "label-shadowing and label-hygiene — Reference's `'a: loop { 'a: loop { break 'a; } ... }` example showing inner labels can shadow outer ones following the same hygiene rules as `let` bindings" moves, future "lifetime parameters `'a`, `'static` as a centered concept — same single-quote syntactic prefix but completely different role (annotations on references rather than disambiguators on loops); deferred indefinitely until references and ownership are installed deep enough to justify it" moves, future "labels on loops that span more than two levels of nesting — composition of today's rule with itself; the inner-most-but-one form `break 'mid;` from a triple-nested context" moves, future "`'_` as a not-valid-loop-label edge case — Reference line 318 names this, parallels lifetime elision conventions" moves, future "interaction of labeled `break`/`continue` with `match` arms and `if let` chains — composition with later pattern-matching moves" moves
evidence:
  - output/docs/rust/book/ch03-05-control-flow.md
  - output/docs/rust/reference/expressions/loop-expr.md
  - experimental/eduratchet2/runs/rust-moves/observations/090-loop-labels.rs
  - experimental/eduratchet2/runs/rust-moves/evidence/090-loop-labels.md
status: accepted
```
